Approval
This thesis has been prepared over six months from February to July at the Section
of Solid Mechanics, Department of Civil and Mechanical Engineering at the Technical
University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulﬁllment for the degree Master of Science in
Engineering, MSc Eng counting 30 ECTS. The thesis has been supervised by Professor,
Dr.-Ing., Dr.techn. Ilmar Ferreira Santos.

ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
As society continues to undergo rapid digitization, online condition monitoring has be-
come an increasing interest within many ﬁelds to secure the reliability of operations. In
the ﬁeld of rotor dynamics, this is no diﬀerent since machinery such as compressors, tur-
bines, pumps, and rotary atomizers become increasingly complex with higher demands of
reliability.
The goal of this master’s thesis is to investigate the possibility of combining controllable
lubrication techniques, signal processing, and operational modal analysis methods (OMA)
to track changes in undamped natural frequencies, damping ratios, and modeshapes of
a rotor-bearing-foundation test rig. The study focuses on the utilization of non-invasive
ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbation and diﬀerent OMA methods to estimate modal parameters with
the goal of addressing the need for online monitoring of rotating machinery supported by
ﬂuid-ﬁlm bearings.
The thesis covers a theoretical background for performing experimental and operational
modal analysis (EMA and OMA), introduces the rotor-bearing test rig, and discusses
the possibility of creating non-invasive ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbations. EMA methods such as
the p-LSCF (PolyMAX) and SDOF methods have been employed together with OMA
methods: ITD, PR, ERA, SSI, and FDD to estimate modal parameters. In pursuit of
online machine monitoring, automated OMA (AOMA) methods, namely A-ERA, A-SSI,
and A-PR are proposed.
At 1000 rpm it is concluded that combining non-invasive testing and OMA methods is
feasible in terms of estimating undamped natural frequencies and modeshapes. Although,
damping ratios are slightly elevated compared to EMA estimates. Furthermore, a natural
passive excitation source is observed when increasing the angular velocity of the rotor.
This natural excitation has proven satisfactory for accurately estimating the dominating
modes of the system at 7000 rpm and hence a complete non-invasive testing is possible. At
7000 rpm the non-invasive ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbation testing again yields good estimates of
undamped natural frequencies and mode shapes. However, damping ratios of the dominat-
ing modes are signiﬁcantly increased compared to passive OMA estimates. The developed
automated methods, A-ERA and A-SSI, are found to yield improved estimates compared
to other OMA methods, especially when considering damped and coupled responses.
Overall, this thesis demonstrates the successful integration of controllable lubrication
techniques, OMA methods, and computational tools for signal processing to accurately
estimate modal parameters to be used for online monitoring of rotor-bearing systems.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Machines such as compressors, turbines, pumps, and rotary atomizers all utilize ﬂexible
rotors supported by radial bearings. The most common types of bearings are rolling el-
ements, electromagnetic, and ﬂuid ﬁlm bearings all of which come with advantages and
disadvantages in terms of cost and reliability. Fluid ﬁlm bearings are often preferred for
applications where high load capacity and rotational speeds are required. These types
of bearings utilize a pressurized thin ﬂuid ﬁlm separating the moving and stationary
parts. Unlike rolling element bearings ﬂuid ﬁlm bearings also possess damping proper-
ties eﬀectively reducing vibration levels. Mainly two types of ﬂuid ﬁlm bearings exist,
namely hydrodynamic and hydrostatic bearings. Hydrostatic bearings require external
pressurized ﬂuid to build up the pressurized ﬂuid ﬁlm, hence no motion is required, while
hydrodynamic bearings rely on the relative movement of the journal and bearing squeezing
the ﬂuid into a converging gap. The word journal refers to the part of the rotor supported
inside the bearing. The ﬂuid ﬁlm will provide signiﬁcant stiﬀness and damping, hence
changes to the ﬁlm will aﬀect the overall dynamics of the rotor system.
Over the last two decades common machine elements have seen tremendous digitalization
into smart mechatronic devices through increasingly advanced integrated sensing and ac-
tuation systems. From these technical advances more advanced ﬂuid ﬁlm bearings have
been designed combining hydrostatic and hydrodynamic lubrication with electronic servo
valves, such as the Active Tilting Pad Journal Bearing (ATPJB) ﬁrst described by San-
tos in [1]. These bearings are speciﬁcally designed to address the issue of destabilizing
cross-coupling ﬂuid forces by allowing the bearing pads to tilt and adjust their equilib-
rium positions. Additionally, oil can be actively injected through oriﬁces in the tilting
pads. However, a limitation of these bearings is that the damping of the oil ﬁlm decreases
as the rotational speed increases, thereby constraining their operational range and hence
requiring monitoring of damping ratios to prevent instabilities. Moreover, rotating ma-
chines of high caliber, such as power plant turbines, are required to be extremely reliable
to prevent costly power outages. Altogether, online monitoring of the operating condi-
tions and modal parameters, i.e. damping ratios, natural frequencies, and modeshapes, of
the rotating machinery supported by ﬂuid ﬁlm bearings, is therefore wished for by many
industrial monitoring systems.
This leads to the need of having modal parameter estimation methods able to estimate
the modal parameters of the machine without disturbing its operating condition. Tradi-
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
1
==PAGE:13==
1.1. PREVIOUS WORK
tional modal analysis (in the setting of mechanical engineering) is performed using Exper-
imental Modal Analysis (EMA), which relies on the estimation of input/output relations
more speciﬁcally Frequency Response Functions (FRF) of the system. Therefore, it often
requires taking the system out of its regular service environment to obtain controlled con-
ditions where excitation forces are measurable. This means that the system may behave
slightly diﬀerently during the modal tests, and EMA is therefore typically used during
machine commissioning. Modal estimation methods that do not require knowledge of the
disturbances are Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) methods. OMA is well-known in
civil construction engineering where it is advantageous when analyzing large structures
such as bridges and high-rise buildings where controlled excitation becomes infeasible and
external ambient vibrations such as driving cars or wind are present. OMA methods are
rarely utilized for rotating machinery due to the invasion of harmonic vibrations violating
basic OMA assumptions of random excitation.
This leads to the need of developing a signal processing routine and perturbation tech-
nique that is able to provide well-conditioned signals during operation to be used for OMA
analysis of rotating machines. For this purpose, an ATPJB can be employed by designing
a non-invasive perturbation force via controllable lubrication techniques. Non-invasive
perturbation forces are forces that are capable of perturbing the rotor-bearing system
via the ﬂuid ﬁlm without signiﬁcantly changing the modal parameters of the system and
the original static equilibrium position of the journal. The perturbation should be able
to excite the rotor-bearing system including its foundation to allow for the identiﬁcation
of natural frequencies, damping ratios, and modeshapes of the rotor and foundation si-
multaneously. To remove invasive harmonics, a so-called run-out of compensation can
be utilized. This compensation is paramount in Active Magnetic Bearings (AMB) that
rely heavily upon good-quality feedback displacements and is mainly used to remove me-
chanical and electrical run-out noise. However, performing run-out compensation at the
operating speed also has the property of removing harmonic synchronous vibrations of
the displacement signals, which is of great advantage considering the application of OMA.
Moreover, in the ﬁelds of Structural Health Monitoring (SHM) for civil structures and
Condition Monitoring (CM) for rotating machinery, modal parameters are utilized among
other indicators to monitor structural changes over time and predict potential failures or
maintenance requirements. Automation is a key aspect of both SHM and CM, as it en-
ables continuous and eﬃcient tracking of structural changes. To support these monitoring
methods with the prediction of modal parameters, there is a strong need for automated
Operational Modal Analysis (AOMA) algorithms. And by automating OMA, the eﬀec-
tiveness of SHM and CM can be enhanced, leading to improved maintenance practices
and safer operation of structures and rotating machinery.
1.1
Previous Work
In order to establish a comprehensive understanding of the relevant ﬁelds for this study,
an extensive literature review is conducted. The objective is to acquire knowledge in
areas such as; the history of the rotor-bearing-foundation test-rig, EMA and OMA ap-
plications for rotating structures, ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation, and the automation of modal
estimation methods. Several key sources are examined and their insights are discussed
below, highlighting their signiﬁcance.
2
==PAGE:14==
1.1. PREVIOUS WORK
Rotor-bearing-foundation test rig
The rotor-bearing-foundation test rig utilized during this project has undergone several
advancements over the years. In 2001 Østergaard [2] originally designed and constructed
the test rig. Notably, Sørensen [3] later relocated the test rig from an elevated test bench
to a cast iron foundation. Christensen [4] conducted ﬂow experiments to experimentally
characterize the input/output relationship of the servo valves.
Subsequent studies focused on the mathematical modeling and validation of the test rig.
In recent years Salazer [5] extensively studied the applicability of model-based control
of ﬂexible-rotor systems using ATPJBs. Gani [6] developed a comprehensive 3D ﬁnite
element model of the foundation, signiﬁcantly enhancing the agreement with experimen-
tal data. Jensen [7] incorporated servo valve dynamics into the mathematical model and
implemented a Linear Quadratic Gaussian (LQG) controller. A notable overhaul of the
test rig was carried out by Bull [8], aiming to enable high-speed testing up to an im-
pressive 10,000 rpm. More recently, Wagner [9] further reﬁned the mathematical model
by incorporating journal rotations. Wagner also conducted various experimental tests,
which laid the foundation for several aspects addressed in this thesis, such as tilting pad
replacement, oil starvation, and improved modal analysis of the foundation.
EMA and OMA for rotating structures
As EMA often requires the commissioning of machinery and is not much diﬀerent when
performed on rotating machinery, original EMA methodologies can often be followed as
described by Ewins [10] or Heylen et al. [11]. However, in-situ EMA testing upon running
machinery can lead to the reduction of signal quality due to unknown disturbances which
have to be considered. OMA has demonstrated promising results in the analysis of static
structures. However, when it comes to rotating machinery, its full potential has yet to be
fully realized due to the presence of harmonic disturbances. Peeters et al. [12] discusses
this issue, and shows how a harmonic ﬁlter can be used to remove harmonics to obtain
trustworthy OMA estimates, considering operational data from an in-ﬂight helicopter and
running diesel engine. Svendsen [13] improved OMA estimation for a rotordynamic test
rig using a notch ﬁlter at synchronous harmonics. Further, Novak [14] used a run-out
compensation performed at the operating speed to successfully eliminate synchronous
harmonics and enhance OMA estimation in an active gas journal-bearing test rig.
Other authors have also successfully utilized OMA for rotating machinery such as in
[15], where Saint Martin et al. use ramp-up unbalance disturbances to identify modal
parameters of the supporting structure using an OMA method. While Carden in [16]
uses radial proximity sensors to estimate lateral rotordynamic modes from measurements
made during normal operation.
Fluid ﬁlm perturbation
First, it is noted how for AMBs it is common to use perturbations of the control current
to perform rotordynamic tests and estimate FRFs taking advantage of their sensing and
actuating systems. One of the pioneering contributions to this ﬁeld was made by Ulbrich
[17], over three decades ago. Today, the use of AMBs for rotordynamic testing has become
a reality in the commissioning of numerous large industrial re-injection compressors [18].
While performing rotor analysis via ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation is a rather new concept. The
ﬁrst contribution to the topic was given by Santos and Cerda in [19] [20] about one decade
ago. Svendsen [21] took some of the ﬁrst steps in characterizing ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation
using an ATPJB with OMA methods. He analyzed the eﬀects of varying AC and DC
3
==PAGE:15==
1.2. PROJECT SCOPE
perturbation parameters upon a rigid rotor system and showed the possibility of using
similar methods for larger ﬂexible rotor systems. In [22] Simonsen further analyzed the
possibilities of using ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbations of the full ﬂexible rotor-bearing-foundation
test rig also used during this project. However, challenges were encountered due to the
high damping characteristics of the ATPJB. This leads to further analysis of gas bearings
where the damping levels are generally lower. Frigaard considered ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation
in a gas bearing and shows in [23] that by perturbation via a piezo-actuated injection
system, it is possible to track natural frequencies and damping ratios using OMA methods.
OMA automation
The automation of OMA (AOMA) is highly desirable for SHM and CM monitoring sys-
tems.
Several researchers have made contributions to the development of automated
OMA routines. One common approach involves utilizing stabilization diagrams provided
by various OMA methods. These diagrams are then processed using unsupervised ma-
chine learning such as K-means clustering, hierarchical clustering, and Gaussian mixture
models to remove spurious poles and automate the choosing of physical ones. Notable
studies in this area, include the works of Paulsen [24] and Dreher [25] who both de-
velop AOMA routines to be used for rotor-dynamic testing and also provide additional
references on the subject.
1.2
Project Scope
The goal of this master’s thesis is to investigate the possibility of combining controllable
lubrication techniques with operational modal analysis (OMA) and computational signal
processing tools with the objective to track changes in damping ratios, natural frequen-
cies, and mode shapes. A rotor-bearing-foundation test rig is employed for this purpose,
using random ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation through a servo valve active control system. 10
radial displacement sensors of the rotor are utilized to obtain vibration measurements
for OMA and EMA analysis. Multiple extensive experimental campaigns are conducted,
accumulating to a test time of 15 hours including only data presented in this thesis.
Many secondary experimental tests are performed aiming to describe the overall dynamics
of the test rig including, unbalance vibrations, the eﬀect of hydrostatic lubrication, the
eﬀect of servo-valve input parameters, and the passive equilibrium position. By discussion
and comparison of EMA and OMA results it is analyzed how and when non-invasive ﬂuid
ﬁlm perturbation is obtained, with the overall goal of answering the two research questions:
RQ1 Is it possible to produce non-invasive perturbations via an Active Tilting Pad Jour-
nal Bearing (ATPJB) in a rotordynamic test rig?
RQ2 Can the non-invasive perturbations be used for output-only modal analysis (OMA)
to obtain consistent estimates of natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode-
shapes?
The novelty of this thesis lies in the utilization of non-invasive ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbation
and Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) methods to estimate modal parameters, includ-
ing natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode shapes, of a real-size rotor-foundation
test rig during operation. Of particular signiﬁcance is the consistent estimation of mode-
shapes, which has not been extensively explored in previous research. Additionally, the
incorporation of an at-speed run-out compensation represents a novel aspect that has
received limited attention in the existing literature.
4
==PAGE:16==
1.2. PROJECT SCOPE
This thesis utilizes various OMA methods, including the ITD, PR, ERA, SSI, and FDD.
Additionally, advanced global EMA methods, such as the p-LSCF (PolyMAX) are studied
and implemented including SDOF EMA methods for comparative analysis. Furthermore,
simple automated OMA methods denoted by the A-ERA, A-SSI, and A-PR are developed
as part of this project.
Throughout this thesis, signiﬁcant time gaps arose due to delays in manufacturing certain
machine elements, which hindered the direct achievement of the thesis goals. However,
these time gaps presented valuable opportunities to conduct a thorough experimental
modal analysis (EMA) of the test-rig foundation and rotor-shaft to be used for future
FEM model ﬁtting, as well as a comparative study of modal analysis methods. All of
these are thoroughly documented in Appendix C-E. Additionally, the extended timeframe
allowed for an in-depth exploration of modal analysis theory, resulting in a comprehensive
theoretical chapter dedicated to this subject, with the hope of providing a modal analysis
overview useful for future students within this ﬁeld.
The work of this thesis culminated in the creation of a paper draft aimed for publication in
the journal of Mechanical Systems and Signal Processing (MSSP). The draft is included in
Appendix A and will be further improved in continuation of this thesis before submission.
A short summary of the major thesis chapters is:
Chapter 2: Presents the theoretical background for performing general modal analysis
of real structures. The description of the utilized EMA and OMA methods
is given together with an introduction to the required statistical tools and
signal processing methods.
Chapter 3: Presents the rotor-bearing-foundation test rig and the data acquisition sys-
tem.
Chapter 4: Presents the main analysis of performing non-invasive rotordynamic testing.
Chapter 5: Final conclusions and future aspects are given.

Chapter 2
Theory
2.1
Theoretical foundation of modal analysis
This section will cover the basic theoretical subjects used to perform general modal anal-
ysis. The main topics covered in this section are Classical dynamics, Random vibrations,
and signal processing.
2.1.1
Models for modal parameter estimation
This section will brieﬂy introduce the main mathematical models used for modal param-
eter estimation including, modal models, state space models, and ARMA models. The
description of the models will be given using a structural dynamic standpoint. In struc-
tural modal analysis, it is generally assumed that the underlying model is a Nd-degree-
of-freedom (Nd-DOF) linear-time-invariant (LTI), viscously damped system. Which can
be described by the second-order diﬀerential equation.
M¨x(t) + C ˙x + Kx(t) = f(t)
(2.1)
˙[ ] denotes the temporal derivative. Where M ∈RNd×Nd,C ∈RNd×Nd, and K ∈RNd×Nd
are the mass, damping, and stiﬀness matrices respectively. x(t) ∈RNd and f(t) ∈RNd
are the response and input vector respectively.
The modal model
Since the aim of modal analysis is to obtain the modal parameters i.e. the undamped
natural frequencies, damping ratios, mode shapes, and participation vectors the model
in which these are deﬁned will be recapitulated. Heylen et al. [11] derives the modal
model from (2.1) as the frequency response function H(jω) of the input-output relation
X(jω) = H(jω)F(jω), in terms of the modal parameters as.
H(jω) =
Nd
X
n=1
 ψnγT

+ ψ∗

(2.2)
Where [ ]H denotes the complex conjugate transpose, [ ]∗is the complex conjugate, and
[ ]T is the transpose of a vector. ψn and γn are the mode shape and modal participation
vector of the nth mode respectively. Mode shapes relate to the response or displacements
of every DOF, while participation vectors relate to the input forces and describe the
eﬃciency of excitation at each DOF. λn is the system pole yielding the undamped natural

Techniques - Theory and Experiments

frequency variable. The modal model can also be written with matrix products as.
H(jω) = Ψ [jωI −Λ]−1 L
(2.3)
Where Ψ and L are the modal and modal participation matrix stacking the mode shapes
and participation vectors in the columns respectively. I is the identity matrix while Λ is
a diagonal matrix holding the system poles λn in the diagonal. Considering discretized
Nd-DOF structures using (2.1) the modal analysis will never estimate the full frequency
response matrix. Since in practice measurements are only performed at No points using
Ni inputs. Therefore the size of the estimated transfer function matrix is always limited
following the expression.

(2.4)

solution f(t) = 0 is given by a linear combination of modal contributions.
x(t) =

(2.5)
Where cn are constants determined from an initial condition. Equation (2.5) shows that
a general free decay (homogeneous solution) is given by the product of mode shapes
ψn and a complex exponential. This fact is especially important when considering the
interpretation of correlation functions as free decays, as will later be discussed.
State space models
A continuous LTI state space model is generally deﬁned as. [26]
z(t) = Az(t) + Bu(t) + w(t)
(2.6a)
y(t) = Cz(t) + Du(t) + v(t)
(2.6b)
Where z(t) ∈Rn is the state vector containing the values of the n states. u(t) ∈RNi
and y(t) ∈RNo are the vectors of Ni inputs and No outputs. w(t) ∈Rn and v(t) ∈RNo
are the process and measurement noise modeled as stochastic processes. A ∈Rn×n is the
continuous time system matrix, describing the dynamics of the n states, while B ∈Rn×Ni,
C ∈RNo×n and D ∈RNo×Ni are the input, output, and feedthrough matrices respectively.
In this thesis, it is generally assumed that the state space equation (2.6) is completely
controllable and observable such that all modes of the system can be excited either by
the deterministic u(t) or stochastic w(t) inputs and that all modes can be observed at
the output y(t).
The general equations of motion (2.1) can be rewritten as a system of ﬁrst-order diﬀerential
equations using the state space format by deﬁning the state vector z(t) = {x(t), ˙x(t)}T
and the matrices.

ARMA and Matrix fraction polynomial models
An auto regressive (AR) moving average (MA) or ARMA modal in short, is a tool orig-
inally developed to describe and predict time varying signals and is widely used within
statistics, electrical engineering, and economics. The description will be given from an
OMA/dynamical-systems standpoint. The ARMA model of AR order NA and MA order
NM predicts a multi-dimensional discrete time signal y(n) at sample n as a diﬀerence
equation.
y(n) =
NA
X
k=1
Aky(n −k) +
NM
X
k=0
Bkf(n −k)
(2.14)
Where f(n) is the vector of inputs, Ak ∈CNo×No, k = 1, 2, . . . , NA are the AR matrices,
Bk ∈CNo×Ni, k = 0, 1, . . . , NM are the MA matrices. The AR matrices multiply the
original signal of previous time steps y(n −k) and hence the AR term can be interpreted
as the approximation of the homogeneous solution to the general state space model. Hence
the AR matrices will describe the physical properties of the system. Whereas the MA
matrices multiply the input f and can be interpreted as the particular solution representing
the external force and noise acting on the system. By Z-transform of (2.14) using the time
shift property y(n −m) ↔z−mY (z) and rearranging one obtains an expression for the
transfer function.
H(z) = Y(z)

function. This model is called a Left-Matrix Fraction Description (LMFD), transpose of
the Right-Matrix Fraction Description (RMFD) H′(z) = B′(z) ¯A′−1(z). Equation (2.15) is
a matrix fraction polynomial with the AR and MA coeﬃcients describing the denominator
and numerator matrix polynomials respectively yielding NA poles (damping and natural
frequency) and NM zeros of the system.

All identiﬁcation techniques return a set of complex conjugate pairs of eigenvalues and
eigenvectors from which the modal parameters of the system can be calculated. When
discrete time poles are obtained they can be transformed into continuous times poles
according to Frobenius’ theorem [26].
λn = ln(µn)

2.1.3
Modal analysis of rotating machinery
Performing modal analysis of rotating machinery comes with several challenges. Unlike
stationary structures phenomena such as:
• Gyroscopic forces
• Non-symmetric stators (bearings). (General property of ﬂuid-ﬁlm bearings)
• Time-varying ﬂuid forces
are potentially present.
Any or all of these will ruin the symmetry of the otherwise
symmetric systems matrices of general non-rotating LTI systems. Internal damping will
also create skew-symmetry of the stiﬀness matrix not normally encountered for non-
rotating structures. A consequence of these features is that the general solution to such
problems does not possess simplicities and symmetries such as reciprocity (switching input
and output yield the same response - symmetric FRF), and are generally referred to as
Non-Self-Adjoint (NSA) systems [10].
Speciﬁcally, the damping (velocity-dependent terms) and stiﬀness matrices will often be
non-symmetric which leads to the fact that the system matrix will have two sets of eigen-
vectors namely the ’right’ and ’left’ eigenvectors, where the right eigenvectors are generally
associated with the mode shapes, while the left eigenvectors are associated with patterns
of forces (also called normal excitation shapes). Moreover, rotation combined with non-
symmetric (often still skew-symmetric) matrices will lead to complex eigenvalues and
mode shapes. In opposite to non-rotating structures high complexity of the mode shapes
is hence expected. The need for complex modes arises from the orbiting nature of the
rotating machinery with gyroscopic forces which introduces the phenomena of forward
and backward whirl modes. They should be understood as modes where the direction of
the modal orbit is in the same (forward) and opposite (backward) direction of the shaft
spin. The interpretation of complex mode shapes of rotating structures is described in
Appendix B.1.
2.1.4
The central limit theorem
One of the most important assumptions within OMA is that the response is a zero-mean
Gaussian signal. Why this assumption is often automatically satisﬁed in random vibration
will here be shortly argued by the central limit theorem. The central limit theorem states
that the linear combination of m independent and identically distributed (IID) random
variables will converge towards a Gaussian distribution as m →∞independent of the
original distribution of the random variables. In structural dynamics, the forced response
y(t) of an LTI system is given by the convolution between the input f(t) and the impulse
response function h(t).
For a sampled signal y(n) at time step n∆t the convolution
becomes a summation of the form, [27].
y(n) =
n
X
k=n−N
h(n −k)f(k)∆t
(2.19)
Where N is the number of sample points that are required in order to reasonably de-
scribe the memory of the system and ∆t is the sampling time. Hence if the input f(k)
is assumed white noise (which is by deﬁnition IID) the response y(n) will for increasing
N approach a normal distribution independent of the distribution of the forcing f(k).
10
==PAGE:22==
2.1. THEORETICAL FOUNDATION OF MODAL ANALYSIS
This also illustrates that lightly damped structures which require a large N in order to
describe the full dynamics will tend to have a more Gaussian response compared to more
damped structures with small N. This is a very important realization when considering
the eﬀectiveness of OMA, hence it is expected that OMA will perform better on lightly
damped structures.
Moreover the necessity of assuming Gaussian response signals comes from the normal dis-
tribution itself. Considering the probability density function of a zero mean multivariate
signal x(t) ∈RNo, it only depends on its correlation matrix R, as given in [27].
p(x) =
1
(2π)No/2|R|e−xT R−1x/2
(2.20)
Hence also the correlation function matrix R will hold all valuable information of the
signal, which is exploited by almost every OMA identiﬁcation method.
2.1.5
Correlation functions (CF)
Correlation functions (CF) are a key component in OMA. They are assumed to extract all
valuable information in a random signal which can be used to estimate modal parameters.
Diﬀerent methods exist to estimate correlation functions such as the unbiased direct
method, the biased and unbiased Welch estimate, and by random decrement. This study
will use the unbiased direct method which is presented below.
The cross-correlation functions of two random signals are deﬁned in [27] as.
Rxy(τ) = E[x(t)y(t + τ)] = E[x(t −τ)y(t)]
Ryx(τ) = E[y(t)x(t + τ)] = E[y(t −τ)x(t)]
(2.21)
Where Rxy(τ) is the cross-correlation function of signals x(t) and y(t), τ is the time lag
and E[ ] is the expected value operator. Also, the correlation of a signal x(t) with itself
Rxx is the autocorrelation of x(t).
When dealing with No measurement channels the measurements can be ordered in the
response vector.
x(t) = {x1(t), x2(t), . . . , xNo(t)}T
(2.22)
Where x(t) ∈RNo is the signal vector. And the correlation functions (CF) can be conve-
niently arranged in a square matrix called the CF matrix.
R(τ)No×No = E

x(t)xT(t + τ)


ergodic the expectation can be replaced by a time average, and the unbiased estimated
CF matrix becomes.
bR(τ) = 1

2.1.6
Spectral Densities (SD)
Frequency domain techniques base their estimates on the spectral density (SD) function.
The SD function is deﬁned as the Fourier transform of the CF function. As explained
by Newland in [28] The CF function indirectly gives information about the frequencies
present in a random process. The idea behind the spectral density is therefore to extract
this frequency information by the Fourier transform as.
Gxx(ω) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
Rxx(τ)e−iωτdτ
(2.29)
Gxy(ω) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
Rxy(τ)e−iωτdτ
(2.30)
Where Gxx(ω) and Gxy(ω) are respectively the power spectral density (PSD) and cross
spectral density (CSD) as a function of frequency ω. Since the calculation of correlation
functions involves convolution integrals in the time domain the spectral densities can be
simply deﬁned as multiplications in the frequency domain. Hence an alternative deﬁnition
of the SD’s is [11].
Gxx(ω) = X(ω)XH(ω)
(2.31)
Gxy(ω) = X(ω)Y H(ω)
(2.32)

==PAGE:24==

Where X(ω) and Y (ω) are the frequency spectra (Fourier transforms) of the signals x(t)
and y(t) respectively. Where [ ]H denotes the complex conjugate transpose (hermitian
transpose), which, in this univariate case, is simply the complex conjugate. When dealing
with multivariate signals the SD matrix is deﬁned as the Fourier transform of the CF
matrix.
G(ω) = 1

(2.33)
Where diagonal and oﬀ-diagonal terms are power and cross-spectral densities respectively.

N n,
k = 0, 1, 2, . . . , (N −1)
(2.34)
Estimating the SD functions as described above works well under one important assump-
tion: That the correlation function has completely decayed to zero inside the considered
time window. If the damping in the system is not suﬃcient or the CF has a signiﬁcant
noise-tail the SD estimate will exhibit what is called side lobe noise. To avoid this prob-
lem the CF is forced to zero by a tapering window. Both exponential and Flat-triangular
windows can be applied, whereas in OMA the ﬂat-triangular window is usually preferred
since it reduces the side-lobe noise without introducing a large bias at the spectral peaks.
However, in this project, the SD functions are mainly estimated using Welch’s method
which avoids this problem. This method segments the signal into blocks and applies aver-
aging with a Hanning window and overlap (normally 50%) to reduce leakage. Although,
leakage cannot be avoided which will lead to blunting of the spectral peaks and hence an
overestimation bias of the modal damping. Averaging is used to remove undesired random
noise components in the signals. If noise is uncorrelated averaging will in the limit lead to
perfect estimations of SD functions however large data sets will be needed and normally
a compromise is taken.
The spectral densities are especially important in random vibration theory since they
describe the input-output relation in a very simple way via the fundamental theorem. For
the MIMO case the fundamental theorem is given by Brincker et al. in [27] as.
Gx(ω) = H∗(iω)Gf(ω)HT(iω)
(2.35)
Where Gf(ω) and Gy(ω) are the input and output spectral densities and H is the system
transfer function matrix (FRF matrix).

An important property of correlation functions is that they can be interpreted as free
decays of a system excited by white noise. This can be realized by interpretation of the
modal decomposition of the correlation functions assuming white noise input, as given by
Brincker et al. in [27].

a square matrix of size No × No, the response matrix Y(k) will also hold No free decays
at No points.

tions is simply R′(τ) = RT(τ). If one accidentally uses the wrong representation of the
correlation functions as free decays a bias will exist on the estimated mode shapes since
the exponential term is only proportional to the modal participation vector γn.

2.1. THEORETICAL FOUNDATION OF MODAL ANALYSIS
2.1.8
Specifying dynamic measurements
The sampling frequency fs deﬁnes the number of samples made per second. The upper
limit of the resulting frequency band is deﬁned by the Nyquist frequency fNyq which is a
consequence of the discrete Fourier transform.
fNyq = fs/2
(2.40)
Hence it is important to specify the sampling rate high enough such that all modes of
interest are within the Nyquist band. However, due to the use of antialiasing ﬁlters that
are often placed with ﬁlter cutoﬀat the Nyquist frequency they attenuate the signal
in a region below fNyq hence it is recommended to use fs > 2.4fmax [27].
Although
this requirement is enough considering the available frequency band, oversampling the
signal further can increase the signal-to-noise ratio of the signal. This can be realized
by considering the measurement noise as a white noise process, i.e. its spectral density
can be assumed ﬂat within the Nyquist band Gn(ω) = Gn0. And by utilizing Parseval
theorem which states that the noise variance σ2
n must equal the area under the spectral
density one simply obtains.
σ2
n = 2Gn0fNyq
⇒
Gno =
σ2
n
2fNyq
(2.41)
Where Gn0 deﬁnes the noise ﬂoor. Hence assuming that the noise variance σ2
n does not
depend on the sampling rate the noise ﬂoor can be decreased by increasing the Nyquist
frequency i.e. increasing the sampling rate. The noise variance σ2
n is in fact independent
from the sampling rate fs for simple analog-to-digital converters used within this thesis.
2.1.9
General signal processing of sampled data and correlation
functions
This thesis employs multiple signal processing techniques on the sampled data to enhance
the quality and eﬃciency of modal estimation methods. The main processing techniques
often utilized for OMA testing are explained by Brincker et al. in [27] while the most
important are presented below. Note that when obtaining data from the APTJB test rig,
the data have always been run-out compensated to remove run-out noise and possibly
synchronous harmonics. The run-out compensation is described in section 3.4.
Filtering
Mainly, two types of ﬁltering exist: analog and digital ﬁlters. Analog ﬁlters are primarily
used as anti-aliasing ﬁlters, removing frequency components above the Nyquist frequency.
On the other hand, digital ﬁlters are commonly employed for post-processing to isolate
or exclude certain frequencies. In this thesis, three main types of digital ﬁlters have been
utilized: ﬁnite impulse response (FIR), inﬁnite impulse response (IIR), and FFT ﬁlters.
Classical FIR and IIR ﬁlters oﬀer fast processing but may introduce phase distortion,
rippling, and mellow ﬁlter cut-oﬀ. These ﬁlters are typically designed for real-time pro-
cessing, which is not necessary for OMA. Therefore, FFT ﬁltering is often preferred as it
has no phase distortion and allows for high customizability. The steps involved in FFT
ﬁltering are as follows: 1) Convert the signal to the frequency domain using FFT, 2) De-
ﬁne a window function, such as a Hanning window, in the frequency domain to shape the
desired ﬁlter response, 3) Multiply the FFT by the window function, and 4) Transform
the resulting signal back to the time domain using the IFFT.
15
==PAGE:27==
2.2. MODAL ASSURANCE CRITERIA (MAC)
Implementation of the FFT ﬁlter in this thesis involves time domain segmentation, where
the data is segmented using a Hanning window with 50% overlap. However, it is worth
noting that performing the ﬁltering in a single-shot FFT (without segmentation) is pre-
ferred to reduce leakage.
The ﬁlters used in this thesis mainly consist of low-pass ﬁlters, which remove energy above
the Nyquist frequency to avoid aliasing during downsampling. Additionally, band-pass
ﬁlters are employed to isolate speciﬁc modes of interest.
Decimation and downsampling
As explained above (Section 2.1.8), the sampling frequency is often oversampled to im-
prove the signal-to-noise ratio. Hence, it is advantageous to re-sample the signal to a
smaller, more ﬁtting sampling frequency, which will improve the speed and eﬃciency of
modal analysis methods. This is denoted as downsampling or decimation. When perform-
ing decimation, it is important to ﬁrst remove the energy above the new desired sampling
frequency since, according to Parseval’s theorem, energy will remain in the signal and
leak into the lower frequency range (aliasing). Hence, a low-pass ﬁlter is always employed
before decimation. Decimation by a factor of n is then simply performed by keeping every
n’th data point and omitting the rest without any errors.
Processing of correlation functions
Correlation-driven OMA methods such as the ITD, ERA, and PR rely directly upon the
estimation of the CF matrix, hence the processing of the CF has a high inﬂuence on
the resulting modal parameters of these methods. As previously mentioned correlation
functions are always calculated using the direct approach (2.27). However one still needs
to decide the number of time lags used.
Correlation functions for damped structures
will tend to have a steep tapering ending in a noise tail of constant amplitude, hence
the number of time lags is often cut such that the correlation function mainly represents
physical dynamics. For very damped structures a compromise must be taken with respect
to the number of time lags. To obtain well-overdetermined problems many time lags are
desired, although this increases the ratio of physical dynamics to noise in the CF. A
tapering triangular window can be used to alleviate this problem, although cutting the
correlation function appropriately is usually enough.
High measurement noise levels are represented in the ﬁrst couple of time lags in the CF,
hence discarding the ﬁrst time lag can be advantageous as discussed by Svendsen in [21].
2.2
Modal Assurance Criteria (MAC)
Modal Assurance Criteria (MAC) are widely used within modal analysis to compare two
sets of modal vectors (and poles), such as mode shapes or participation vectors.
In
general any MAC(v1, v2) ∈[0, 1] results in a real scalar where 1 implies that the vectors
are parallel/equivalent, and 0 means that they are orthogonal.
Vacher et al. [30] describe diﬀerent extensions of the original MAC criterion. The original
and simplest MAC is able to measure the degree of linearity between two monophase
vectors v1, v2.
MAC(v1, v2) =

for example modeshapes of a proportionally damped system also called normal modes.
A complex eigenvector v is only computed up to a multiplicative complex factor. So that
the phases of an eigenvector are given up to an angle β i.e. the eigenvector is determined
up to a rotation of an arbitrary angle as v(β) = ve−iβ. A phase normalization of a vector
is deﬁned by searching the optimal angle ˜β of β that renders a complex vector "as real as
possible". Vacher et al. [30] argue that by maximizing the norm of the real part of the
vector v(β) the optimal angle ˜β is.
˜β = ∠(vTv)

General use of the MAC
MAC- comparisons are extensively used throughout this thesis. The main applications
are listed below.
• Matching of modes shapes or participation vectors in stabilization diagrams.
• As a distance measure dMAC(XP) used for clustering of similar modal parameter
sets. dMAC = 1 −MAC.
• To generate a weighting vector w when calculating mean modal estimates.
• Comparing experimental modeshapes to their self, also called AutoMAC. Serves
as a good measure of spatial aliasing. Meaning that modes appear similar due to
insuﬃcient response positions.
• Comparing diﬀerent sets of modal parameters with each other.
Such as model
parameters from diﬀerent experimental setups or modal identiﬁcation techniques.
17
==PAGE:29==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
2.3
Operational Modal Analysis (OMA)
Having established the theoretical foundation for performing modal analysis, OMA meth-
ods and assumptions will now be presented. Four time domain methods are chosen namely
the Ibrahim time domain (ITD), data-drive stochastic subspace identiﬁcation (SSI), eigen-
system realization algorithm (ERA), and poly reference (PR), including one frequency
domain method namely the enhanced frequency domain decomposition generally denoted
the FDD. Several OMA methods are utilized for two main reasons: To analyze their dif-
ferences in applicability and to compare modal estimates across methods. It is generally
assumed that when a mode compares well across diﬀerent estimation methods that mode
is estimated with high accuracy, as discussed by Paulsen in [31].
The CF is calculated from the entire signal and bias-free estimates are therefore easier
to obtain using time domain methods. However, additional spurious modal parameters
are often estimated, necessitating a subsequent sorting of physical modes.
The time
domain methods ITD, ERA, and PR are correlation driven, meaning that they rely on
pre-calculated correlation functions. This has the eﬀect of being able to reduce the signal
noise before estimation, whereas the data-driven SSI must handle the noise internally by
estimating additional noise modes. Interesting is also that the model order of the SSI,
ERA, and PR techniques can be varied making them advantageous for automation.
The frequency domain techniques rely on the estimation of the spectral density matrix,
where physical modes are easily separated from noise by only ﬁtting the dominant fre-
quency bands of the dominating modes.
As a consequence, this requires the user to
actively pick the desired frequencies. And because only a small band is used for identiﬁ-
cation the modal estimates are often biased, especially considering damped and coupled
systems.
2.3.1
OMA Assumptions
Obtaining reliable modal parameter estimates through OMA relies on the following main
assumptions.
• The excitation of the structure is multiple inputs, that is, many independent exci-
tation forces are present.
• Excitation sources have white noise properties, exciting a broad frequency band.
I.e. their spectral densities should be ﬂat.
• The analyzed system is linear and time-invariant (LTI), and viscously damped.
• The response of the system is stationary and ergodic. As a consequence time aver-
ages can replace ensemble averages.
• Response signals are zero-mean Gaussian distributed or nearly Gaussian such that
they can be totally described by their second-order properties - Correlation functions
and Spectral densities.
The dynamics of LTI structures can be described by a linear combination of the response to
many independent forces f(t), hence according to the central limit theorem, the resulting
response will be approximately Gaussian independent of the input distributions. And the
last OMA assumption is therefore often easily satisﬁed. And since Gaussian distributions
18
==PAGE:30==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
are totally described by their second-order properties such as correlation functions it is
customary to remove the mean or trends from all signals [29].
The second assumption about white noise excitation is normally accepted within OMA
theory. However, it is not necessary for the actual physical forces acting on the structure.
This is explained by Ibrahim et al.
in [32] where it is shown by including a pseudo
linear ﬁlter which is assumed to transform white noise input into the actual force to the
structure (colored noise). The ﬁlter does not change the properties of the identiﬁed modal
parameters. Hence in this case the assumption of white noise input is maintained but the
properties of the linear ﬁlter are also needed to be identiﬁed.
2.3.2
Rank of the problem and measurement system
In OMA methodology the "rank" of the problem is a very important concept to consider
when designing a test setup. In general, the rank of the problem is deﬁned as the sum
of the number of closely spaced modes and the number of independent noise sources. If
for example two noise sources are present, and two closely spaced modes are found, then
the rank of the problem is Rp = 2 + 2 = 4. The number of closely spaced resonances
in a given frequency band can be estimated by utilizing the rank of the spectral density
(SD) matrix. Consequently, if N sensors are used the SD matrix is N × N and since the
rank cannot exceed the smallest dimension of the matrix (N) the rank is limited to the
number of sensors N. However, if sensors are placed close to each other they are likely
to represent the same information, which consequently reduces the dimension of the SD
matrix.
In this thesis, there will be made a distinction between the "rank of the "problem" and the
"rank of the measurement system". The "rank of the problem" is an unknown quantity
that can be estimated and used to predict the required number of sensors. While the
"rank of the measurement system" is the physical rank of the SD matrix which can be
controlled in cases of many uncorrelated inputs.
Since a measurement system should be capable of describing all sources of noise and
physical responses the number of sensors should be larger than the rank of the problem.
Determining the rank of the problem is not a trivial task, and a recommendation by
Brincker [27] is to never assume the rank of the problem smaller than four. If one is
unable to increase the rank of the measurement system to match the rank of the problem
it becomes impossible to see or identify closely spaced resonances, while noise sources will
be leaked into the physical response and increase the modal parameter uncertainties.
Loading sources
Having enough sensors is not the only requirement when considering the rank of the
problem. The number of independent loading/input sources will also limit the obtainable
rank of the measurement system. If only one independent input is used the rank of the
measurement system is limited to 1. This means that it will be impossible (independent
of the identiﬁcation method) to estimate closely spaced resonances. The physical rank
of the input is deﬁned by the rank of the input correlation and spectral density matrix.
Further to see how the input can limit the rank of the measurement system consider the
input-output frequency domain relation.
X(ω) = Hxf(ω)F(ω)
(2.47)
19
==PAGE:31==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
Where Hfx(ω) is the FRF matrix of the system, and F, X are the frequency spectrum
of the input f(t) and output x(t) respectively. As explained by Ibrahim et al. [32] the
loading f(t) can be considered as the output of a linear ﬁlter driven by white noise e(t),
in the frequency domain described by.
F(ω) = Hfe(ω)E(ω)
(2.48)
Where Hfe(ω) is the loading ﬁlter describing the coloring of the loading (deviation from
white noise) and E(ω) is the frequency spectrum of the white noise. If the white noise
source E(ω) only contains a single source the resulting response X(ω) and its time domain
equivalent x(t) are governed by this single source and hence the covariance matrix of x(t)
is forced to rank 1. Utilizing the fundamental theorem (2.35) and (2.47, 2.48) one obtains
the relation from noise to the output.
Gx(ω) = H∗
xf(ω)H∗
fe(ω)Ge(ω)HT
fe(ω)HT
xf(ω)
(2.49)
Hence if white noise E(ω) only comes from one source the input noise SD matrix Ge(ω)
will be of rank one. And since the rank of a matrix product can never be larger than
the minimum rank of the contributing matrices the output SD matrix Gx(ω) will also be
forced to be of rank 1. It is also important to notice one can have multiple structural
loadings x(t) however if they correlate or originate from the same input source e(t) the
measurement system is still forced to be of rank 1.
The rank of the measurement system can in practice be estimated by the number of non-
zero singular values of the SD matrix (excluding noise singular values). While also the
number of dominating modes in certain frequency bands can be analyzed by plotting the
singular values as a function of frequency and counting the number of peaking singular
value lines, assuming that the number of sensors and uncorrelated inputs is higher than
the number of closely spaced modes.
Recap
From this discussion, it is seen that both the number of sensors and the number of
independent inputs can lead to rank deﬁciency problems. Which consequently can disable
the identiﬁcation of closely spaced resonances and the ability to separate noise sources
from physical information. This is especially important considering the applicability of
ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbation where the input is only generated from a single bearing which is
likely to limit the rank of the measurement system.
2.3.3

shapes and exponential decays.

the number of measurement points No, and cannot be controlled by the user as in ERA,
PR, or SSI. Although it is possible to increase the number of estimated modes by adding
pseudo measurements to the response vector without adding more physical information.
2.3.4
Stochastic Subspace Identiﬁcation (SSI)
Subspace identiﬁcation is a general collection of time domain identiﬁcation techniques
originally formulated within the control theory ﬁeld. The mathematical background is
thoroughly documented by Overschee and Moor [34] using a control theory terminology,
while an OMA oriented description is given by Brincker et al. [27, 35].
Two types of SSI techniques exist, correlation-driven SSI and data-driven SSI. The ﬁrst of
which uses the correlation function matrix as input while the data-driven SSI estimates
the correlation functions as an integrated part of the identiﬁcation. The correlation-driven
SSI is very similar to the Eigensystem Realization Algorithm (ERA) presented in section
2.3.5. This project will use the data-driven SSI according to algorithm 2 presented by
Overschee and Moor [34].
The general technique is formulated by assuming a linear and time-invariant (LTI) model
of a general process described by the discrete-time state-space model shown in (2.9). In
22
==PAGE:34==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
general subspace identiﬁcation can be applied for deterministic w(k) ≡0, v(k) ≡0 with
known input as well as stochastic systems with no external input u(k) = 0. Regarding
OMA only the latter is of interest since no external input is available and the system is as-
sumed to be excited by white noise w(k) while the measured output will be aﬀected by the
measurement noise v(k). The problem of stochastic subspace identiﬁcation (SSI) is then
to determine the state space matrices given the measurements x(k) ∈RNo, k = 1, 2, . . . , N
with No measurement channels and N total samples. In OMA the main interest is the
system matrix Ad from which the natural frequencies, damping ratios, and modeshapes
can be obtained.
The ﬁrst step in the data-driven SSI is to form a block Hankel matrix based on the shifted
output x(k) with 2s block rows.

columns of the projection matrix will represent free decays of the system given by the
initial conditions speciﬁed by Hp [35]. It should also be mentioned that the size of the
Hankel matrices easily becomes large such that the direct calculation of the projection
matrix in (2.67) becomes infeasible. The projection is therefore carried out by a QR
factorization where it is furthermore shown by Overschee and Moor [34] that only a part
of the R matrix is needed for the projection.

Diﬀerent forms of the weighting matrices form the standard SSI algorithms as the prin-

algorithms (CVA) as presented by Overschee and Moor [34]. The CVA option is always
used within this thesis.
2.3.5
Eigensystem Realization Algorithm (ERA)
The Eigensystem Realization Algorithm (ERA) was introduced in the mid-eighties by
Juang and Pappa in [36] and Pappa in [37] from a control theory setting. A short recap
of the main ideas of ERA is given by Brincker and Ventura in [27]. The ERA technique
is closely related to the correlation-driven SSI which is not used within this thesis.
ERA is also derived from the discrete-time state space formulation given in (2.9). The
deterministic homogeneous solution of (2.9) i.e. the free decay of the system is given by
(2.13) repeated here:
y(k) = CAk
dz0

where all correlation functions are arranged in the matrix Y(k). The initial conditions
corresponding to the No free decays are also collected in a matrix Z0.
Z0 = [z01, z02, . . . , z0n]

2.3.6
AR Models and Poly Reference (PR)
One of the simplest ways to perform OMA is to use an auto regressive (AR) model on the
free decays from the correlation functions. The AR model is obtained by only considering
the AR part of an ARMA model. As explained by Brincker et al. in [27] OMA ARMA
models are not widely used since problems often arise considering calculation time and
convergence of large nonlinear models. ARMA models can be directly applied to the
measured signal and will estimate all modal parameters in one step. However, in OMA
it is often an advantage to use the two-stage approach of ﬁrst estimating the correlation
functions and then ﬁtting a free decay model. This sets the stage for introducing the
AR identiﬁcation approach. Since correlation functions are considered free decays of the
system only the AR (homogeneous) part is required. Hence by discarding the MA term
and moving the AR term to the lhs. of (2.14) the general AR model becomes.
y(n) −

(2.91)

nomial coeﬃcient matrices from a given free decay y(n) ∈RNo, n = 1, 2, . . . N with No
measurement and N samples. This procedure will in general be referred to as poly refer-
ence (PR) identiﬁcation throughout the remainder of this thesis.
It starts by building one large block Hankel matrix with NA + 1 block rows and splitting
it into a large block Hankel matrix H1 with NA block rows and a single block row Hankel
matrix H2.

2.3.7
Frequency domain methods and the frequency domain de-
composition (FDD)
Previous sections covered some of the main time-identiﬁcation methods within OMA
where interpretation of CF functions as free decays is mainly used to provide modal pa-
rameters. This section will cover some of the main frequency domain approaches used
within OMA. The frequency domain techniques base their estimates on the spectral den-
sity functions which hold valuable frequency information from the CF functions. In the
time domain frequency information is spread throughout all time, however in the fre-
quency domain modes naturally decompose since each mode can be considered dominant
within a band of its natural frequency. This is the main advantage of the frequency do-
main approach. However, a disadvantage of the frequency domain techniques is that the
spectral density estimates often suﬀer from biases such as leakage.
28
==PAGE:40==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
Basic Frequency Domain
The basic frequency domain technique (classical frequency domain) is a natural extension
of frequency peak-picking which additionally provides an estimate of the modeshape. The
main idea is shortly presented below in the format given by Brincker and Ventura [27].
The method works by considering a single frequency line at a resonant peak in the SD
function. At this frequency, it assumed that the structural response x(t) can be described
by a 1-DOF response.
x(t) = ψq(t)
(2.99)
Where ψ is the mode shape and q(t) is the modal coordinate of the isolated mode.
Utilizing the deﬁnition of the CF matrix (2.23), the CF matrix becomes.
Rx(τ) = E

x(t)x(t + τ)T
= ψE

q(t)q(t + τ)T
ψT = Rq(τ)ψψT
(2.100)
Where the modeshape has been moved outside the expectation since it is not dependent on
time and the CF function Rq of the modal coordinate has been recognized. Transformation
to the frequency domain via Fourier transformation (2.33) yields the spectral density
matrix of x(t).
Gx(ωk) = Gq(ω)ψψT
(2.101)
Where Gq(ωk) is the SD function of q(t) at the chosen frequency ωk. Since the SD matrix
is proportional to the outer product of the mode shape ψ it will be of rank one hence
all the columns and rows are linearly dependent and all estimates of the mode shape. As
explained by Brincker [27] an improved mode shape estimate can be found by taking the
average over all columns of Gx. Moreover, The damping can be estimated utilizing the
half-power criteria B = 2ζnfn similar to FRF peak picking described by Ewins [10].
The basic frequency method assumes that a single mode is active at the chosen frequency.
However, this is rarely the case and the resulting estimates will become heavily biased for
the case of closely spaced modes. More speciﬁc limitations are summarized by Svendsen
[21]. Moreover to ﬁnd all resonances in the response and validate that no closely spaced
resonances exist, requires that all SD functions are analyzed. Since the number of SD
functions equals N 2
o this quickly becomes a tedious process while noise on the SD function
also makes it hard to even spot close resonances. This downside leads to the requirement
of a more general frequency domain technique as will be described below.
Frequency domain decomposition (FDD)
Instead of modeling the response signal x(t) as a 1-DOF system by peak picking the
response x(t) is modeled as a linear combination of Nm modal coordinates.
x(t) =
Nm
X
n=1
ψnqn = Ψq(t).
(2.102)
Where Ψ ∈RNo×Nm is the modal matrix with modeshapes in the columns and
q(t) = {q1(t), q2(t), . . . , qNm}T is the vector of modal coordinates. Similar to the derivation
for the 1-DOF modal system above the SD matrix of the response Gx(ω) becomes.
Gx(ω) = ΨGq(ω)ΨT
(2.103)
It is then assumed that the modal coordinates are uncorrelated hence the oﬀ-diagonal
elements of the modal CF matrix Gq become zero. Consequently, the SD matrix becomes
29
==PAGE:41==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
diagonal and positive according to the Parseval theorem. Further, it is known that the
SD matrix is Hermitian [27] and that the modeshapes are generally complex quantities
hence the Transpose is replaced by the Hermitian. From these assumptions (2.103) can
be written.
Gx(ω) = Ψ[g2
n(ω)]ΨH
(2.104)
Where g2
n(ω) are power spectral densities i.e. the diagonal elements of Gq(ω). By rec-
ognizing the simple form of (2.104) as the SVD of a Hermitian, and positive deﬁnite
matrix.
Gx(ω) = USUH = U[s2
n]UH
(2.105)
If follows from (2.104,2.105) that the singular values s2
n should be interpreted as spectral
densities of the modal coordinates, and that the singular vector matrix U should be
interpreted as the modal matrix Ψ. This interpretation of the SVD was patented by
Brincker and Andersen until 2020 [38].
Before continuing the explanation of how model parameters are practically estimated using
the FDD the main assumption of the FDD should be discussed. According to (2.38) the
modal decomposition of the SD matrix under the assumption of white noise input cannot
be considered directly related to the modal model, while it is also clear that it cannot be
decomposed by the SVD. This realization means that the FDD will always be approximate
and the modal parameters will be biased. The quality of the assumption can be further
analyzed from two perspectives: The modal decomposition in the frequency domain under
the assumption of white noise input and the assumption of uncorrelated inputs. It is shown
by Brincker et al. [27]. That in the case of lightly damped structures and well-separated
modes the modal participation factor becomes approximately proportional to the mode
shape γ ≈c2
nψ. Where c2
n is a positive constant. Hence the modal decomposition of the
SD matrix (2.38) reduces to.
Gx(ω) ≈
Nm
X
n=1
 c2
nψψT
iω −λn
+ c2
nψ∗ψH
−iω −λ∗
n

(2.106)
This form of modal decomposition can be represented by the SVD eq(2.105) and hence
the FDD is a good approximation when modes are lightly damped and well separated
assuming that the input is white noise.
The assumption of uncorrelated inputs also leads to a very similar modal decomposition of
the SD matrix [27]. And the FDD approximation can be considered good. The challenge
is then to evaluate the degree of correlation between modal coordinates. As shown in
[27] the modal coordinates will be perfectly uncorrelated for vanishing damping or for
well-separated modes. Hence when the white noise assumption is not valid it is still often
enough to have well-separated lightly damped modes to obtain good FDD approximations.
Since the SVD of the Gx(ωk) exists at every discrete frequency ωk at where the SD matrix
is deﬁned raises the question of where the modeshapes should be estimated. If the number
of outputs No matches the number of active modes Nm in the response x(t) and the FDD
assumptions are perfectly satisﬁed all modeshapes could theoretically be estimated from
any frequency ωk in the spectrum. However, this approach is in practice never used. The
main problem is that the SVD forces the singular vectors and hence also the modeshapes
30
==PAGE:42==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
to be geometrically orthogonal. Whereas in structural dynamics, the mode shapes are
not geometrically orthogonal but only orthogonal with respect to the mass matrix, and
hence a large bias is expected. Therefore it is important to estimate the mode shapes at
a frequency where the bias introduced by forcing the singular vectors to be geometrically
orthogonal has the smallest inﬂuence on the result. Brincker et al. [27] proves that this
bias can be minimized and states the following rule: "Any mode shape in FDD should be
estimated as the ﬁrst singular vector at a frequency line where the corresponding singular
value is dominating". Hence in practice, the singular values of the SD matrix are plotted
for all discrete frequencies and the user chooses the dominant frequency peaks.
This simple form of the FDD as presented above only provides the modes shapes and
natural frequencies.
Since the natural frequencies are simply picked as peaks in the
singular values it is restricted to the accuracy of the frequency resolution of the SD matrix.
While an estimation of the damping ratio is not provided. Therefore the FDD is normally
followed by a second step where the estimate of the natural frequency and damping ratio
in enhanced. This procedure is performed by inverse Fourier transformation (IFFT) of a
narrow frequency band around the mode of interest which approximately provides a 1-
DOF response of the mode which is fed into an arbitrator time domain SDOF technique.
In this project, the 1-DOF ITD is mainly used (described in section 2.3.3). However,
this direct method is not good when modes are closely spaced since the narrow band will
also include frequency components from other modes. Therefore a modal ﬁlter is used to
isolate the target frequency. Considering a frequency band with a certain set of active
modes, expanding (2.104) writes.
Gx(ω) = ψ1g2
1ψH
1 + ψ2g2
2ψH
2 + · · ·
(2.107)
It is desired to isolate the modal spectral densities g2
n hence a matrix V = [v1, v2, . . .] is
deﬁned to obtain an orthogonal set of vectors.
VHΨ = I
(2.108)
Hence V = (Ψ+)H and the column vectors v1,v2, . . . becomes orthogonal to the mode-
shapes ψ1,ψ2, . . .. Hence the inner product of any vectors from the orthogonal set over
the SD matrix isolates the spectral density of the corresponding modal coordinate as.
vH
n Gx(ω)vn = g2
n(ω)
(2.109)
The spectral density of the modal coordinate can then be taken back to the time domain
by IFFT where contributions from other modes have been removed and the estimate using
an SDOF ﬁt is signiﬁcantly improved. Since the SVD of Gx is only the size of No it is
impossible to perform modal ﬁltering for more modes than the number of measurements
No. Further a compromise has to be taken when considering the number of modes in-
cluded in the modal ﬁlter (2.109). On one hand, using fewer modes makes the ﬁlter over
determined and more robust towards noise and estimation errors. On the other hand, in
order to allow for the modeling of residues from close modes, more modes than just the
modes needed to be estimated should be included in the ﬁlter.
2.3.8
Modal participation
The described identiﬁcation techniques all yield estimated eigenvalues (poles) and eigen-
vectors (mode shapes) of the analyzed system under the assumption of the modal decom-
position in the time or frequency domain, see (2.36,2.38). However, to fully determine the
31
==PAGE:43==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
decomposition the modal participation vectors L also need to be determined. The modal
participation vectors are related to the input forces and can be understood as a measure
of the eﬃciency of excitation of each excitation degree of freedom of each mode [11]. The
determination of the modal participation factors also serves as a useful tool when deciding
between physical or computational poles since computational poles will normally show up
with signiﬁcantly smaller participation factors. Moreover, the determination of partici-
pation factors involves ﬁtting the measured correlation and spectral densities, hence the
quality of the ﬁt can be used as a model validation. As explained by Brincker et al. in
[27] determination of the modal participation factors can be performed both in the time
and frequency domain, both covered below.
2.3.9
Fitting correlation functions
In the time domain, the modal participation factors can be found by performing a ﬁt
of the estimated modal model to the correlation functions used for the identiﬁcation
process. Assuming that all modes are deﬁned by complex conjugate pairs of eigenvalues
and vectors the summation of the modal decomposition of the correlation functions (2.36)
can be extended to 2Nm modal parameters. Restricting to only positive time lags the CF
matrix sampled at time lag τ = k∆t can be written as
Rx(k) = 2π

2.3.11
Automation of OMA
OMA automation (AOMA) is important considering the optimal use of non-invasive ﬂuid
ﬁlm perturbation, i.e. with the desired online monitoring it is required to obtain contin-
uous estimates of modal parameters without user interaction. Therefore a simple AOMA
routine has been developed and utilized during this project. The routine draws inspiration
from Paulsen et al. [24], and Dreher et al. [25] who both developed automated OMA
methods based on the SSI technique. The routine is also very similar to the p-LSCF
routine developed in section 2.4.4 with minor diﬀerences due to the availability of mode
shapes that are directly estimated by the OMA methods. The routine is also further auto-
mated minimizing required user interaction. It should also be noted that full automation
is not strived for. Automation is only acquired after setting several crucial parameters
which require expertise knowledge and prior analysis. However, the routine can be used
for any system by re-tuning the AOMA parameters.
33
==PAGE:45==
2.3. OPERATIONAL MODAL ANALYSIS (OMA)
The AOMA routine is based on the creation of stabilization diagrams, hence the SSI,
ERA, and PR will be used since these methods allow for a model order variation and
construction of stabilization diagrams. The routine follows the steps graphically presented
in Figure 2.1
Figure 2.1: Flowchart of the AOMA routine(s).
The steps are summarized as the following:
(a) Calculate modal parameters using the SSI, ERA, or PR method for diﬀerent model
orders ∀n ∈[nmin, nmax]. The SSI and ERA allow for sub-solutions by truncating
the n number of singular values of respectively equation (2.71) (2.87) whereas in
PR identiﬁcation the problem has to bed resolved at every order NA.
(b) Certain spurious modal parameters are discarded by purely evaluating the pole
λn.
A Modal parameter set is discarded when its damping is strictly less than
0 or greater than 1, i.e. showing signs of instability or non-oscillatory dynamics
respectively. It is also required that the complex conjugate of the corresponding
pole exists in the set of modal parameters since these poles are unlikely to represent
physical dynamics.
(c) A stabilization diagram is built by comparing model estimates of model order n to
model order n −1. If their relative diﬀerence is within the accuracy parameters
εf, εζ, and εψ that modal set is identiﬁed as stable. The comparison of poles follows
the logic also used for the p-LSCF routine shown in the upcoming Chapter Figure
2.2.
(d) An agglomerative hierarchical clustering using the dMACXP distance measure is
used to cluster the stable modal sets. A cluster cutoﬀdMACXPcut and the number
of returned clusters Nclust must be speciﬁed.
(e) Global estimates are obtained by a weighted average (2.124).
(f) Lastly to avoid duplicated sub-clusters caused by parameter variation through dif-
ferent model orders, only the cluster with the highest number of modal parameters
is retained if two clusters are within the frequency tolerance εf,c. Care should be
taken in selecting the value of εf,c to balance the removal of closely-spaced modes
without discarding relevant sub-clusters.
Global modal parameters are calculated using a weighting vector w. The weighting vector
is obtained by MACXP comparison between all estimates of the respective modal cluster
34
==PAGE:46==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
as described by Paulsen in [24].
Illustrated for the damping ratio ζ the mean µ and
standard deviation σ are calculated as.

The description of the AOMA routine has been kept short since other sections of this thesis
will discuss the subject further. The reader is referred to section 2.4.5 for discussion of
stabilization diagrams, and section 4.8.3 for the general use and discussion of the AOMA
routines.
2.4
Experimental Modal analysis (EMA)
Experimental Modal analysis (EMA) is a broad collection of vibration measurement tools
used to identify the natural frequencies, damping ratios, and modeshapes of a structure.
The general terminology is well documented by Ewins [10] and Heylen et al. [11]. EMA
is performed by exciting a structure or system with a known input while measuring the
resulting dynamic response(s). Due to the knowledge of input forces (unlike OMA) a
complete input-output relation can be obtained in form of a frequency response function
(FRF). It is also possible to conduct EMA analysis in the time domain using impulse re-
sponse functions, however, this is not utilized during this project. When performing EMA
it is often required to remove the system from its operating state to obtain controllable
conditions where input forces can be easily measured. This is a signiﬁcant disadvantage
of EMA since in-situ measurements are unlikely to be possible which often results in
operational down-times during testing.
2.4.1
Frequency response function (FRF) estimation
Single input single output (SISO)
For a single-input single-output (SISO) system, the output v(t) of a linear time-invariant
system due to any input u(t) is commonly deﬁned as the convolution integral between
the system’s impulse response function h(t) and the input u(t) as [26].
v(t) =
Z t
0
h(t −τ)u(τ)dτ
(2.125)
Since (2.125) is a convolution integral taking the Fourier transform simply results in the
product.
V (ω) = H(ω)U(ω)
(2.126)
Where V (ω) and U(ω) are the frequency spectrum (fourier transforms) of the true undis-
turbed output and input respectively and H(ω) is the frequency response function (FRF).
The FRF is consequently deﬁned as
H(ω) = V (ω)
U(ω)
(2.127)
Ideally, the measured input and output signals are noise-free, however, in practice signal
noise cannot be avoided. Assuming that the measured time signals consist of the true
35
==PAGE:47==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
signal plus the measurement noise, e.g. for the output x(t) = v(t) + dv(t). The FRF is
approximated by
bH(ω) = V (ω) + Dv(ω)
U(ω) + Du(ω) = X(ω)
F(ω)
(2.128)
Where bH(ω) is the estimated FRF and F(ω) and X(ω) are the spectra of the measured
input and output respectively. Du(ω) and Dv(ω) are likewise the spectra of the input
and output measurement noise respectively. In practice estimating the FRF by Fourier
transformation of the input f(t) and output x(t) signals is not ideal since this has the
risk of F(ω) = 0. Therefore in practice, the FRF is found utilizing spectral densities.
Postmultiplying by the complex conjugate of the input or output spectrum yields two
diﬀerent estimates of the FRF. And by recognizing the power and cross-spectral densities
one obtains
H1(ω) = bH(ω)F ∗(ω)
F ∗(ω) = X(ω)F ∗(ω)
F(ω)F ∗(ω) = Gxf
Gff
(2.129)
H2(ω) = bH(ω)X∗(ω)
X∗(ω) = X(ω)X∗(ω)
F(ω)X∗(ω) = Gxx
Gfx
(2.130)
Where Gff, Gxx and Gxf, Gfx are the power and cross-spectral densities of the corre-
sponding measured signals respectively. These two estimates H1 and H2 are more robust
than the direct estimate since calculating the spectral densities can be done via averaging
and Welch’s method such that uncorrelated noise sources can be reduced. By inserting
(2.128) into (2.129) and (2.130), after reduction one obtains.
H1(ω) = H(ω)

1 + GDu(ω)
Gu(ω)
−1
(2.131)
H2(ω) = H(ω) + GDv(ω)
Guv(ω)
(2.132)
From (2.131) and (2.132) it is evident that when the input measurement noise GDu is
vanishing the H1 estimator converges to the true FRF. While if the output measurement
noise GDv is vanishing the H2 estimator converges to the true FRF. Near resonances,
the input force is small and likely to be degraded by noise while the output is large and
typically signiﬁcantly larger than the measurement noise. By combining these observa-
tions one can conclude that the H2 estimator will perform best at resonances. While the
opposite argument can be given at antiresonances where the H1 estimator is preferred.
A measure of the FRF quality can be deﬁned by the ratio of the H1 and H2 estimators
known as the coherence. For a SISO system, a measure of the least squared error is
deﬁned by the coherence function (squared) as.
γ2 = H1(ω)
H2(ω) =
|Gyf|2
Gxx(ω)Gff(ω),
0 ≤γ2 ≤1
(2.133)
The coherence function can vary between 0 and 1, with a value of 1 indicating a perfect,
noise-free linear relationship between the input and output. A coherence value of less
than one is usually indicative of measurement noise, non-linearity, or leakage issues.
36
==PAGE:48==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
The phase of the FRF estimates (H1, H2) is by deﬁnition the same (unlike their magnitude)
[10], and can be conveniently calculated by the arccos function restricting the phase within
ϕ(ω) ∈[0, 180]deg.
ϕ(ω) = 180
π arccos

|H(ω)|
