(2.134)
Multiple input multiple output (MIMO)
By generalization to multiple inputs f(t) ∈RNi and multiple output x(t) ∈RNo (MIMO)
the input-output relation (2.126) becomes a matrix equation
X(ω)No×1 = H(ω)No×NiF(ω)Ni×1
(2.135)
Here the true input u(t) and output v(t) signals are represented as their measured quan-
tities f(t) and x(t).
Similar to the SISO case the H1 approach assumes no noise on the input and uncorre-
lated noise do(t) ∈RNo on the response. One obtains by adding the noise in (2.135) and
dropping the ω dependency for clarity.
X −Do = HF
(2.136)
Where Do is the frequency spectrum of the output noise. Postmultipying both sides of
(2.136) by FH and recognizing spectral densities leads to.
XFH −DoFH = HFFH
⇒
Gxf −Gdof = HGff
(2.137)
Since the output noise is assumed uncorrelated to the input the cross-spectral density
between the output noise and input Gdof becomes zero. And the MIMO H1 estimator
becomes.
[H1(ω)]No×Ni = [Gxf]No×Ni [Gff]−1
Ni×Ni
(2.138)
From (2.138), a very important necessity is that the power spectral density matrix Gff
has to be non-singular. Which means that the input forces should be uncorrelated. This
also has a good physical interpretation since if the input signals were correlated it would
be impossible to distinguish between their eﬀect on the measured response. By a similar
approach the MIMO H2 estimator can be derived by assuming no noise on the output
and uncorrelated noise on the input [11].
[H2(ω)]No×Ni [Gfx]No×Ni = [Gxx]No×No
(2.139)
However one sees that the H2 estimate only uniquely exists when the number of inputs
equals the number of outputs No = Ni. Since when No > Ni a generalized inverse will
be needed, resulting in a non-unique estimate. For this reason, the H2 estimator is often
only used in the SISO case.
To evaluate the estimation error of the FRF a coherence function will also be deﬁned for
the MIMO case. However the ordinary coherence function has little meaning since the
outputs will have contributions from diﬀerent inputs and therefore lead to a small ordinary
37
==PAGE:49==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
coherence. Hence, a multiple coherence function γM can be deﬁned by comparing the
measured and estimated power spectrum Gxx as given by Ewins [10] as.

γ2
M(ω)

No×1 = G−1
xxGxfG−1
ff Gfx
(2.140)
Which is a measure of how well the response is predicted by all inputs.
The normal coherence function (2.133) is however valuable in judging the correlation
between input forces, where a small coherence is desired.
For the MIMO case similar pros and cons can be said about the H1 and H2 estimators
as explained for the SISO case. The H1 estimator assumes no noise on the input which
is a good approximation at antiresonances where the force is large compared to the mea-
surement noise. And the H2 estimator assumes no noise on the response which is a good
assumption near resonances where the displacement is large compared to measurement
noise.
MIMO experimental tests often deal with a vast amount of FRFs making it cumbersome
to analyze the global dynamics of the system. Therefore it is common to process the FRF
matrix into one manageable visualization where resonances are clearly identiﬁable. Such
processing tools are known as modal indicator functions (MIF). This thesis often indirectly
utilizes MIFs to gain an initial overview of the measured dynamics, and a discussion on
the various types of MIFs is therefore provided in Appendix B.2.
Note also that single input multiple output (SIMO) testing is utilized heavily within this
thesis. SIMO FRFs can simply be obtained by repeating the SISO FRF calculation for
multiple outputs, or by utilizing the MIMO formulation for only one input.
2.4.2
Random Excitation - Shaker testing
Shaker excitation is the main excitation source used within this thesis when performing
EMA. Hence a short discussion of its use is given here.
Excitation of a structure is
commonly performed using a mechanical shaker that excites the structure through a
ﬁxed shaker-stinger-structure connection [39]. The stinger is typically a thin metal rod
which ensures that the shaker only applies forces in a longitudinal direction. Since the
shaker is connected to the structure an interaction between the structure and the shaker
is expected, which typically leads to drops in coherence near resonances of the structure
where the shaker interaction is most signiﬁcant. This is a downside of this test setup
whereas impact testing does not have this problem. It is important to measure the force
between the structure and the stinger to ensure the correct ratio between the response and
input spectra. For example, if the spectra of the input signal sent to the shaker is used
instead of the measured force the resulting FRF matrix will describe the dynamics of the
structure including the dynamics of the stinger, shaker, and electrical circuit. Typically
the test is performed with a small number of inputs and several outputs.
2.4.3
Single degree of freedom (SDOF) identiﬁcation
The most simple modal parameter identiﬁcation methods are the single degree of freedom
(SDOF) methods. A few diﬀerent SDOF approaches are available such as peak picking,
circle ﬁtting, and many more described by Ewins [10] and Heylen [11]. The SDOF ap-
proach uses the SDOF assumption that in the vicinity of a resonance, the behavior of
the system is dominated by a single mode. This assumption is good in the case of well-
separated and lightly damped modes [10]. In this thesis, the SDOF approach is mainly
38
==PAGE:50==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
used to obtain fast baseline estimates and for academic comparison of modal identiﬁcation
methods.
The SDOF method used within this thesis simply consists of ﬁtting the measured FRFs
by an SDOF model. The SDOF compliance H = X
F can be obtained by Laplace transform
of the SDOF version of the equations of motion (2.1):
X(s) = (ms2 + cs + k)−1F(s),

(2.141)
Where m, k and c are the modal mass, stiﬀness, and viscous damping respectively. And

Modeshapes can also be estimated by an SDOF peak-picking approach. The approach
is described by Inman [40] where the elements of the modeshape vector ψo,n at output
o and mode n is estimated by the magnitude of the corresponding FRF |Ho(ωn)|, while
the sign (phase of 0◦or 180◦) of the modal coordinate is determined by the slope of the
corresponding phase ∠Ho(ωn). The mode shapes are then built by sequentially analyzing
all FRFs from No outputs at all natural frequencies of interest. This method can be
cumbersome since the determination of the phase slope can be hard to determine especially
considering systems of high damping, and therefore often requires user interaction for
every modal coordinate. Hence another SDOF method for modeshape estimation is used.
This method uses the imaginary(or real) part of the FRF as a direct approximation to
the modal coordinate. This idea can be illustrated by evaluating the compliance FRF
eq.(2.142) at a resonance ωn =
p
k/m.
Hx(ωn) = (ic
p
k/m)−1
(2.147)
Hence it is seen that the compliance becomes fully imaginary and can be used as a direct
measure of the modal amplitude of the n’th mode. A similar expression to eq.(2.147) can
be obtained for the mobility and accelerance FRF where they respectively become fully
real and imaginary. For more complex structures the imaginary (or real) part of the FRF
will still approximately scale with the modal coordinate in the vicinity of a resonance,
however for increasing modal coupling and damping this approximation becomes poor.
2.4.4
Poly reference least squares complex frequency (p-LSCF)
identiﬁcation technique
Applying the conventional peak-picking method and SDOF-ﬁt to analyze FRFs across
multiple channels and resonances can be a tedious procedure. Moreover, this method
only provides local estimates and is limited in dealing with closely spaced modes due
to its SDOF assumption. In order to overcome these limitations, a more eﬃcient, and
global modal identiﬁcation technique has been implemented in this project. The cho-
sen technique is known as the Poly-Reference Least Squares Complex Frequency-domain
(p-LSCF), which is a MIMO extension of the well-established Least Squares Complex
Frequency-domain (LSCF) method [41]. The LSCF method can be viewed as the frequency-
domain counterpart of the widely used time-domain Least-Squares Complex Exponential
(LSCE) method [11]. The p-LSCF is also commercially known as the PolyMAX estimator
which is a trademark of LMS-International. If the testing is single input the p-LSCF is
equivalent to the LSCF, however for MIMO testing the p-LSCF is advantageous. The
p-LSCF method was ﬁrst presented by Guillaume et al. in [41] and further tested and
described in [39, 42, 43]. A simpliﬁed derivation enough to be used for implementation is
presented here.
The p-LSCF assumes that the relationship between output o = 1, . . . No and all Ni inputs
can be modeled in the frequency domain by means of the right matrix-fraction description
(RMFD) [39].
Ho(ωk) = Bo(ωk)A−1(ωk)
(2.148)
Where Bo(ωk) ∈C1×Ni is the numerator row-vector polynomial and A(ωk) ∈CNi×Ni is
the denominator matrix polynomial while Ho(ωk) ∈C1×Ni is the FRF row-vector from
all Ni inputs to output o. All are deﬁned at the discrete frequency ωk (k = 1, 2, . . . , Nf).
40

theoretically be computed from the numerator coeﬃcients, however, it is common to take
another approach as will be discussed next.

In modal analysis, it is desired to estimate global unbiased modal parameters independent
of signal noise and other disturbances. It is therefore commonly recommended to over-
specify the model order signiﬁcantly and build a so-called stabilization chart/diagram.
42
==PAGE:54==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
The stabilization diagram is built by plotting the estimated poles as a function of the
model order. Using the stabilization diagram it is possible to separate the physical modal
parameters from the mathematical/spurious ones. By interpretation of the stabilization
diagram, the modal model order is determined and the corresponding physical poles and
participation vectors are obtained.
The mode shapes Ψ are found in a second step by the Least Squares Frequency Domain
(LSFD) estimator.
This estimator uses the so-called pole residue model, speciﬁed in
equation (2.162) for a receptance FRF. Assuming that the poles and participation vectors
obtained from the stabilization diagram are consistent estimates (asymptotic estimates)
the least squares estimation is also consistent.
Further using a weighted least square
improves eﬃciency and results in an ML (maximum likelihood) estimation of the modes
shapes [39].

LR, UR ∈RNo×Ni are respectively introduced to model the lower and upper residuals of
the out-of-band modes. Equation (2.162) is solved in a least-squares sense very similar to
how spectral density functions are ﬁtted in section 2.3.10. If the participation vectors γn
are known from previous steps, the modeshapes ψn can be estimated directly. Alterna-
tively, if only the poles λn are available, the residues Rn = ψnγT
n can be estimated. From
the residues, the corresponding modeshapes and participation vectors can be estimated
by the singular value decomposition (SVD) of Rn, assuming that the residue matrix Rn
is of rank 1, i.e. only the ﬁrst singular value will be non-zero. Under this assumption, the
singular value decomposition becomes [44].

If the Residue matrix Rn is not of rank one (e.g pole with multiplicity 2 or more), it
is impossible to uniquely derive the corresponding modeshapes and participation vector.

2.4.5
Practical implementation of the p-LSCF
A semi-automatic implementation of the p-LSCF identiﬁcation method is developed and
used within this thesis. The main mathematical approach has been described above and
the practical aspects of stabilization diagrams and identiﬁcation workﬂow will now be
presented. The main ideas of the general p-LSCF identiﬁcation technique are described
in the literature e.g. by Guillaume et al. [41, 43] while inspiration is also taken from
Paulsen et al. [24].
Stabilization diagrams are built by comparing the poles λi,p and participation vectors
γi,p of model order p to the poles λj,p−1 and participation vectors γj,p−1 of a one-order-
lower model. If their relative diﬀerences are within preset tolerances the corresponding
pole-participation vector set is labeled accordingly. The logic imposed for the labeling of
the stability status is presented in Figure 2.2, with inspiration from Heylen et al. [11].
Starting from a user-deﬁned maximum model order pmax all poles of every model are
compared to the model order below until pmin. The comparison of participation vectors is
performed using the MACX criterion deﬁned in (2.45). Stable poles will tend to appear at
43
==PAGE:55==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
identical frequencies and stabilize for increasing model order whereas the spurious poles
with no physical interpretation will tend to vary randomly in the spectrum.
Figure 2.2: Logic imposed for labeling of stability status.
i = 1, . . . , pNi/2 and j =
1, . . . , (p −1)Ni/2 are modal parameters indices and p is the model order.
In structural modal analysis, modal parameters always come in complex conjugate pairs.
Hence to simplify the analysis of poles, participation factors, and modeshapes only the
modal parameter sets with positive imaginary part are saved. Also if pNi is odd the
eigenvalue problem will provide poles that are not-complex conjugates. These poles are
simply discarded since they are unlikely to represent physical dynamics.
The check of the damping factor determines if a pole has been modeled as stable or
unstable (same as checking the sign of the poles’ real part). Changing the sign in the
polynomial basis function Ωr(ωk) = e(±)iωk∆tr consequently switches the stability status
which can be advantageous in certain situations, see [39]. An overdamped pole ζ > 1
does not correspond to oscillatory dynamics and also receives the same label.
A stabilization diagram example is shown in Figure 2.3. The Figure is a zoom of a larger
stabilization chart from an analysis of the test rig foundation, which explains the high
model order. ( see appendix C for further information)
Figure 2.3:
Stabilization chart example.
Zoom of Figure C.7
The stabilization diagram indicates that 3
physical modes are likely to be present at
29, 34, and 36Hz by repeated stable poles.
Interpretation of the stabilization diagram
is semi-automated using hierarchical clus-
tering.
The modal assurance criterion
MACXP (2.46) is utilized by deﬁning the
distance measure
dMACXP = 1 −MACXP
(2.164)
The dMACXP is then 0 when a modal pa-
rameter set is equal/parallel and 1 if they
44
==PAGE:56==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
are orthogonal in a (pole,modal-vector)
space. A dendrogram is then built using the dMACXP distance measure as the crite-
rion for deﬁning clusters. Clusters can then be returned in two ways: By specifying a
cluster cutoﬀdMACXPcut, or by specifying the maximum number of clusters Nmax,clust.
Both parameters require tuning with respect to diﬀerent analyzed structures. The cluster
cutoﬀdMACXPcut should be chosen such that all signiﬁcant clusters are separated, while
the choosing of Nmax,clust can be estimated from the stabilization diagram. The number
of clusters should correspond to the number of unique stable frequencies observed in the
stabilization diagram which is often larger than the amount of stabilized modal sets since
stable poles can be falsely identiﬁed. Using Figure 2.3 as an example, Nmax,clust should
be chosen as 5 since stable poles are found at approximately 5 distinct frequencies 25, 29,
34, 36, 37Hz.
Finally to obtain global modal parameters a weighted mean of the Nclust highest populated
clusters is then extracted. The weighting follows a principle using the MACXP criteria to
improve accuracy as shown by Paulsen in [31, 24]. Again using Figure 2.3 as an example,
only the Nclust = 3 highest populated clusters should be returned.
A simpler alternative to hierarchical clustering is frequency picking. Here, the frequency
of stabilized poles is identiﬁed by the user, and global modal parameters are estimated by
a weighted average across model orders falling within a tolerance of the chosen frequencies.
The general workﬂow of the p-LSCF identiﬁcation routine is presented graphically in
Figure 2.4. Each step of the workﬂow is explained in more detail below:
a) The routine takes a 3D FRF matrix of size HNf×No×Ni as input.
b) The normal equation system matrix Mpmax is build for the highest model order pmax
using (2.154). The denominator coeﬃcients α are then eﬃciently determined for all
p ∈[pmin, pmax] by considering submatrices of Mpmax, (2.158). From the denominator
coeﬃcients corresponding poles and participation, vectors are obtained from (2.160).
c) Build and interpret stabilization diagram following logic from Table 2.2.
d) Pair stable model parameter sets using simple frequency picking or hierarchical
clustering with dMACXP distance measure. Return weighted mean estimates of
stable clusters following equation (2.124).
e) Estimate mode shapes using the LSFD method, (2.162).
f) Validate the modal model by comparing synthesized and measured FRFs.
45
==PAGE:57==
2.4. EXPERIMENTAL MODAL ANALYSIS (EMA)
Figure 2.4: Flow diagram of the full p-LSCF EMA identiﬁcation technique.

Chapter 3
Test Facilities
This chapter will describe the main test facility utilized during this project, namely the
Rotor-Bearing-Foundation test rig. The main components of the test rig will be presented
together with the hydraulic and data acquisition system. An on-demand run-out compen-
sation will also be explained which is used to remove run-out noise and harmonics from
the sampled data.
The description of the test facility has been given by numerous students including Wagner
(2023) [9], Bull (2021) [8], Jensen (2019) [7], and Gani (2018) [6] since when the layout of
the test rig has hardly changed. However several crucial components have been repaired
and changed during this thesis, including new journal pads, re-polished rotor shaft, new
oil injection nozzles, and oil ﬁlters.
The Rotor-bearing-Foundation test rig is located at the Technical University of Denmark
(DTU) in the laboratories of Building 414. The test rig was initially designed and built
by Østergaard [2] and has undergone several alternations and improvements since then.
The purpose of the test rig is to imitate an industrial overhung compressor supported
by an active tilting pad journal bearing (ATPJB). Most recently the test rig was run by
Wagner [9] who experienced a destructive breakdown. Hence during this project, the test
rig has been disassembled and assembled to replace worn-out and broken parts.
3.1
Rotor-Bearing-Foundation test rig
A picture of the assembled rotor-bearing-foundation test rig is shown in Figure 3.1 with
indices explained in Table 3.1.
3.1.1
Components
Foundation
The test rig is mounted on a (3500 × 1100 × 105)mm T-slot grey cast iron foundation
10 of approximately 3 tons. The foundation is situated on a rubber mat to isolate the
test rig from the laboratory ﬂoor. The left side of the rotor-bearing-foundation test rig as
shown in Figure 3.1 is denoted the drive-end while the right side is denoted the free-end
or overhung-end. An experimental modal analysis of the foundation without the rotor
shaft is presented in Appendix C.

Rotor shaft
The main ﬂexible rotor shaft 4 is supported by the ATPJB 7 in the overhung end, and
a spherical ball bearing 3 in the drive end. During this project, the journal surface (the
surface inside the ATPJB) has been re-polished to a surface roughness of ra = 0.06 µm
and a measured diameter of 99.496 mm. At the overhung end of the shaft, 2 discs 8 of 80
and 100mm thickness can be mounted to mimic the loading of an overhung compressor.
Further description and modal analysis of the rotor shaft with numerous components is
given in Appendix D.

Ball bearings and pedestals
The ball-bearing pedestal 3 contains a spherical ball bearing of type SKF 2210 ETN9
rated for max 9500 rpm. The coupling pedestal (or layshaft) 11 contains two ball bear-
ings of type SKF 6208-2Z and SKF 6207-2Z both rated for max 9000 rpm. The ball
bearing pedestal and ATPJB housing are supported by stiﬀeners 13 and 15 mounted
by Mortensen [45] to remove unwanted transverse vibrations. The tightening torque used
to ﬁx the stiﬀeners is known to aﬀect the dynamics of the foundation. In this thesis,
the bearing and ATPJB stiﬀeners are tightened to a torque of approximately 60 Nm and
90 Nm respectively.
Mechanical excitation
The rotor can be excited using a mechanical shaker 16 of the type MB Electronics PM 50.
The mechanical shaker is hung from a crane and connected to the shaft via a stinger, force
transducer, and excitation bearing 9 . The crane can be moved such that excitation can
be applied from diﬀerent directions. The excitation bearing is of the type SKF 6008-2Z
rated for max 11000 rpm.
Motor drive system
The shaft is driven by the drive motor 2 controlled by a VLT 1 and connected via a
belt, layshaft, and a ﬂexible coupling 12 serving to decouple the drive motor dynamics
from the rotor. The drive motor is a 5.5 kW AC motor from RS PRO. The frequency
converter (VLT) is a Danfoss VLT Micro Drive FC51 with a power supply of 5.5 kW.
A braking resistor is connected to the frequency converter to get rid of the accumulated
energy when breaking. The brake resistor is a 80 Ωresistor of the type Danfoss MCE 101
with a maximum braking power of 2.2 kW.
Active magnetic bearing
The active magnetic bearing (AMB) 5 can be used to excite the system, but has not
been utilized during this project.
Active tilting pad Journal Bearing (ATPJB)
The ATPJB 7 is the core of the test rig and is the crucial component within this thesis,
where it is used to perform non-invasive rotor testing. The main properties of the ATPJB
are given in Table 3.2. A picture of the ATPJB and its internal components are shown
in Figure 3.2 and 3.3 with corresponding component indices explained in Table 3.3.

==PAGE:61==

The bearing has 4 pads ζ made
of brass that are able to tilt on
steel pivots θ . The tilting pads
are placed symmetrically around
the bearing separated by 90 de-
grees between each pivot in a
load-between-pad conﬁguration,
meaning that the static load of
the rotor is placed between the
two lower pads. The pads have
a pad oﬀset of 0.5 meaning that
the pivot is placed centrally on
the pad. This has the eﬀect of
lowering the load-carrying capac-
ity but enables the rotor to run
identically in clockwise and coun-
terclockwise directions. By using
tilting pads the equilibrium posi-
tion can change during operation
which increases the stability by
reducing cross-coupled forces. As mentioned the test rig had experienced a destructive
breakdown which burned and initiated cracks in the pads, see Figure 3.3a. Therefore
new pads have been manufactured by following an updated machine drawing shown in
Appendix F.1. The measured ﬁnal dimensions of the pads are also listed in Appendix
F.2. The old and new pads are shown in Figure 3.3.
The ATPJB has the capability of both passive hydrodynamic and active hydrostatic lubri-
cation, denoted passive and controllable lubrication in the upcoming chapters respectively.
Passive hydrodynamic lubrication is obtained by low-pressure (LP) oil entering the bear-
ing at the top of the housing β . The oil is then distributed by internal passageways and
sprayed onto the shaft by four nozzle heads placed orthogonally inside the bearing ε . The
50
==PAGE:62==
3.1. ROTOR-BEARING-FOUNDATION TEST RIG
nozzle heads have three small injection nozzle bolts angled by 45 ◦between each other to
distribute the oil well. During the disassembly of the test rig, it was found that several
of the low-pressure injection nozzles were completely clogged by debris. This discovery
backs up a conclusion from Wagner [9] who tested and saw that the frequency response
was dependent on the LP supply pressure and suggested the cause to be starvation of
the pads (pads not fully ﬂooded). It was therefore decided to produce new nozzle bolts
changing the inner diameter from 0.7 mm to 1.5 mm. The machine drawing of the new
nozzle bolts can be found in Appendix F.3.
Active hydrostatic lubrication is obtained by high-pressure (HP) oil, injected via two
externally mounted servo valves α and 6 . The control ports of the servo valves are con-
nected to two pads placed opposite each other. The high-pressure oil is injected through
an injection oriﬁce/nozzle η placed in the center of the pads. It should be mentioned
that in previous projects the injection oriﬁce has been modeled as a cylindrical tube of
length l0 = 21 mm and diameter d0 = 3.3 mm which is not the case. An interchangeable
oriﬁce κ is screwed inside the nozzle reducing the diameter to dn = 0.5 mm across a small
length of approximately ln = 0.5 mm. These oriﬁces have previously been discussed and
tested by Christensen [4]. To reduce confusion in future projects a drawing of the pad
oriﬁce assembly is shown in Appendix F.4.
Servo valves
Figure
3.4:
Illustration
of
MOOG 760 series servovalve,
from [46].
The high-pressure hydrostatic lubrication is controlled
using two servo valves α and 6 of type MOOG E760-
912. The servo valves are both underlapped 4-way 2-
stage spool valves.
The two stages consist of a pilot
and an output stage. The pilot stage is electronically
controlled by sending a control current to a dry torque
motor causing a deﬂection of the ﬂapper. Deﬂection of
the ﬂapper initiates the output stage by guiding oil to
the spool-ends, displacing the spool. This (older) type
of servo valve does not have internal electronics and the
valves are therefore controlled by sending a voltage signal
to an external servo ampliﬁer converting the signal to a
control current. And consequently, no feedback signal
of the spool position is available. An illustration of the
servo valves is shown in Figure 3.4. Here the 4-ways are
denoted by the supply port P, the return port T, and
the two control ports A and B. The control ports A, B are connected to a set of opposite
pads. When the spool is centered, the supply and return P and T ports are almost closed
allowing a small leakage ﬂow due to the underlapped design. And when the spool is
displaced to either side, oil is led through the supply port P to one of the control ports,
while the opposite control port is connected to the return port T allowing a back-ﬂow.
3.1.2
Instrumentation
The test rig is equipped with numerous sensors for the purpose of monitoring the running
conditions of the test rig. The most essential instrumentation on the test rig is indicated
by the black letter indices in Figure 3.1 explained and in Table 3.1.
51
==PAGE:63==
3.1. ROTOR-BEARING-FOUNDATION TEST RIG
Displacement sensors
10 displacement/proximity sensors are used to measure the vibrations of the rotor shaft.
Three diﬀerent sensors are used. Sensors 1 and 2 C are inductive sensors of the type
Pulsotronic KJ4-M12MN50-ANU. Sensors 3 and 4 H are Vibrometer type 102 eddy
current displacement sensors with external IQS 603 demodulator conditioners. Sensors
5-10 E F G are all inductive sensors of type Pulsotronic KJ2-M8MB4-ANU. The Pul-
sotronic sensors conveniently have in-build electronics, however, these electronics produce
a variable electric noise and small time delays which signiﬁcantly increase the noise char-
acteristics compared to the Vibrometer sensors.
Moreover, no analog low-pass ﬁlters are installed to remove aliasing eﬀects. Hence it is
crucial to analyze the possibility of aliased frequency components from unknown high-
frequency vibrations when performing analysis using these sensors.
The position sensors are mounted in vertical-horizontal pairs where vertical and horizontal
sensors are even and odd-numbered respectively. The sensors are distributed from the
overhung to the drive end of the shaft by increased numbering. The speciﬁc positions of
the sensors are indicated in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: Positions of the displacement sensors
and shaker excitation
.

found compared to old calibrations [8]. The calibration procedure is summarized in Ap-
pendix G. The calibration yields a linear relationship between the output voltage V (x)
and displacement x.
V (x) = V0 + Sx
(3.1)
Where V0 and S are the oﬀset voltage and sensor sensitivity respectively. The voltage

should be positioned such that they measure a voltage around V ∗.

3.2. HYDRAULIC SYSTEM
Force transducer
A force transducer of type B&K 8230-001 (Sens. 22 mV/N) is mounted in between the
stinger and excitation bearing to measure the applied force. The speciﬁc position of the
excitation point is given in Figure 3.5. The force signal is ampliﬁed by a Brüel & Kjær
precision conditioning ampliﬁer of the type 2650. The ampliﬁer has an in-build analog
low-pass ﬁlter set to 1kHz to remove aliasing eﬀects considering the used sampling rate
of fs = 3 kHz.
Rotary encoder
An incremental quadrature encoder D of the type RS PRO 2061279 is used to measure
the speed and angle of the rotor shaft. The encoder has a maximum operational speed
of 12000 rpm and provides 1024 lpr (lines per revolution). An incremental quadrature
encoder outputs 2 digital signals phase-shifted by 90◦and by triggering on both rising
and falling edges the resolution can be quadrupled (4-fold subdivision). Hence the angle
resolution becomes 360 dpr/(4 · 1024 lpr) = 0.0879 dpl (degrees per line).
Pressure, Flow and Temperature monitoring
The dynamics of the rotor system heavily rely on the state of the oil, hence many sensors
are dedicated to monitoring the hydraulic system. A digital ﬂowmeter A is used to
monitor the HP line, while a digital pressure probe B monitors the LP line. Two Analog
manometers are also placed on HP and LP lines (see ﬁgure 3.6) and used to adjust the
pressures to a desired level. The viscosity of the oil is dependent on the temperature. As
tested by Jensen [7], who states that the oil temperate normally varies within [35, 65]◦C
resulting in a viscosity change of [9.1, 26.0]mm2/s. Hence it is crucial to make sure that a
steady state temperature is reached before performing any tests to avoid capturing non-
stationary data breaking OMA and EMA assumptions. Digital temperature probes are
therefore placed at the HP and LP B inlets, on the return line, and in the oil tank.
A controllable heater is also installed inside the oil tank to speed up the temperature
transient.
3.2
Hydraulic System
The ATPJB is equipped with a hydraulic system supplying low-pressure (LP) oil for
passive hydrodynamic lubrication and high-pressure (HP) oil for active hydrostatic lubri-
cation. The used oil is a high-performance anti-wear hydraulic Mobil DTE 22 Ultra oil
with ISO viscosity grade VG22. A picture of the hydraulic system is shown in Figure
3.6, and a list of the main components are described in Table 3.5. An associated circuit
diagram is also shown in Figure 3.7.
Two main pressure circuits (low and high) both pull oil from the common reservoir 13 .
First, consider the (HP) line used to provide oil directly through the middle of the pads
for hydrostatic lubrication. A constant speed electric motor 6 is connected to a variable
displacement pump 5 with pressure control. The pump pressure i.e. the HP supply
pressure ps,HP is controlled via the pressure control knob 4 in the range [12 −100] bar,
limited to 100 bar due to sealing issues [7]. A pressure relief valve 12 is installed to
protect the up-line components and avoid oil bursts. An analog manometer 9 is placed
before the pressure relief valve to monitor the high-pressure oil. A medium-pressure inline
oil ﬁlter 11 of type PI 3605-11 is used to ﬁlter the oil before it enters the servo valves. As
previously mentioned the servo spool position is controlled via an electrical signal and will
dictate the HP-oil ﬂow direction. The ﬂow directions are dependent on the voltage signal

Now considering the LP line used for passive hydrodynamic lubrication.
A constant
speed motor 8 drives the LP gear pump 3 and return pump 10 . The delivery ﬂow is
controlled using a restrictor valve 15 that connects the return pump to the LP line. The
delivery pressure is typically kept within 0.6-1 bar resulting in a ﬂow of approximately
1 L/min. After having changed the LP nozzles increasing the LP pressure above 1 bar
results in large oil leakage from the bearing. The LP delivery line is equipped with an
analog manometer 1 and a low-pressure inline oil ﬁlter 2 of the Type Fleetguard -
hf6500. The LP oil enters the bearing at the top where it is distributed through four
nozzles. All excess oil from the bearing is led back to the reservoir by the return pump.
The oil passes through a cooler 7 driven by the constant speed (HP) motor.
The breakdown of the test rig has likely produced a signiﬁcant amount of metal debris
that could reduce the lifetime of many components, and the discovery of clogged nozzle
bolts is a clear indicator of contaminated oil. Therefore it is decided to change all ﬁlters.
The medium-pressure ﬁlter 11 has an interchangeable 10 µm inner ﬁlter element, of type
PI 1105 MIC 10 which has been changed. The low-pressure ﬁlter 2 is a spin-on ﬁlter of
Type Fleetguard - hf6500. And the coarse oil ﬁlter 14 within the oil tank has also had
its ﬁlter element replaced, of type Filtrec RMR443E20B.

the data processing has also been semi-automated utilizing the ASAM XIL standard [47]
which is an API standard for communication between test automation tools and test
benches. Automation is especially convenient since the full test procedure involves nu-
merous steps due to the pre-test run-out compensation, described in the following section.
3.4
Run-out compensation
Since the displacement sensors are measuring on a rotating target the raw signals will
be inﬂuenced by both mechanical and electrical run-out noise. Mechanical run-out is
caused by surface asperities and non-circularities of the shaft which are both common
due to machining tolerances. Electrical run-out is caused by variations in the electrical
and magnetic properties of the shaft material [48], which disturbs the sensor sensitivity.
Run-out noise is assumed to correlate perfectly with the angular position of the shaft, and
hence a run-out compensation strives to remove all variations that are correlated with the
shaft angle. Other signal variations correlated to the shaft angle can and will therefore also
be removed. This includes measured vibrations from shaft unbalances and eccentricities.
Eccentric movement of the shaft can also lead to periodic variations measured by the force
transducers which can also be removed.
In practice, the run-out compensation is performed by measuring the shaft angular posi-
tion using the rotary quadrature encoder at a high sampling rate (16 kHz) over 20 seconds.
The measured displacement and force signals are averaged across shaft angles and saved
56
==PAGE:68==
3.4. RUN-OUT COMPENSATION
as a reference in look-up tables. The compensation is then completed by subtracting
the reference from the recorded data. It is important to build the run-out reference just
prior to any experimental test since the temperature variations cause the shaft to expand
both axially and radially which easily alters the sensor measuring position. Moreover,
the run-out reference can be made in two diﬀerent ways; at the current testing speed
or at a low speed, denoted at-speed and low-speed run-out respectively. Both methods
will remove the mechanical and electrical run-out although the low-speed run-out is likely
to provide a better angle resolution. However, since unbalance and other synchronous
vibrations are proportional to the rotational speed, the low-speed run-out will not remove
these harmonics but the at-speed run-out will.
Examples of measured and averaged run-out data at 500 rpm are shown in Figure 3.9. As
previously mentioned the vibrometer sensors (3 and 4) have superior noise characteristics
compared to the remaining Pulsotronic sensors, as also clearly seen in the shown run-out
data. Moreover, it is observed that sensors 4 and 6 exhibit a rough sinusoidal pattern,
suggesting a potential eccentric movement or oval shape of the shaft. In contrast, sensor
2 shows a relatively straight proﬁle with only minor variations attributed to electrical and
mechanical run-out. During the assembly of the test rig, the surface targeted by sensors
2 (and 1) was balanced by adjusting the disc positions using an indicator, which could
explain the smoother response of sensor 2.
(a) Sensor 2
(b) Sensor 4
(c) Sensor 6
Figure 3.9: Measured and averaged run-out data at 500 rpm of 168 revolutions.
By comparing the run-out measurements to previous builds of the test rig, a notable
decrease in the peak-to-peak variation of the sensor readings across angles is observed.
The maximum peak-to-peak variation of the current test rig at 500 rpm is approximately
40 µm, whereas in previous years (Jensen [7]), it was found to be 150 µm. This suggests
that the replacement of pads and the repolishing of the shaft have signiﬁcantly enhanced
the overall operating conditions of the ATPJB system.
Since the 10 sensors are placed in vertical and horizontal pairs, their run-out measure-
ments should ideally be equal when one is shifted by 90 deg. Therefore, a representative
comparison of the averaged run-out of sensors 3 and 4 is made by shifting the sensor 4
signal by 90 deg, as shown in Figure 3.10a. The signals are almost identical with minor
deviations, likely caused by a misalignment of the sensor pedestal. Finally, a comparison
between a compensated and uncompensated signal is shown in Figure 3.10b during a
bi-directional chirp excitation. It is evident that the peak-to-peak variations are reduced,
57
==PAGE:69==
3.4. RUN-OUT COMPENSATION
resulting in a much cleaner signal that exempliﬁes a chirp response.

Chapter 4
Analysis and results of non-invasive
rotordynamic testing
This Chapter presents the main analysis of performing non-invasive rotordynamic test-
ing. First, the rotor-bearing-foundation test rig will be experimentally assessed to gain an
overview of its dynamics after it has been reassembled. Then an investigation of the con-
trollable lubrication will be made and the perturbation strategy will be discussed. Lastly,
non-invasive ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation is combined with OMA to obtain modal estimates
at 1000 and 7000 rpm, and the accuracy of the estimates will be discussed in relation to
EMA results.
4.1
Investigation of unbalance response and natu-
rally induced vibrations
An overall assessment of the unbalance response of the ATPJB test rig under normal
operation conditions (only passive hydrodynamic lubrication) is obtained by conducting a
run-up and coast-down experiment. The shaft rotational speed Ωis swept linearly within
Ω∈[1, 7] krpm during 10 minutes by sending an appropriate signal to the VLC. The
recorded data is run-out compensated using a run-out reference created at 500 rpm, hence
only removing the run-out noise. Figure 4.1 shows the resulting run-up spectrograms of
sensors 3 and 4 (horizontal and vertical) plotted against the measured rotational speed.
The synchronous ﬁrst-order 1Ωvibrations are naturally present throughout the full range
with seemingly constant amplitude. Higher orders 2Ω,3Ω. . . are also present with de-
creasing amplitude. More interesting is the increased vibration amplitudes at 50 and 70
Hz starting around 5 krpm. These ampliﬁcations are more or less constant in the spec-
trogram, suggesting a potential correlation with modes of the ATPJB system. Around
70 Hz 0.8Ωand 0.6Ωsub-synchronous vibrations are also excited at 5.5 and 6.8 krpm
respectively.
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments

4.1. INVESTIGATION OF UNBALANCE RESPONSE AND NATURALLY INDUCED
VIBRATIONS
This happens when the 1Ωvibrations coincide with 60 Hz mode as visible in Figure 4.1a
where a valley is observed in the spectrogram. Since displacement sensors are mounted on
the foundation and knowing that the 60 Hz component is most likely a foundation mode
from EMA analysis of the foundation in Appendix C, the amplitude decrease is likely
caused by a sudden in-phase movement of the foundation and shaft and not necessarily a
decrease in rotor displacement.
Natural excitation
To further analyze the apparent excitation of the large ampliﬁcations around 50 and 70 Hz
four separate tests have been made at constant speeds 1, 3, 5, and 7 krpm, with at-speed
run-out compensation to mitigate synchronous vibrations from the signals. Figure 4.3
shows histograms, normal-probability plots, and the three largest singular values of the
SD matrix for the four measurements.
From the signal histograms and normal probability plots it is clearly noticed that the
displacement of the rotor becomes larger as the speed increases and that the displace-
ment also becomes increasingly normally distributed. Note that the unbalance has been
removed (as good as possible) from the signal, hence, the observed displacement is a com-
bination of measurement noise and other vibrations not correlated to the rotational speed.
From the SVD plot of the SD matrix, it is observed that at low speeds, the singular value
curves do not show signiﬁcant diﬀerences and are likely representative of measurement
noise. But when the speed is increased to 5 and 7 krpm two singular value curves rise
above the noise ﬂoor showing signs of system dynamics at the same positions as observed
in the run-up tests around 50 and 70 Hz. Hence there is a strong indication of a natural
excitation source from the ATPJB system, which amplitude increases with the rotational
speed, and provides a normally distributed response of the rotor, with maximum ampli-
tude variations of approximately ±10µm being 12% of the assembled radial clearance.
The exact cause of this excitation is unknown, although various potential sources are
listed below.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.3: Histogram and normal probability plot of sensor 3 (horizontal) and the three
largest singular values of the spectral matrix in (a) and (b) respectively.
61
==PAGE:73==
4.2. VARIATION OF THE HYDRODYNAMIC SUPPLY PRESSURE
• Unbalance and other synchronous vibrations, are in their nature harmonic excita-
tions and generally undesirable regarding OMA. However, while its harmonics are
removed by the at-speed run-out compensation the induced vibrations may leak
into other frequencies. From the spectrograms in Figure 4.1 it is noticed that the
modes at 50 and 70 Hz are excited after the crossing of the 1Ωfrequency, supporting
the possibility of an unbalance excitation. In [16] Carden also shows the possibil-
ity of using unbalance vibrations during run-up together with an OMA method to
estimate modal parameters, thereby supporting this idea.
• Marginal stability. With increasing speed, the system naturally approaches its sta-
bility limit, and above a certain speed, the response is likely to reach a marginal
stability state. In this state, the rotor position will randomly change between equi-
librium positions, creating a sort of random excitation.
• Turbulent ﬂuid. The ﬂow between pads and journal is with high certainty laminar
as explained by Jensen [7], however inside the bearing there are many cavities and
sharp edges which easily make the ﬂuid turbulent around other parts of the rotor.
Hence exciting the rotor with random ﬂuid forces.
• Seal forces and two-phase ﬂows. When operating the bearing at high speeds a large
and sometimes aggressive leakage ﬂow is observed through the mechanical seals of
the bearing. The oil is clearly squeezed out of the bearing, while the exiting oil is
foamy. Air bubbles in the ﬂuid ﬁlm can easily alter the ﬂuid characteristics, possibly
creating a turbulent condition between pads and bearing, while forces occurring from
the seal are also known to provide possible excitation of the rotor.
4.2
Variation of the hydrodynamic supply pressure
Wagner [9] concluded that the bearing suﬀocated from oil starvation at rotational speeds
above 3 krpm. For this reason, the hole diameter of the low-pressure (LP) injection nozzles
has been increased from 0.7 mm to 1.5 mm. To analyze the eﬀect of the new injection
nozzles, the hydrodynamic (LP) supply pressure is varied within ps,LP ∈[0.6, 1.25] bar
at a rotational speed of 3 krpm, where Wagner saw indications of starvation. FRFs are
calculated by the H1 estimator using a bidirectional chirp excitation [20, 250] Hz with
a period of 20 sec in the vertical direction with at-speed run-out compensation, and a
sampling rate of fs = 3000 Hz. Figure 4.4 shows representative FRFs using the vertical
sensor 4.
In areas with good coherence, no clear deviation is seen between the diﬀerent FRFs.
Whereas in regions with poor coherence e.g.
around 25Hz one can see diﬀerences in
the FRFs, however, this cannot be deemed signiﬁcant due to the decreased signal quality.
Hence it is concluded that the new injection nozzles have removed the starvation problems.
FRFs from other sensors show very similar results and are therefore not shown.
During the test, it was discovered that raising the hydraulic supply pressure, ps,LP, above
1.25 bar resulted in excessive oil leakage from the bearing. This indicates potential over-
saturation of the bearing with oil, exceeding the capacity of the return pump, and suggests
that the hole diameter of the new injection bolts may be unnecessarily large. Therefore,
it is recommended to either modify the injection bolts or enhance the return-pump line,
such as by increasing the pipe diameter to reduce ﬂow resistance. For further analysis,
62
==PAGE:74==

the hydrodynamic supply pressure is kept at ps,LP = 0.6 bar to minimize leakage.

4.4
Rotor equilibrium position and servo valve input
This section will experimentally describe the relation between servo valve input and the
rotor equilibrium position. Figure 4.6 shows the expected ﬂuid ﬂow direction as a function
of servo valve (SV) input changes.
Figure 4.6: Schematic in-
dicating ﬂuid ﬂow direc-
tions
dependent
on
the
servo valve inputs SV1 and
SV2.
Measurements are performed by varying the input voltage of
servo valves 1 or 2 (SV1, SV2) within [1, 4] V while keeping
one input constant in steps of SVi = {1, 2.5, 4} V. In this
way, the (SV1, SV2) domain is discretized to obtain a global
picture of the possible rotor movement. The rotor position is
obtained by the average position over 5 s.2 The rotor position
is obtained using the vibrometer sensors 3 and 4 resembling
the Y and Z direction inside the bearing respectively and
denoted dY,Z. Measurements are made with supply pressures
ps,HP = {12, 15, 20, 60, 100} at 1000 rpm and with ps,HP =
{20, 60, 100} at 5000 rpm. All data have been oﬀset with
respect to the passive equilibrium such that (0,0) corresponds
to the passive equilibrium measured before the tests.
Before starting the discussion of results an assumption is
made about the centered spool (CS) position of the servo
valves.
• It is assumed that the spool is centered when the eﬀect of changing the servo valve
input is maximized with respect to rotor movement.
This assumption is made on the basis that when the spools are centered an equal leakage
ﬂow will exist through both valve ports, and that small changes from this spool position
will have the maximum eﬀect on the ﬂow through the valve ports and hence also the
rotor position. The input voltage that positions the spools in their center is called the CS
input.
The resulting rotor equilibrium positions obtained by varying the servo valve inputs are
shown in a (Y, Z) map in Figure 4.7.
Analyzing Figure 4.7, it is evident that a simultaneous high and low servo valve input
positions the rotor to the right and left outer positions, respectively, as expected from
Figure 4.6. And opposite input magnitudes (low and high) of the servo valves position
the rotor at the maximum and minimum vertical positions.

==PAGE:77==
4.4. ROTOR EQUILIBRIUM POSITION AND SERVO VALVE INPUT
From Figure 4.7a for 1000 rpm it is observed that the maximum possible rotor position
tracks out rectangles in the (Y, Z) plane with increasing area proportional to the supply
pressure ps,HP. This indicates that the servo valves can control two orthogonal directions
in the (Y, Z) plane and that their eﬀect on the rotor is decoupled. The increased size
of the region is simply explained by the increased ﬂuid forces available for higher supply
pressures. It is also noticed that for decreasing supply pressure the lowest possible Z-
position of the rotor converges towards the passive equilibrium. Hence for small ps,HP one
requires a large (from spool center) servo valve input to reach the passive equilibrium.
It is also noted that measurements with SV = 2.5 V are not necessarily centered in the
rectangle, indicating that 2.5 V is not the input corresponding to the CS input.
From Figure 4.7b for 5000 rpm the maximum possible rotor position no longer tracks out
perfect rectangles, but lines have become curved and more variation is found between
measurement points of equal input values.
This indicates that the servo valve forces
have become slightly coupled. The non-coinciding corner points are likely to arise from
temperature variations that are easily obtained at high speeds and have a signiﬁcant eﬀect
as shown in section 4.3.
The possible displacement range in the Z-direction for ps,HP = 100 bar and 1000 rpm is
approximately within dz ∈[−20, 60]µm hence it is possible to displace the rotor further
above than below the passive equilibrium. This is possibly explained by considering that
a speed of 1000 rpm puts the rotor relatively low in the bearing, hence the minimum
ﬂuid ﬁlm thickness is small and it is hard to displace the rotor any further in the negative
Z-direction. Whereas at 5000 rpm, the vertical displacement range is dz ∈[−35, 22]µm for
ps,HP = 100 bar. At this speed, the rotor has risen towards the bearing center. Hence the
ﬂuid ﬁlm thickness has become large and it is now possible to signiﬁcantly displace the
rotor in the negative Z-direction. However this can only be achieved for large pressures,
and for ps,HP = 20 bar it is observed that it is impossible to move the rotor back to its
passive equilibrium, see Figure 4.7b pink lines.
The equilibrium position can also be plotted in 3D plots for separate Y and Z directions
as a function of the input signals SV1 and SV2, as shown in Figure 4.8 for both 1000
and 5000 rpm. It is noticed that by varying one servo input the rotor response resembles
the characteristic of a logistic curve. Hence to obtain a full surface representation of
the rotor displacement as a function of the servo valve input a logistic objective function
dj : R2 →R for j = {Y, Z} is ﬁtted for both Y and Z directions for every pressure level.
The ﬁt is performed using the Levenberg-Marquardt method in MATLAB. The objective
function is of the type.
dj(SV1, SV2) =
L1,j
1 + e−k1,j(SV1−xo1,j) +
L2,j
1 + e−k2,j(SV2−xo2,j) + fo,j for j = {Y, Z}
(4.1)
Hence dj(SV1, SV2) describes the rotor displacement in the j = {Y, Z} direction as a
function of the two servo valve inputs. Li,j is the supremum, xoi,j is the function midpoint
(point of largest gradient), ki,j is the logistic growth rate, all with respect to the i = 1, 2
servo valve inputs, while fo,j deﬁnes the function displacement oﬀset.

(d) Z-direction 5000 rpm

It is observed that the function ﬁt seems to be quite good for the 1000 rpm measurements,

this trend is deemed insigniﬁcant and the mean val-

SV1,CS = xo1 = 2.6 V and SV2,CS = xo1 = 2.0 V. A
similar trend is found for the CS input at 5000 rpm
in Figure 4.9b, although a slightly steeper trend for
xo1 is observed.
The CS input for servo valve 1 can be compared to
ﬂow experiments conducted by Christensen in [4],
who ﬁnds the corresponding average value for servo
valve 1 to be 2.6 V, matching perfectly. Sadly, no ex-
periments were conducted using servo valve 2, hence
no comparison can be made. A reason why the CS
input is diﬀerent for the two servo valves is not eas-
ily given. It is however, concluded that the match-
ing CS inputs of servo valve 1 in this experiment
and Christensen’s ﬂow experiment are enough to support the hypothesis about the spool
center, and it is therefore assumed valid throughout the rest of this thesis.
4.5
EMA analysis of the ATPJB system
This section will present FRFs and modal parameters of the ATPJB system obtained
using shaker excitation.
All EMA tests within this section are performed using shaker excitation through the
excitation bearing. The excitation signal is a bidirectional-Chirp [20, 250]Hz with a period
of 20 s. Tests are acquired during 240 sec with a sampling rate of 3000Hz and at-speed
run-out compensation. FRFs are calculated using the H1 estimator with 175 averages
giving a frequency resolution of fres = 0.7Hz.
Only passive hydrodynamic injection is used with a supply pressure of ps,LP = 0.6bar.
4.5.1
EMA at 1000 rpm
To obtain benchmark modal parameters of the passive ATPJB system at 1000 rpm EMA
tests are run using shaker excitation in horizontal, vertical, and 45-degree directions. A
comparison of the resulting FRFs is shown for sensors 3 and 4 in Figure 4.10.
68
==PAGE:80==
4.5. EMA ANALYSIS OF THE ATPJB SYSTEM
(a) Horizontal Y-direction, sensor 3
(b) Vertical Z-direction, sensor 4
Figure 4.10: FRF comparison for horizontal, vertical, and 45-degree excitation.
By inspection of the FRFs, it becomes clear that the cross-coupling eﬀect of the tilting
pad journal bearing is small. Horizontal sensor 3 shows minor ampliﬁcations during the
vertical test, and vice versa, vertical sensor 4 shows small ampliﬁcations during horizontal
excitation.
The coherence also indicates that excitation orthogonal to the measuring
direction provides a decreased signal quality. The 45-degree excitation has reasonably
good coherence for both horizontal and vertical sensors whereas the amplitude is higher
in the horizontal direction (sensor 3). It is also noted that the large ampliﬁcation around
95 Hz appears to include two resonance peaks for horizontal and 45-degree excitation
but only one for vertical excitation. This might be caused by two close and damped
modes that are highly coupled. Such modes are likely related to rotor movement which is
expected to be less damped in the horizontal direction for general TPJBs, and therefore
better excited by the horizontal and diagonal excitation. Note also that only the vertical
sensor 4 during diagonal and vertical excitation shows a low-frequency ampliﬁcation at
27 Hz. This resonance is expected to be the ﬁrst bending mode of the foundation hence
horizontal excitation is not able to excite this mode.
FRFs from other sensors are similar and therefore not shown. However, their coherence
is signiﬁcantly reduced due to the use of the Pulsotronic sensors with more measurement
noise.
To obtain estimates of the modal parameters, the p-LSCF EMA routine is used including
measurements from all 10 sensors. The coherence is used as a weighting to reduce the
contribution from frequency bands of poor signal quality. An example of a stabilization
diagram and an LSFD ﬁt is shown in Figure 4.11 for the 45-degree excitation.
From the stabilization diagram Figure 4.11a it is seen that approximately 9 modes are
identiﬁed as stable using a model order above 200. Interesting are the modes around 100
Hz, where it is seen that two close modes are identiﬁed but only at a high model order.
Since the testing is SIMO coupled modes are hard to decouple, hence errors are expected
for coupled modes. Figure 4.11b shows the LSFD ﬁt using the 9 identiﬁed stable poles.
Apart from a lacking ampliﬁcation at 91Hz in the FRF of the highest amplitude, the ﬁt
is satisfactory.
Global modal parameters are obtained by averaging stabilized poles across appropriate
model orders, and the resulting modal parameters are summarized for all three tests

Some modes are only captured by speciﬁc excitation directions as expected due to the
modal forms (see also next section 4.5.1). Signiﬁcant variations in modal parameters for
modes 5 and 6 are observed across diﬀerent excitation directions, likely due to shaker in-
terference and high modal coupling. Mounting the shaker in a non-invasive conﬁguration,
particularly for the vertical setup, was found challenging due to the risk of accidentally
displacing the shaft via the stinger connection. It is expected that such external forcing
might disturb the stiﬀness, mass, and damping of the rotor system, signiﬁcantly disturbing
the estimated modal parameters. The shaker’s impact on the foundation is anticipated
to be less pronounced compared to the rotor modes, further supporting the notion that
modes 5 and 6 are predominantly associated with the rotor. A noteworthy high stan-
dard deviation is also found for the damping ratio of the 5th mode using horizontal and
45-degree excitation. From further analysis, two diﬀerent damping ratios seem to be pre-
dicted from this mode of approximately 1% and 10%, indicating that two modes might
70
==PAGE:82==
4.5. EMA ANALYSIS OF THE ATPJB SYSTEM
exist in this region although overﬁtting is more likely the cause. The average modal esti-
mates across the diﬀerent excitation directions give a sense of modal parameter accuracy
and might eliminate some of the shaker bias.
EMA ATPJB modeshapes
Modeshapes predicted from the p-LSCF algorithm are generally complex and hence able
to capture the expected complexity of the orbiting rotor modes.
However, by SIMO
testing the modeshapes can only be built under the assumption of self-adjointness which
is generally not the case for rotor systems that are generally non-self-adjoint. Hence the
shown modeshapes are expected to be biased.
Since the displacement sensors are mounted on top of the foundation they will be attached
in a moving reference frame seen from the rotor. Meaning that without knowledge of the
foundation vibrations, foundation movement will be impossible to decouple from rotor
movement. This is a huge downside considering the complete documentation of the system
modes, however, with foregoing knowledge from mathematical models and or other modal
tests it is often possible to still gain valuable information from such modes. A positive
feature of mounting the sensors on the ﬂexible foundation is that both rotor modes and
foundation modes can be observed in the sensor signal without having a large rotor-
foundation coupling. This can be of great value in SHM and CM-related problems, where
it is often advantageous to observe many modes with a minimum amount of sensors.
Moreover, in SHM and CM settings prior knowledge and detailed documentation of the
expected system modes are common and can be used in modal expansion techniques and
elevate the understanding of the captured modes.
First to analyze the quality and similarity of the modeshapes between the diﬀerent exci-
tation directions the AutoMACX and MACX are plotted for all excitation directions and
modes respectively in Figure 4.12. From Figures 4.12 (a), (b), and (c) it is observed that
signiﬁcant spatial aliasing exists since diﬀerent modes compare well to each other. This
is expected due to the unseparated rotor and foundation modes. Especially mode 8 and 9
are similar across all excitation directions. Analyzing the similarity between modes across
the excitation directions in Figure 4.12d good agreement between modes is found (when
they are found). Although modes 5 and 8 show diﬀerent predictions when excited from
diﬀerent directions

Figure 4.12: AutoMACX for horizontal, vertical, and 45-deg tests in (a), (b), and (c).
And Comparison between horizontal, vertical, and 45-deg modes in (d).
To obtain a deeper understanding of the predicted modeshapes they are plotted together
with foundation modes of similar frequency. The foundation modes are obtained from
the EMA analysis performed on the foundation before the shaft was mounted, hence it
is expected that alike modes will diﬀer in frequency due to the added mass and possible
stiﬀness of the rotor.3 The rotor modes are mainly plotted using the 45-deg excitation,
although modes 2 and 5 are plotted using vertical and horizontal excitation data respec-
tively due to missing estimates and MAC similarity of the mode respectively.
To further assist the assessment of modes they are compared to modes from the rotor-
bearing-foundation model created by Wagner in [9].4
When interpreting the modes the mode number refers to the "rotor mode" i.e. a mode
predicted by measuring on the rotor, but not necessarily a rotor mode.
Also, claims
regarding foundation modes are described in Appendix C.3.
Mode 1 (27Hz): This mode shows pure vertical movement, indicating that orbiting
motion of the shaft is limited. Moreover, horizontal excitation is not able to excite this
mode. Altogether this mode is with high certainty the ﬁrst bending mode of the founda-
tion.
Mode 2 (33Hz): The mode is predicted with very small participation (Table 4.1) and
hence its modeshape is likely highly erroneous. However, by comparison to the model by
3See analysis in Appendix C.3 for further information
4Wagner presents modal parameters for 2000 rpm (page 57) and hence rotor modes aﬀected by gyro-
scopic eﬀects are expected to diﬀer slightly in frequency.

==PAGE:84==
4.5. EMA ANALYSIS OF THE ATPJB SYSTEM
Wagner, the ﬁrst torsional foundation mode is found at this frequency as also indicated
by the EMA foundation mode.
Mode 3 (60Hz): Mainly horizontal movement is observed and the mode is likely related
to the second torsional mode of the foundation. Also backed up by the model.
Mode 4 (69Hz): This mode exhibits pure in-phase movement, mainly in the vertical
direction with limited orbiting. This mode is likely related to the second bending mode
of the foundation. Also backed up by the model.
Mode 5 (92Hz): This mode shows a signiﬁcant orbiting nature of the shaft and is likely
related to the ﬁrst bending mode of the of rotor, mainly vertical. The model predicts
a similar rotor bending mode but in the horizontal direction. The foundation mode at
94Hz is not considered a bending mode of the foundation but is likely related to other
components, which further backs up the existence of a rotor bending mode.
Mode 6 (98Hz): This mode is likely related to the ﬁrst bending mode of the rotor,
mainly horizontal. The model predicts a rotor bending mode but in the vertical direction.
Mode 7 (118Hz): This mode is very similar to mode 3 while the shown foundation
mode is not considered a transverse mode of the foundation. Hence this mode might
appear from other vibrating components of the foundation.
Mode 8 (126Hz): This mode is likely related to the third torsional foundation mode
also backed up by the model. The signiﬁcant orbiting motion of the mode may not be
trustworthy also considering the poor similarity between all modes in the MACX plot
(Figure 4.12d).
Mode 9 (140Hz): Signiﬁcant horizontal movement is found with minimal orbiting,
hence the mode is likely related to the third foundation bending mode. This is further
supported by the inability of horizontal excitation to excite this mode.
Mode 10 (182Hz): This mode looks very similar to modes 3 and 7 and is likely related
to the fourth torsional mode of the foundation, also backed up by the model.
