4.6. PASSIVE HYDRODYNAMIC VS CONTROLLABLE HYDROSTATIC LUBRICATION
INCLUDING TEMPERATURE VARIATIONS
It is generally observed that the rotor modes at 90-100 Hz are initially signiﬁcant at 1000
rpm. However, as the speed increases they become damped and their resonance peaks
vanish from the response. Above 5.5 krpm two signiﬁcant frequency components at 60
and 70 Hz increase signiﬁcantly in amplitude while their damping also seems to decrease.
These modes are likely related to the second torsional foundation mode (∼60 Hz) and
the backward rotor mode that has changed its frequency from 92 Hz to 73 Hz due to
gyroscopic eﬀects.
Note also the minor response peaks that stay more or less constant through the angular
velocities at approximately 126 and 140 Hz. These peaks are likely associated with the
third torsional and third bending modes of the foundation, respectively.
Analyzing the measured temperature of the outlet oil. It is clear that the temperature in-
creases signiﬁcantly with the angular velocity. Therefore, the changes in system dynamics
can be attributed not only to the increased angular velocity but also to a decrease in oil
viscosity.
It is also evident from the FRF that the run-out compensation completely eliminates
the 1Ωunbalance vibrations at speeds below 5.5 rpm. However, above 5.5 rpm, small
ampliﬁcations related to the 1Ωcomponents are observed.
This is expected because,
at high speeds, the rotational speed is known to vary signiﬁcantly, while the encoder
lines pr. sampling frequency increases, consequently decreasing the quality of the run-out
compensation.
4.5.4
Summary of EMA experiments
From the EMA analysis of the foundation, it can be concluded that with diﬀerent excita-
tion directions, diﬀerent modes of the system can be captured. Due to foundation-sensor
coupling, rotor and foundation modes are both captured but cannot be distinguished
without additional knowledge. In total 8 foundation modes and 2 rotor modes within 250
Hz can be found using the displacement sensors mounted on the foundation for 1000 rpm.
And at 7000 rpm only three modes below 80 Hz are found using horizontal excitation,
while more modes are expected to exist. It is also documented how the dynamics of the
ATPJB system change with the rotational speed, where damping of the second torsional
foundation mode and backward rotor mode is vanishing with increasing speed. Many bias
sources are present including, shaker interference, identiﬁcation of closely spaced modes
using SIMO, assumption of self-adjointness, and sensor-foundation coupling. Hence it
must be concluded that the general EMA results are signiﬁcantly biased. Consequently,
it is crucial to exercise caution when utilizing the EMA results as a benchmark for modal
parameters.
4.6
Passive hydrodynamic vs controllable hydrostatic
lubrication including temperature variations
This section will analyze how the supply pressure ps,HP of the controllable hydrostatic lu-
brication aﬀects the system dynamics including temperature eﬀects. Figure 4.17 presents
FRFs obtained using horizontal shaker excitation for diﬀerent controllable and passive
lubrication setups.5 The controllable lubrication is used with two spool positions, a cen-
5To obtain a good quality run-out compensation it is important to perform the run-out measurement
with controllable lubrication at a steady state temperature
77
==PAGE:89==
4.6. PASSIVE HYDRODYNAMIC VS CONTROLLABLE HYDROSTATIC LUBRICATION
INCLUDING TEMPERATURE VARIATIONS
tered spool (CS), and settings that push the shaft to its passive equilibrium (PE) while
varying the supply pressure ps,HP. The average temperature of the outlet oil from the
ATPJB is also obtained for each experiment. Three passive tests are performed pre and
post, the controllable tests including an experiment with artiﬁcially heated oil. The EMA
p-LSCF routine has been used to estimate the modal parameters of all tests. Insigniﬁ-
cant ﬂuctuations are found considering undamped natural frequencies as also indicated
by the alignment of FRF peaks. However, the damping ratio is found to vary slightly for
the modes at 60, 92, and 97 Hz, where the damping ratios of the latter are indicated in
Figure 4.17.
Figure 4.17: FRF comparison using passive hydrodynamic and controllable hydrostatic
lubrication. CS: centered spool, PE: passive equilibrium.
By analyzing the passive experiments the temperature is found to correlate positively with
the supply pressure ps,HP as can be expected due to an increased ﬂow and consequently
frictional forces. It is also observed that with increasing temperature an amplitude in-
crease is found for the 92Hz resonance, whereas the remaining parts of the spectrum are
more or less unchanged. The amplitude increase is likely explained by the decreased oil
viscosity which is expected to reduce the energy dissipation properties of the oil.
Assessing the passive vs. controllable lubrication FRFs, diﬀerences are found in the low-
frequency range below 60 Hz while also around the 90-100 Hz resonances. Other regions
in the analyzed spectrum show small to no variation between FRFs.
Ampliﬁcations of the FRFs in the low-frequency range for CS lubrication indicate that
the rotor system is softened proportional to the supply pressure. This might be explained
by the fact that an increasing amount of oil will be supplied around the circumference
of the rotor increasing the ﬁlm thickness and consequently reducing the ﬁlm stiﬀness. It
is also seen that extra damping has been introduced for the 97 Hz mode, which also ﬁts
78
==PAGE:90==
4.7. FLUID FILM PERTURBATION
the theory of softening, allowing for better energy dissipation considering rotor-related
modes.
The PE lubrication has a much smaller eﬀect on the low-frequency band where a small
reduction is found. The amplitude of the rotor modes around 95Hz is again proportional
to the supply pressure. Although for ps,HP ≤20 the response is almost unchanged with
respect to the passive FRFs. The damping ratio of the 97 Hz mode is also found to reduce
slightly for high supply pressures.
From this analysis, it is concluded that using passive hydrostatic lubrication has little to
no eﬀect on the modal parameters of the system, while the response amplitude changes
slightly in certain frequency regions. Passive equilibrium (PE) lubrication is found to
have the smallest eﬀect compared to passive conditions and can be considered the least
invasive during shaker excitation.
4.7
Fluid ﬁlm perturbation
This section will cover the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation strategy and terminology used within
this thesis.
4.7.1
DC and AC levels
In this thesis ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation will only be performed using a Gaussian band-limited
white noise signal sent to the servo valves. This choice of excitation is likely to comply
with the OMA assumption of white noise input, although bearing perturbation using
chirp signals has also been found successful by other authors such as Simonsen [22] and
Svendsen [21].
Figure 4.18: Example of servo valve signal
from a band-limited white noise signal gen-
erator.
The servo valve signal is described by its
DC and AC levels. An example of a Gaus-
sian band-limited white noise servo valve
signal is shown in Figure 4.18. The signal
to servo valve i can in simple terms be de-
scribed by the relation SVi = DCi+ACi(t)
hence the DC and AC levels are deﬁning
the constant and temporal properties of
the signal, respectively. The DC value de-
scribes the mean of the servo valve signal,
hence when the servo valves are not used
for excitation, the DC level deﬁnes the po-
sition of the spools and can be used to con-
trol the position of the rotor (see section
4.4). In this project, the AC level ACi(t)
is always a zero-mean Gaussian band-limited white noise process, purely deﬁned by its
noise standard deviation σSVi = σSV , omitting the index i since the same standard devi-
ation will always be used for both servo valves. The standard deviation of the Gaussian
white noise σSV hence controls the amplitude distribution of the excitation. Using the
general rule of thumb the maximum amplitude variation of the Gaussian white noise
signal is approximately ±3σSV [26].
79
==PAGE:91==
4.7. FLUID FILM PERTURBATION
4.7.2
Perturbation strategy
By controlling the two servo valves, the shaft can be displaced in any direction in the
Y Z-plane bounded by the tilting pads and ﬂuid ﬂow. The ﬂuid ﬂow injection direction
as a function of servo valve inputs SVi is illustrated in Figure 4.6. Hence it is possible to
excite the rotor horizontally by sending the same AC signal to both valves and vertically
by equal AC signals of opposite signs. While these perturbation methods would provide
a good comparison to EMA tests they are not desired in an OMA test setting. In OMA
one desires to have as many independent inputs as possible such that the rank of the
measurement system is not limited. By sending the same signal to both servo valves
the input will be of rank 1 since it stems from the same distribution. Therefore in this
project, it is decided to use two uncorrelated servo signals. In practice, this is obtained
by using two diﬀerent seeds for the Gaussian white noise generators. Figure 4.19 shows
the singular values of the spectral matrix for exemplary experiments with varying supply
pressure and AC levels. First, it is highlighted from Figure 4.19 that two singular value
curves are seen to rise above the noise ﬂoor for almost every test. This observation suggests
that two independent inputs have been applied to the system, consistent with the use of
two uncorrelated servo valve signals.
Hence it is also concluded that the rank of the
measurement system is likely reduced to 2 which is small compared to recommendations
from Brincker [27].
(a) Varying AC level with ps,HP = 20bar.
(b) Varying supply pressure ps,HP .
Figure 4.19: Singular values of the spectral density matrix for diﬀerent perturbation
settings.
Simonsen [22] who also performed ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation recommended using top pad
excitation only for obtaining the best non-invasive conditions. Top pad excitation can be
obtained by using a DC level SV1 > 2.6 and SV2 < 2 which will naturally focus the ﬂuid
toward the top pads. However pure top pad excitation is hard to obtain in practice since
the bottom pads will in this conﬁguration allow a varying leakage ﬂow which will disturb
the bottom ﬂuid ﬁlm and might also excite the rotor.
Another approach is to position the spools in their center position SV CS
i
= {2.6, 2} which
only allows for a small leakage ﬂow through all inlets. Perturbation could then be per-
formed by displacing the spools slightly, which easily alters the rotor position as the rotor
displacement vs. SV curve dY,Z is steep. A hypothesis is that displacing the spool such
that it closes the leakage underlap of the bottom pad port and opens the top pad port
would result in top pad excitation with enough power to excite the rotor. There are
however several implications regarding this approach. First, the signal amplitude needed
80
==PAGE:92==
4.7. FLUID FILM PERTURBATION
to displace the spool such that the bottom pad port closes is unknown. However, it is
expected to be very small considering the steep rotor displacement vs. SV signal curves
around the spool center. A qualiﬁed guess of the signal amplitude would be in the range
of [0.01, 0.1]V corresponding to a noise std. σSV = [0.0033, 0.03]. However from Figure
4.19a it is observed that the two dominating singular values for σSV = 0.05V no longer
have any clear indication of dynamics and all singular values are predominantly measure-
ment noise. It is therefore expected that using a small AC level of σSV = [0.0033, 0.03] is
not suﬃcient for delivering the required excitation to distinguish the rotor response from
the measurement noise. Hence the idea of displacing the spool within the underlapped
region is likely impossible.
Figure 4.19b shows the singular values of the spectral matrix when changing supply pres-
sure ps,HP. It is noticed that for all pressure settings, the two largest singular values all
rise signiﬁcantly from the noise ﬂoor, indicating that the system dynamics are properly
captured. However, a correlation is found between the magnitude of the two largest singu-
lar values and the supply pressure. It is also noticed that for a decreasing supply pressure,
the diﬀerence between the two large singular values of each ps,HP increase, indicating that
the pad injection dynamics are becoming more coupled, decreasing the ability to iden-
tify closely spaced poles. Remembering the supply pressure discussion from section 4.6,
where it is naturally found that decreasing the supply pressure ps,HP signiﬁcantly reduces
the eﬀect of the controllable lubrication. It is concluded that a low supply pressure of
ps,HP = [12, 20] bar will be suﬃcient for modal parameter estimation while keeping the
controllable perturbation least invasive.
From the above discussion also considering the eﬀects of the supply pressure and rotor
position (section 4.6) two main perturbation strategies have been imposed.
1. Centered spool (CS) perturbation. Meaning that the DC levels of the servo valves
are adjusted to SV CS
i
= {2.6, 2} such that the rotor is symmetrically excited through
both top and bottom pads.
2. Passive Equilibrium (PE) perturbation.
This requires the machine operator (or
potentially an integral controller) to adjust the servo valve inputs such that the
rotor is positioned in its passive equilibrium, meaning the position of the rotor
during its operating condition with no controllable hydrostatic lubrication. This
will result in a dominating top pad excitation due to the required shift in the DC
level of the servo valves.
Figure 4.20 tries to illustrate how the two perturbation strategies will aﬀect the rotor
displacement. The plots are made by plotting the ﬁtted logistic function dY,Z(SV1, SV2)
on top of the probability density functions (PDFs) of the Gaussian white noise input
signal for diﬀerent AC levels.
The dY,Z function is sliced in two ways: dY,Z(SV1, 2)
and dY,Z(SV1, 1.7) resembling CS and PE perturbation respectively. Where SV2 = 1.7
bar is the approximate value needed to push the rotor to its passive equilibrium posi-
tion. The displacements are oﬀset such that zero displacement corresponds to the passive
equilibrium. It is visible from Figure 4.20 that with a DC level of SVi = {2.8, 1.7}V
one approximately reaches the passive equilibrium position, hence the vertical DC-line
crosses the dY,Z curves at approximately zero in both Y and Z directions. It should be
mentioned that the plots assume no delay between the servo valve signal and the rotor
movement, hence a direct relation between peak-to-peak response during white noise ﬂuid
81
==PAGE:93==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
ﬁlm excitation is not directly possible.
DC = 2.6V
DC = 2.8V
DC = 2.6V
DC = 2.8V
Figure 4.20: Illustration of the expected perturbation range as a function of the Gaussian
servo valve input at 1000 rpm.
Plots are made by ﬁxing SV2 and plotting the ﬁtted
dY,Z(SV1) function.
From the plots, it is noticed that even for σSV = 0.3V the full range of possible positions
of the rotor is covered by the corresponding PDF, and for higher σSV the rotor is simply
more likely to be in the maximum possible positions. When using CS perturbation the
rotor will naturally be displaced equally in positive and negative directions since the mean
of the servo valve signal (DC level) coincides with the dY,Z midpoints. Whereas when
using the PE perturbation the DC level of the excitation distribution is shifted such that
the displacement is more likely to become saturated in one direction compared to the
other. More speciﬁcally it is expected that the shaft will be displaced signiﬁcantly more
in the negative and positive Y and Z directions respectively using PE excitation. However,
for small σSV the excitation is likely still symmetric since the input PDFs do not reach
the saturated region.
4.8
OMA analysis at 1000 rpm
This section will utilize the previous knowledge of how the bearing and equilibrium po-
sition is aﬀected by supply pressure, temperature, and servo valve input to analyze the
possibility of performing non-invasive testing via ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation at 1000 rpm.
Several OMA methods will be utilized under diﬀerent perturbation conﬁgurations and
the results will be discussed.
4.8.1
Test plan
A test plan is built aiming at analyzing the eﬀect of the supply pressure ps,HP, the
spool position i.e. the DC level of the SV signal (CS or PE), and the AC level i.e. the

previous sections low supply pressures are naturally seen to be least invasive towards
passive lubrication while also a pressure of 20 and 12 bar is enough to yield signiﬁcant
excitation of the ATPJB system. Hence only these pressure levels are used. In total 24
tests are performed as summarized in Table 4.4.

Using the EMA results from Figure 4.1, a conservative estimate of the required test time

of 27Hz. With an assumed damping of 3% the required test time becomes 247 s. From
this estimation, the test time is chosen to be 240 s (4 min) for all tests. It should be noted

measurement.
4.8.2
Preliminary signal analysis
Preliminary signal analysis is performed to analyze the quality of the vibration measure-
ments made during ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation.
OMA methods assume Gaussian responses, making it crucial to validate this assumption.
Verifying it helps gauge the potential bias in the ﬁnal modal parameters. Therefore to
analyze the distribution of the measured signals a histogram and normal probability plot
are shown in Figure 4.21 for sensor 4 (vertical) using experiments made with σSV = [0.1, 1]
V, ps,HP = [12, 20] bar, and with two diﬀerent DC levels; CS (SV = {2.6 ,2 } V) and PE
(SV ≈{2.8 ,1.7 } V).
83
==PAGE:95==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
Figure 4.21: Histograms and normal probability plot of sensor 4’s (vertical Z-direction)
signal during diﬀerent perturbation conditions.
The histograms clearly demonstrate that using CS perturbation results in a wider distri-
bution that closely resembles a normal distribution, as expected from section 4.7. This
observation is further supported by the normal probability plots, which show that the
data approximately follows a straight line. However, deviations from normality are ev-
ident when considering highly negative data points, which indicate drops in the rotor’s
negative Z-directions. These drops are likely caused by irregularities in the ﬂuid ﬁlm, pos-
sibly caused by a known ﬂaw in the high-pressure pump. This pump ﬂaw intermittently
causes a loss of pressure for a short duration of [0.5-1] seconds, which likely contributes to
the presence of outliers in the data. It is important to note that the normal probability
plot does not fully capture the vast number of data points, and thus the outliers repre-
sent extreme values that are not statistically signiﬁcant as they reside in the outermost
quantiles of the dataset.
When the DC level is changed such that the rotor is positioned in its passive equilibrium
(PE) the distributions become more narrow and also appear to be right-skewed especially
for σSV = 1V, meaning that the rotor is mostly displaced in the positive Z-direction. This
result is explained by the saturation of the servo valve signal with respect to the rotor
position. As previously illustrated in Figure 4.20 increasing the servo signal signiﬁcantly
away from its center will eventually have no eﬀect on the shaft position. Hence when the
DC value of the valves is adjusted to push the rotor down into its passive equilibrium
position, the rotor can be excited well in the upward positive Z-direction but poorly in
the downward Z-direction. The skewness of the response is also visible in the normal
probability plots where the data clearly falls below the normal line.
Analyzing the inﬂuence of the supply pressure ps,HP it is clear that increasing the supply
pressure increases the width of the signal distribution. This is expected since increas-
ing the supply pressure ps,HP naturally allows for higher ﬂuid forces and hence a larger
displacement of the rotor.
Moreover with higher supply pressure the skewness of the
84
==PAGE:96==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
response is also reduced. This can be explained by analyzing the required DC level to
displace the bearing into its passive equilibrium. For 20 bar the DC values are approxi-
mately SVi = {2.78, 1.65} while for 12 bar they are SVi = {2.81, 1.58}. Hence for 12 bar
a larger deviation from the CS input is needed to deliver the required ﬂow and the signal
is therefore closer to the saturation limit which increases the amplitude skewness.
Very similar observations can be made for the horizontal sensor 3 data shown in Appendix
H, but now with a left-skewed nature using PE perturbation (skewed towards negative
Y-direction).

From the power spectra, it is again clearly noticed that decreasing the AC level decreases

the spectrum. When the response is below 10 dB some lightly damped spikes appear in
the spectrum. These are not multiples of the rotational speed and are likely to appear
from other vibrating components of the test rig. However, it should be noted that these
spikes are likely insigniﬁcant considering their minimal amplitude of 20 dB below other
signiﬁcant system dynamics. A drop in input power is expected when exceeding the nat-
ural frequency of the servo valves. And from the power spectrum of especially sensor 1, it
becomes apparent that the response energy becomes small above 150 Hz. It is therefore
also expected to be impossible to obtain good estimates of modal parameters above 150
Hz.

Initial data processing
To avoid aliasing from downsampling and to isolate the frequency band of interest, a
low-pass ﬁlter is applied to all data sets using an FFT ﬁlter with Hanning tapering from
250-300Hz. The data is then decimated 6 times to a sampling rate of 500Hz giving a
Nyquist frequency of 250Hz.
The correlation function (CF) matrix is calculated using the direct method using 100
discrete time lags. To remove noise accumulated in the ﬁrst time-lags, the ﬁrst 2 time-
lags have been removed. An example of the R1,1 CF is shown in Figure 4.23a. It is
observed that the function quickly decays within a few time lags as expected for heavily
damped systems. After the initial decay, the CF function oscillates with almost constant
amplitude indicating that small undamped components in the signal are apparent. These
are likely the same frequency components observed as noise peaks below 10 dB in the
power spectrum, Figure 4.22.
The CF matrix length of 100 time-lags is chosen as a
compromise trying to eliminate these harmonics but still supply the OMA routines with
suﬃcient data to avoid having poorly conditioned problems.
86
==PAGE:98==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
The SD matrix is calculated using Welch’s method with Hanning windows and 50% overlap
and is the main input for the FDD. Examples of spectral densities are shown in Figure
4.22.
It is also noted that the ITD, ERA, and PR use the CFs as input, while the SSI is
data-driven and hence uses the data matrix directly.
(a) Correlation function
(b) Half Spectrum (FDD)
Figure 4.23: (a): Correlation function R11(k) and (b): singular values of the half spectrum
for CS excitation with ps,HP = 20 bar and σSV = 1 V, including FDD ﬁts.
ITD and FDD setup
The ITD has no adjustable parameters other than the state of the given correlation
matrix. Hence the number of output poles of the ITD is restricted to the number of
sensors No = 10.
The FDD method utilizes manual peak picking of signiﬁcant ampliﬁcation in the singular
value plot. The chosen frequencies are [27,60,69,92,98,117,125,140]Hz. To avoid estima-
tion errors, only 8 out of the 10 maximum modes (corresponding to the number of sensors)
are selected, although 8 modes may still be considered relatively high. It is generally ob-
served that the FDD estimate does not align well with the experimental data, particularly
in terms of damping, which appears to be underestimated with sharper resonance peaks.
This discrepancy can be observed from the half-spectrum ﬁts in Figure 4.23b.
A-SSI, A-ERA, and A-PR automation parameters
The SSI, ERA, and PR methods are automatized using the approach described in section
2.3.11, and hence denoted A-SSI, A-ERA, and A-PR respectively. For ease of understand-
ing the ﬂowchart of the AOMA routine is repeated here in Figure 4.24.
Figure 4.24: Flowchart of the AOMA routine(s). Repeated ﬁgure from section 2.3.11.
87
==PAGE:99==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
The stabilization accuracy parameters (εf, εζ, εϕ) are chosen by visual inspection of the
stabilization charts.6 Where the aim is to obtain stabilized and distinct modal parameters.
The accuracy of the A-PR method had to be increased compared to A-SSI and A-ERA to
obtain any form of stabilization. The order range [nmin, nmax] for the diﬀerent methods
is also chosen considering their stabilization charts. The number of block Hankel rows
s and the maximum number of AR coeﬃcients NA are chosen such that the maximum
number of modes is 200 for all methods. The model order range of the data-driven SSI is
signiﬁcantly over-speciﬁed to properly model the noise in the signals, and the order range
is signiﬁcantly lower for ERA and PR which use processed CFs. The model order of the
PR method directly follows the number of AR coeﬃcients since the problems have to
be individually solved by least squares for every order, with no possibility of sub-ordered
problems. The ﬁtted CFs to R1,1 are also shown in Figure 4.23a using the maximum model
order for both SSI, ERA, and PR methods, i.e. 100, 50, and 200 modes, respectively. The
ﬁt exhibits good performance for initial time lags but worsens in quality for higher time
lags, which are not of high interest anyway.
An example of stabilization charts for each method is shown in Figure 4.25.
(a) SSI n ∈[50, 200]
(b) ERA n ∈[4, 100]
(c) PR na ∈[1, 40]
Figure 4.25: Stabilization diagrams from SSI, ERA, and PR methods plotted on top the
singular values of the spectral matrix σ(G). For test data with CS excitation, ps,HP = 20
bar, and σSV = 1 V. The grey region indicates low input energy.
It is noticed that the SSI method estimates many poles throughout the full spectrum, this
is mostly caused by the high model order. The ERA method shows good stabilization
of many modes already for small model orders. It is also noticed that only a few modes
are estimated at higher frequencies above 150 Hz whereas the SSI estimates many non-
stabilized modes in this band. This is an advantage of the ERA since modes in this region
are not expected to represent physical dynamics due to poor excitation. The poles of the
PR method almost appear random across the spectrum and only yield a few stabilized
modes.
The hierarchical clustering method requires the choice of two parameters: A cluster cutoﬀ
dMACXPcut and the number of returned clusters Nclust. The cluster cutoﬀis chosen as
dMACXPcut = 0.04 for all tests based on the separation of the rotor modes around 95 Hz.
The number of returned modes is simply chosen as the same number of modes identiﬁed
by EMA Nclust = 10.
An example of a dendrogram presenting the distance between
6The accuracy parameters are tolerances used to compare modal parameters between model orders
88
==PAGE:100==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
clusters calculated from hierarchical clustering is shown in Figure 4.26 including the used
cutoﬀseparating the colored clusters.
Figure 4.26: Dendrogram showing the distance between clusters calculated from the hier-
archical clustering including the cutoﬀseparating the colored clusters. For test data with
CS excitation, ps,HP = 20bar, and σSV = 1V.
The colored groups of modal parameters indicate that some clusters hold signiﬁcantly
more modal parameters than others. Following the strategy from Paulsen [24] it is claimed
that the more modal parameters that estimate a speciﬁc mode shape, the more likely
that modeshape is to be physical. In this way, the mode clusters are sorted and the Nclust
biggest clusters are returned.
Often, the OMA method will not return the Nclust = 10 modes identiﬁed by EMA, and
most frequently, a fraction of the returned clusters include smaller sub-clusters of other
already identiﬁed modes or, alternatively, noise modes also present in the data.
One
could argue that lowering Nclust would solve this issue. However, this creates a problem
where low participation modes (modes that are unique but weakly present) will not be
included in the top inhabited clusters, and modes will often be concentrated around high-
energy frequency bands. In pursuit to keep Nclust relatively high while removing possible
sub-clusters, a last frequency cluster-sort is performed. If two clusters are within the
frequency tolerance εf,c only the cluster with the highest amount of modal parameters
is kept. This has the wanted eﬀect of removing duplicated sub-clusters that often arise
due to parameter variation through diﬀerent model orders (convergence). However one
has to be very careful in choosing the value of εf,c. If the tolerance is too high, possible
closely-spaced modes will be discarded, and if the tolerance is too low, sub-clusters are not
removed properly. Setting the tolerance hence requires pre-analysis or prior knowledge of
the system dynamics. In this project, the two closest modes are approximately 92 and 98
Hz hence εf,c is chosen as 3 Hz such that these modes do not cancel each other.
4.8.4
Results
The results obtained by ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation at 1000 rpm using the ITD, A-SSI, A-ERA,
A-PR, and FDD OMA methods will now be presented.
The methods have been run on all 24 experimental tests. To gain an overview of the pole
placement, the resulting undamped natural frequencies fn are presented in Figure 4.27a.
The marker type and color indicate the test type, pressure, and AC-level respectively.
The diﬀerent OMA methods are distributed on the y-axis and further subdivided into
test types (pressure and spool setting). EMA results are indicated by black lines.
89
==PAGE:101==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
(a) Undamped natural frequencies
(b) Damping ratios
Figure 4.27: Results from all tests and OMA methods. (a): undamped natural frequen-
cies. (b): damping ratios of modes corresponding EMA estimates. The ﬁgure legend
applies to both (a) and (b).
First, it is noticed that many frequencies are scattered in-between EMA estimates using
the AOMA methods. This is likely a result of a model over-speciﬁcation which is required
to capture all meaningful dynamics but has the downside of yielding mathematical and
spurious modes. A higher density of frequencies is nevertheless found around EMA modes,
whereas other clusters of modal parameters are also identiﬁed at 47, 150, and 174.7 Hz,
marked by red dashed lines. By considering the previous discussion of the power spectrum
and its singular values these frequencies match well with possible noise peaks in the
spectrum. To simplify the analysis of modal parameters, it is assumed that parameters
closest to the EMA estimate for every test are considered an estimate of that mode. This
assumption is considered decent for some modes where the OMA estimates are rather
90
==PAGE:102==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
consistent: i.e. 60 and 69 Hz whereas less good for estimates of the rotor modes at 92
and 98 Hz. The simpliﬁcation is made nevertheless and its eﬀect has to be considered
in the following discussions. The unknown modes are also sorted into their own groups
of modal parameters. The sorting allows plots of the damping ratio for every mode as
shown in Figure 4.27b.
The performance of every OMA method will now be analyzed one by one considering the
plots of the undamped natural frequencies and damping ratios in Figure 4.27:
ITD:
The ITD method clearly does not predict all modes of interest. A concentration
of modes is found at low frequencies below 27 Hz all with very high damping. The method
does however provide reasonable estimates of the two foundation modes 3 and 4 at 60
and 69 Hz when the AC level is high (markers are mostly red). Modes are also predicted
around the rotor modes 5 and 6 at 90-100 Hz, although the ITD is unable to separate
the two rotor modes and predicts the natural frequency as a combination of the two.
Due to the mode-sorting one should therefore take care when concluding the estimated
damping ratios of modes 5 and 6, although it is clear that the damping is close to the EMA
estimates. The ITD’s overall performance is expected due to several limitations. With
only No = 10 input channels, it can predict a maximum of 10 modes and is also known
to struggle with coupled and damped responses. Expecting accurate predictions for 10
unique modes is hence optimistic, especially considering measurement noise which requires
the prediction of additional modal parameters. Consequently, the predicted modes of the
ITD are likely biased but physical. The ITD also fails to identify unknown modes (red
dashed lines), suggesting their weak presence as noise components in the data.
A-SSI:
The A-SSI shows estimates at all expected EMA frequencies including a signif-
icant amount of spurious poles. Spurious poles are expected due to the high modal order
required for a data-driven SSI. Undamped natural frequencies are consistent for modes at
27, 60, 69, and 140 Hz ignoring estimates for low AC levels. More scattered estimates are
found for modes at 92, 98, 116, and 125 Hz. Considering the damping estimates they are
often reasonable but scattered across tests. The noise modes at 47, 150, and 174 Hz are
also very consistent although often only found for low AC levels (blue markers) indicating
that these do not relate to the ATPJB system dynamics.
A-ERA:
The A-ERA shows much fewer estimates of spurious poles compared to the
other AOMA methods, as expected from its more clear stabilization diagram (Figure
4.25b). Consistent undamped natural frequency estimates are also obtained across diﬀer-
ent test setups for almost every EMA and unknown mode. The estimated damping ratios
are also reasonably well estimated compared to the EMA estimates, although a spread is
still seen across all modes for diﬀerent test setups.
A-PR:
The A-PR shows a more random scatter of undamped natural frequencies across
the frequency band, as expected from the confusing stabilization diagram (Figure4.25c).
A concentration of modes is however found for some EMA modes, especially at 60 and 69
Hz. It is also noted that the ﬁrst foundation mode at 27.5 Hz is almost only predicted using
PE perturbation. Additionally, unlike the A-SSI method, there are very few estimates of
91
==PAGE:103==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
the rotor mode at 98 Hz. While the noise components at 150 and 174.4 are still estimated
whereas the 47 Hz component is very rarely found.
FDD:
The FDD is the only frequency domain technique and relies on a frequency-
picking procedure of the 8 frequency points {27, 60, 69, 92, 98, 117, 125, 140}Hz. Hence
only an estimate of these modes is expected. Weirdly an estimate of the 140 Hz modes
is missing but an extra mode below 117 Hz is seen. All modes are consistently estimated
however they all seem to be underestimated in both frequency and damping. From inspec-
tion of the singular value ﬁts as the one shown in Figure 4.23b the modal ﬁt appears to be
quite poor with less coupled modes of more sharp peaks (lower damping). By inspection
of the singular values of the spectral matrix, only 2 signiﬁcant singular values are found,
however, the ATPJB response is expected to be heavily coupled hence more than 2 modes
will contribute to the response at almost all frequencies. This makes it very hard for the
modal ﬁltering to work properly and the estimates will be accordingly poor.
Figure 4.28: Undamped natural frequencies fn and
damping ratios ζn for all tests of the unknown modes
at 47, 150, and 174.6 Hz.
The unknown noise modes at 47,
150, and 174.7 Hz will now be fur-
ther discussed.
The undamped
natural frequency fn and damp-
ing ratio ζn are plotted as a func-
tion of the AC level for the A-
SSI, A-ERA, and A-PR methods
(ITD and FDD do not ﬁnd them)
in Figure 4.28. First, it is noted
that the damping ratio of the
noise modes is small in relation
to the ATPJB modes, especially
for noise modes 2 and 3 where it
is often estimated well below 1%,
implying that these modes are of
other sources. It is also noted that
the number of estimates reduces
as the AC level increases. Especially for noise mode 1. The reason for this can be ex-
plained by analyzing the power spectra as the ones shown in Figures 4.22. As the AC
level increases, the sharp peak at 47 Hz vanishes completely. This eﬀect is less notice-
able for the 174.4 Hz component since the input energy is always small independent of
the AC level in this frequency band. From this discussion, it is concluded that these
modes correspond to small frequency components in the signal not related to the ATPJB
system dynamics. The 150 Hz component is with high certainty 3 times the electrical
power source frequency, whereas the 47 and 174.4 modes might appear from sources such
as vibrating piping or potential aliasing.
One could easily believe that the frequency
components are caused by super-synchronous vibrations of the unbalance, however, no
clear order relation is identiﬁed. While also the mode at 174 Hz, could potentially corre-
spond to the natural frequency of the servo valves. However by inspection of Figure 4.19b
this frequency component is found constant when increasing the supply pressure which
disproves the hypothesis. 7

4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
MAC analysis
From the analysis of the undamped natural frequency and damping ratios using Figure
4.27 it is troublesome to see any signiﬁcant eﬀects caused by the changing DC level (SC
and PE), AC-level σSV , and supply pressure ps,HP. Hence to further enrich the analysis,
the modal estimates are compared using the MACXP assurance criteria in Figure 4.30.8
Separate MACXP plots are made for the two spool perturbation settings: Centered spool
(CS) and passive equilibrium (PE) such that a comparison between the two perturbation
settings is possible by matching the plots. The MACXP axes are divided into the OMA
methods and further subdivided into speciﬁc tests. A detailed explanation of the MACXP
axes is shown in Figure 4.29 by zoom-in of the lower right corner of mode 6 (PE). Hence
there are 12 tiles separating each method, with every 6th tile representing the same supply
pressure. The left and top tiles include the three EMA tests (horizontal, vertical, and
45-deg), enabling EMA-OMA comparison. When an estimate of a mode is missing for
a given combination of OMA or EMA method and test setup the tiles corresponding to
that combination are given a grey color.
Figure 4.29: Detailed explanation of MACXP plot by zoom-in of the lower right corner.
MACXP plot of mode 6 - PE perturbation.
Upon deep inspection of the MACXP plots, numerous minor conclusions can be derived.
However, it is important to acknowledge that these conclusions often lack signiﬁcance
and cannot be extrapolated across all modes. Therefore, to ensure a general analysis, the
discussion of the plots will adopt a more holistic and global approach.
First, it is noticed that almost no estimates are found for modes 2 and 10 across all
estimation methods and test setups. Mode 2 was also found with a very low participation
factor in the EMA results and it is therefore expected that the OMA methods won’t
ﬁnd this mode. Mode 10 is not found due to poor excitation at this frequency as also
previously discussed.
8This criterion compares both mode shapes and pole simultaneously, see section 2.2 for further infor-
mation

Figure 4.30: MACXP comparison for all tests and OMA methods. The grey color indicates

94
==PAGE:106==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
Across all plots, a checker pattern is observed, see e.g. mode 4 where this eﬀect is clear.
The pattern arises due to missing estimates when the AC level becomes too small. Hence
it becomes clear that for all estimation methods, there is a limit to when the signal-
to-noise ratio becomes so small that information on the system modes disappears. To
consistently obtain modal estimates using all OMA methods, it is recommended to use
an AC level of at least σSV = 0.7V.
By comparing CS and PE perturbation plots they appear very similar although small
diﬀerences can be observed for individual modes where the CS perturbation method pro-
vides a slight increase in the number of estimated parameters, see e.g. modes 3, 4, 6, and
8 where the number and comparison between successful modal estimates increases.
Modal parameter sets that compare well to EMA estimates are often found using the A-
SSI, and A-ERA methods. See e.g. modes 1, 3, 4, 6, and 9 indicated by a red color along
the top and right center tiles corresponding to A-SSI, A-ERA, and EMA comparisons.
These modes generally also compare well with other AOMA methods indicated by oﬀ-
diagonal blocks of red tiles.
Optimal analysis method and non-invasive tests
Based on the preceding analysis, it is concluded that the A-SSI and A-ERA methods
consistently identify the most relevant modes while minimizing the number of spurious
poles. Additionally, employing a high AC level above σSV = 0.7V yields an optimal signal-
to-noise ratio, thereby enhancing the performance of OMA methods. The subsequent
analysis will now put focus on how the diﬀerent perturbation parameters aﬀect the ATPJB
modal estimates. And if they do so, in what way?
The undamped natural frequency fn and damping ratio ζn are plotted against the AC
level for only the A-SSI and A-ERA in Figure 4.31. The marker type indicates the test
setup while EMA estimates are shown by horizontal black lines. The fn plots show ±3%
of the EMA estimate to establish a common sense of deviation across modes.
A general convergent trend is found for fn and ζn when increasing the AC level. Modal
estimates across methods and tests are observed to be increasingly concentrated often
towards the EMA estimate.
However, the direction of convergence is not necessarily
consistent. E.g for modes 1 and 3 fn is respectively underestimated and overestimated
for small σSV . Some modes do not have such converging nature such as mode 7 where
the natural frequency seems to vary for all AC levels, although its damping ratio clearly
seems to become more consistent across estimation methods and tests.
Analyzing the eﬀect of supply pressure ps,HP and perturbation setting (CS and PE) it
is very hard to ﬁnd any overall trends. A possible correlation is found between ζn and
σSV of mode 9 predicted by SSI considering the red square markers (PE ps,HP = 12bar)
where the damping ratio increases monotonically with the AC level. However, the trend
is not repeated by the ERA estimates on the same tests (yellow square markers) while
similar trends are also hard to ﬁnd elsewhere. Hence it is in general not possible to ﬁnd
any signiﬁcant trends between the diﬀerent perturbation setups and their eﬀect on the
modal parameters.
95
==PAGE:107==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
Figure 4.31: Undamped natural frequencies fn and damping ratios ζn versus AC level for
A-SSI and A-ERA. fn axis shows the ±3% of the EMA estimate.
To further analyze the eﬀects of perturbation settings new MACXP plots are generated by
discarding poor estimation methods and testing setups. Hence the ITD, FDD, and A-PR
methods are removed, while tests using an AC level of σSV = {0.05, 0.1, 0.3, 0.5}V are also
removed. The reduced MACXP plots are shown in Figure 4.32 including an explanation
of the new axes.
Analyzing the reduced MACXP plots it is clear that modes 3, 4, 6, and 9 are predicted
very consistently among EMA methods, AOMA methods, and diﬀerent tests. Hence it
must also be concluded that no eﬀect of the diﬀerent perturbation settings is visible in
the resulting modal estimate.

The rotor mode 5 is estimated consistently across both AOMA methods however the

provides slightly better comparisons between all methods.
Mode 1 is also reasonably well estimated although an estimate of A-SSI for CS. ps,HP = 20,

shapes is found.

participation. The same can be said about modes 7 and 8 where by analysis of the spectral
density and corresponding singular values, only small ampliﬁcations are found at these
frequencies. Hence if the interest is only on the most dominating modes of the ATPJB
system these modes are not signiﬁcant, and they would likely be discarded in a SHM or
CM routine where the focus would be on the more dominating modes.

Again OMA estimates of some modes are missing for reasons already discussed.
A good agreement is generally seen comparing the undamped natural frequency with a
relative deviation of all below 3% and many below 1%. Analyzing the damping ratios
generally larger deviations are found up to 25%, while the damping estimates from OMA
are generally larger than those predicted by EMA, indicating that the damping might be
slightly increased using ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation. However, for mode 1 the OMA method
consistently estimates a damping of 3.4, 3.5% which is signiﬁcantly smaller than the EMA
estimate of 4.67%. The damping ratio of modes 8 and 9 shows the best agreement between
EMA and OMA estimates, which is likely caused by the smaller damping ratio of these
modes allowing for better separation of these modes. Note also that a deviation of 25%
98
==PAGE:110==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
is considered small when analyzing damping ratios that can easily diﬀerentiate by orders
of magnitude.
The discrepancies seen between OMA and EMA methods do not necessarily mean that the
OMA is wrong, and vice versa. As previously mentioned the EMA estimates are expected
to be biased due to the shaker attachment and the assumption of self-adjointness. With
that said one could come to the conclusion that the OMA estimate is the true (or close to
true) value of the modal parameters, especially considering that there exists a quite small
diﬀerence in both fn and ζn across both method and perturbation setups. However, such
a conclusion is not necessarily true either. As already discussed it is still unknown if the
ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation has an eﬀect on the modal parameter. However, the eﬀect (if it
exists) is likely smaller than the error related to the OMA methods since no signiﬁcant
trends can be found when varying the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation settings.
4.8.5
Discussion and conclusion of OMA analysis at 1000 rpm
This section will discuss and conclude upon results obtained from OMA analysis at 1000
rpm.
The above analysis has greatly exploited sorting the modes according to EMA estimates.
This sorting improves the OMA methods by removing all spurious poles without any
signiﬁcant premise for doing so. However, in real-world scenarios where prior knowledge
of modal parameters is unknown, such sorting is not feasible. Nevertheless, alternative
approaches can be used to determine the position of physical modes by examining con-
centrations of modal parameters across tests. Stabilization diagrams, a widely recognized
method in modal testing are exactly employed for this purpose. By comparing results
from multiple tests or varying input parameters, clusters of physical poles can naturally
emerge, while spurious and mathematical poles tend to appear randomly in the spectrum.
Thus, sorting model parameters into modes based on these modal parameter clusters is
feasible even without prior knowledge or EMA estimates. It is also worth noting that
prior knowledge is often available in SHM and CM situations, further enhancing the
applicability of such sorting techniques.
Five diﬀerent OMA methods have been employed and signiﬁcant diﬀerences are found
in the quality of their modal estimates. A conclusion of the diﬀerent OMA methods is
possible considering their proﬁciency in analyzing the speciﬁc APTJB systems utilized
during this thesis. It is expected that these conclusions can be extrapolated to other ﬂuid
ﬁlm rotor-bearing systems of similar damping levels, and bearing properties.
The FDD performs the worst among the ﬁve OMA methods. Its estimates and resulting
model ﬁts are poor. It was concluded that the method performed poorly due to a heavily
damped and coupled system, which made it hard for the modal ﬁlter to properly decom-
pose the unique modes. The number of uncorrelated inputs was also believed to remove
the advantage of naturally separating noise modes, hence the measurement noise instead
acts as a bias on the estimated modal parameters.
The ITD was deemed inappropriate considering the number of sensors compared to the
desired number of estimated modes (both 10) since a requirement of noise modeling will
yield signiﬁcantly fewer physical modes than desired. However, the method can be used
to give an initial picture of the most prominent modes of the system, although these are
expected to be biased by other coupled modes and measurement noise.
99
==PAGE:111==
4.8. OMA ANALYSIS AT 1000 RPM
The A-PR provided many spurious and scattered modal parameters not related to the
physical dynamics of the system. Its results were therefore hard to analyze and sort in
terms of physical poles.
The A-SSI method performed well, although a higher model order requirement led to a
notable presence of spurious poles. Nevertheless, the modal estimates obtained through
A-SSI were found to be consistent and compare well to other methods.
The A-ERA was considered to perform best among the OMA methods. It generated
clear stabilization diagrams while also yielding consistent estimates compared to other
methods. The ERA and SSI are very similar in their mathematical formulation and the
advantage of ERA over SSI can be attributed to its use of processed CFs, that have
undergone prior noise removal through the elimination of initial time lags and noise tails.
The AC level (σSV ) naturally correlates (positively) to the vibration amplitude of the
rotor. Also when increasing the AC level, modal parameters seemed to converge and be-
come more consistent across OMA methods and perturbation settings. It was suggested
that an AC level above σSV = 0.7V should be used to obtain a satisfactory signal-to-noise
ratio. Moreover, the maximum analyzed AC level of σSV = 1V was found to provide
the most reliable estimates. An AC level of σSV = 1V means that the servo valve sig-
nal will ﬂuctuate within ±3V from its DC value, eﬀectively spanning the entire signal
range. Consequently, the servo valve spool will exhibit random displacements, alternat-
ing between its extreme positions leading to maximum changes of in-ﬂow and back-ﬂow
through both ports. Changing the AC level showed no signiﬁcant eﬀect on the estimated
modal parameters, and it was generally considered that the uncertainty obtained from
the OMA estimates was larger than the eﬀect of changing the AC level. This was con-
cluded from the fact that diﬀerent perturbation settings did not yield similar trends for
increasing AC levels. For σSV = 1 V the rotor moved at a maximum displacement of
±14 µm being approximately 17% of the radial clearance. Considering that the AC level
does not have an apparent eﬀect on the modal parameters, amplitude variations within
17% are considered to be non-invasive. Andréas and Santiago also conclude in [50] that
linear journal bearing coeﬃcients are still applicable, hence also modal parameters, for
amplitude variations within 50% of the radial clearance, backing up this conclusion.
The supply pressure ps,HP also correlated with the response amplitude. It was found
that using a small supply pressure of 12 and 20 bar minimized the amplitude discrepancies
in FRFs with respect to passive tests. Furthermore, these pressure levels still enabled
signiﬁcant rotor displacement and maintained a satisfactory signal-to-noise ratio during
ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation. And using ps,HP at levels of 12 and 20 bar was found to have no
clear eﬀect on the modal estimates for using both OMA and EMA methods.
Considering the DC level perturbation settings CS and PE perturbation, it was observed
that the passive equilibrium setting yielded an FRF amplitude closer to the passive FRF.
There were also found no signiﬁcant diﬀerences in the estimated modal parameters by
OMA using the two settings. However, the CS perturbation showed a slight advantage in
comparing modal estimates across tests and estimation methods, arguing that the result-
ing modal parameters are more accurate. This can be attributed to the larger response
amplitude achieved with CS perturbation, resulting in a better signal-to-noise ratio. Ad-
ditionally, signal distributions for CS perturbation were closer to Gaussian distributions,
better aligning with OMA assumptions. In contrast, the data from PE perturbation did
not follow a normal distribution for high AC levels, as it was aﬀected by the saturation
100
==PAGE:112==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
of rotor displacement versus servo input, resulting in a signiﬁcantly skewed distribution.
Certain modes, particularly those with high response contribution, were consistently es-
timated more accurately across methods. In contrast, modes that had low response con-
tributions exhibited inconsistent estimations.
While the inability to obtain consistent
estimates for these modes is not ideal, it is worth noting that low participation modes
are often of minimal interest in CM and SHM scenarios, as they typically have minimal
impact on the machine’s operating condition.
A general source of error related to all OMA identiﬁcation methods is a possible reduction
of measurement rank by only having two uncorrelated inputs. And it is hence expected
that all OMA estimates will be biased to some degree by the measurement noise especially
considering closely spaced resonances.
Comparing OMA results obtained with σSV = 1 V to EMA estimates yields good agree-
ment between mode shapes and natural frequencies within 3%, however, indications of
slightly increased damping ratios are seen up to 25% deviation. However, the expected
bias of EMA estimates prevents a clear conclusion considering the value of the true (pas-
sive) modal parameters.
To summarize, it is indicated that by selecting appropriate ﬂuid-ﬁlm perturbation param-
eters, non-invasive estimation of the system’s modal parameters is likely to be possible
within the estimation uncertainty related to the OMA methods of the natural frequencies
and mode shapes. However, damping levels are expected to be slightly increased. Pertur-
bation at the passive equilibrium is likely the best choice for achieving non-invasiveness in
terms of load capacity. However, slightly improved perturbation conditions are observed
when the servo valve spool is centered, with no noticeable change in model parameters.
4.9
OMA analysis at 7000 rpm
4.9.1
Eﬀects of high-speed testing
Figure 4.33:
Rotor posi-
tion and servo valve input
at 7000 rpm.
Before starting the main OMA analysis at 7000 rpm some
high-speed eﬀects must be highlighted.
As previously ob-
served in section 4.4, at 5000 rpm it becomes impossible to
push the rotor back to its passive equilibrium position for
supply pressures below 20 bar. At 7000 rpm this eﬀect is
further pronounced, and a few tests illustrating this obser-
vation are shown in Figure 4.33.
Tests are performed at
ps,HP = {12, 20, 40, 60, 80} bar with two servo valve inputs:
at the spool center (CS), and using a servo valve input, min-
imizing the distance to the passive equilibrium of the rotor.
It is observed that for CS inputs, the rotor position in the Z-
direction increases with an asymptotic trend correlated pos-
itively to the supply pressure.
This phenomenon is likely
caused by an increased oil ﬂow through the bearing that will
increase the oil ﬁlm thickness, but stagnate when the rotor
approaches the bearing center. When trying to position the
rotor in the passive equilibrium it is clear that with higher
supply pressure one is able to move the bearing an increasing distance from the CS po-
sition. And only with 80 bar is it possible to get close to the passive equilibrium. From
101
==PAGE:113==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
these insights, it is concluded that performing passive equilibrium (PE) perturbation at
7000 rpm with low supply pressure is not possible, and this test will therefore be excluded
from the 7000 rpm test campaign.
4.9.2
Test plan
A test plan is formulated with the objective of analyzing the eﬀect of supply pressure ps,HP
and AC-level σSV . In contrast to the 1000 rpm tests, a wider range of supply pressure
will be employed. This adjustment is motivated by the observation that higher pressure is
required to induce signiﬁcant rotor displacement, suggesting the need for increased supply
pressure. Conversely, it is observed from section 4.1 that the two dominating frequencies
around 60 and 70 Hz are naturally excited during passive conditions above 5000 rpm.
Therefore, tests are also performed simply by measuring the vibrations induced during
hydrodynamic passive lubrication. To ensure repeatability, a total of four passive tests
are performed in between ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation tests.
The DC-level is there adjusted to the CS input of the spool at SVCS = {2.6, 2.0} V for all
tests, despite the likelihood of variation in the CS input at high supply pressures (section
4.4).
The test plan is summarized in Table 4.8.

In terms of measurement duration, damping levels are slightly lower for 7000 rpm (1-2%
at 27Hz) increasing the ANSI S2.47 standard above 240 sec. However, 240 sec is still
more than enough to satisfy the maximum correlation time requirement stated in [27].
Nevertheless, the measurement duration is kept at 240 sec throughout all tests.
4.9.3
Preliminary signal analysis
Preliminary signal analysis is again performed to analyze the satisfaction of OMA as-
sumptions and the quality of the vibration measurements made during both ﬂuid ﬁlm
perturbation and passive tests.
Examples of signal histograms for ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation and all passive tests are shown
in Figure 4.34 for sensor 4 signals. Histograms of remaining tests for sensors 3 and 4
are shown in Appendix H.4.1. It is observed that the acquired signals during ﬂuid ﬁlm
perturbation closely resemble normal distributions, evidenced by the straight lines in
the normal probability plot. At 7000 rpm, the signal distributions are more Gaussian
compared to 1000 rpm tests, as expected due to the lower damping levels (central limit
theorem). Additionally, it is noted that the distribution width remains similar for diﬀerent
102
==PAGE:114==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
supply pressure levels, with a slight reduction observed at 12 bar.
However, further
analysis of the histograms in Appendix H.4.1 reveals that the 12 bar test exhibits a
wider distribution for low AC levels, potentially indicating increased damping associated
with higher pressures. Analyzing the passive test data, it is again seen that the signals
closely resemble normal distributions. A slight variation is found in the histograms where
test 1 varies slightly from the remaining tests. This variation could be explained by a
temperature variation, as noted in the ﬁgure legend where test 1 has been performed
at lower oil temperatures.
Other sources that might cause test-to-test variations are;
diﬀerent run-out compensations, shaft temperature, or other irregularities of pumps and
motors.

To further study the time and frequency content of the measured signals the singular
values of the spectral matrix, correlation functions, and power spectrum are shown for
representative tests in Figure 4.35. Only a single passive test is shown since the remaining
tests are close to identical.

Figure 4.35: Singular values, correlation functions, and power spectral densities of exem-

First, it is highlighted from Figures 4.35 (a) and (d) that 2 singular value curves are
seen to rise above the noise ﬂoor, indicating that the measurement system is of rank 2.
Although the third singular value does exhibit minor ampliﬁcations around 27 and 60 Hz,
which are likely indications of resonances or improved excitation conditions. Interestingly,
it is also observed that the passive test shows the same amount of rising singular values,
indicating that the system is excited similarly even without external perturbation. It
is also observed that the singular value peaks are signiﬁcantly sharper for the passive
test compared to the perturbation tests, again showing that the damping is signiﬁcantly
altered using ﬂuid perturbation. An ampliﬁcation peak is observed in the singular value
plots around 117 Hz. This frequency component is equal to the 1Ωsynchronous vibrations
and is likely present due to diﬃculties of performing a perfect run-out at 7000 rpm, where
speed variations are likely to occur.
By analysis of the correlation functions in Figures 4.35 (b) and (e) their diﬀerence to the
1000 rpm tests in Figure 4.23 is clearly noticed. At 7000 rpm the correlation functions
clearly show signs of a less damped system with longer decay times. Perturbation tests
have approximately decayed to a constant amplitude after 60 time lags and for passive
tests approximately after 100-time lags. The constant amplitude after the initial decay
is generally representing small amplitude noise components in the signals, hence it is
indicated that the noise sources of the perturbation signals are generally smaller compared
to the passive test.
By further analysis of the power spectrum plotted in linear scale in Figures 4.35 (c) and
(f), it is clearly observed that almost no response amplitude is obtained above 100 Hz
for both perturbation and passive tests, hence it is expected that physical dynamics will
be hard to estimate above this frequency. It is also noted from Figure 4.35f that the
passive response peak around 52 Hz has moved to slightly higher frequencies compared to
104
==PAGE:116==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
perturbation tests, indicating that the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation might also alter the natural
frequencies of the system.
4.9.4
Data processing and OMA setup
The setup of OMA methods follows a very similar approach to the one described for 1000
rpm in section 4.8.3. Again the ITD, A-SSI, A-ERA, A-PR, and FDD will be used for
all tests. All data processing and OMA setup parameters used for the perturbation and
passive tests are given in Appendix H.4.2. A summation of the most signiﬁcant choices
is given here.
All signals are sampled at fs = 3000 Hz and have been at-speed run-
out compensated to remove run-out noise and harmonic synchronous vibrations. Signals
have been low pass ﬁltered and decimated 6 times to a sampling rate of fs = 500 Hz.
Correlation functions of 60 and 100 time lags are used for perturbation and passive tests
respectively. The increased number of time lags for the passive tests is attributed to the
apparent decrease in damping. Fit points for the FDD are chosen by picking prominent
peaks in the SVD plot of the spectral matrix. Peaks are found at approximately 27, 54,
68, and 72 Hz.
The number of returned clusters from the AOMA methods is Nclust = 7 and Nclust = 5
for perturbation and passive tests respectively. Higher for perturbation data due to an
increased amount of excited modes. To simplify the analysis clusters are only extracted
in the frequency band [20,80] Hz where the most signiﬁcant dynamics are observed. The
order range for the AOMA stabilization diagram has generally been reduced compared to
1000 rpm since the number of dominant modes has decreased. Examples of stabilization
diagrams for both perturbation and passive tests are shown in Figure 4.36. Poles in the
grey region with low response amplitude are excluded during clustering. The grey ranges
are simply depicted for completeness and reproducibility.
(a) SSI n ∈[2, 100]
(b) ERA n ∈[4, 80]
(c) PR na ∈[1, 30]
(d) SSI n ∈[2, 100]
(e) ERA n ∈[4, 40]
(f) PR na ∈[1, 20]
Figure 4.36: Stabilization diagrams from SSI, ERA, and PR methods plotted on top the
singular values of the spectral matrix σ(G). Perturbation results are shown in (a), (b),
and (c) for ps,HP = 40 bar, and σSV = 0.5V. Passive results in (d), (e), and (f) for test 2.
105
==PAGE:117==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
By analysis of SSI and ERA results, in Figures 4.36 (a), (b), (d), and (e) ﬁve stabilized
frequencies are identiﬁable below 80 Hz at approximately 27, 54, 59, 68, and 72 Hz for both
perturbation and passive results. Where the 54 and 72 Hz components clearly align with
peaks of the singular values as clear indications of physical modes. Stabilization diagrams
for SSI and ERA of perturbation results in Figure 4.36 (a) and (b) show signiﬁcantly faster
stabilization compared to 1000 rpm (Figure 4.25). SSI and ERA stabilization diagrams for
the passive results Figure 4.36 (d) and (e) are further simpliﬁed. Especially for the ERA
method, the maximum model order can be reduced to 40 while good mode stabilization
is still obtained. The PR stabilization diagrams in Figure 4.36 (c) and (f) exhibit poor
stabilization, as also observed for the 1000 rpm tests.
4.9.5
Results
The results obtained at 7000 rpm using the OMA methods ITD, A-SSI, A-ERA, A-PR,
and FDD will now be presented.
The OMA methods have been used for the 24 and 4 ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation and passive
tests respectively. To gain an overview of the estimated natural frequencies, the resulting
undamped natural frequencies fn are presented in Figure 4.37a. The marker type and
color indicate the pressure and AC level respectively whole diﬀerent OMA methods are
distributed on the y-axis and further subdivided into test types (pressure levels). Passive
results are indicated by green stars while EMA results are indicated by black lines.
The undamped natural frequencies are clearly clustered across methods and test settings
at frequencies 27, 54, 58, 67, and 72 Hz. From EMA only the 54, 67, and 72 Hz components
are estimated which is likely a result of the single excitation direction. In comparison to
1000 rpm results (Figure 4.27) natural frequencies are seemingly better separated with
fewer scattered spurious poles. The A-SSI method singularly predicts modes around 36
Hz, which is likely a weakly excited mode and will not be considered in the upcoming
discussions.
It is clear that the ﬁrst-bending foundation mode at 27 Hz is consistently predicted across
all methods and test setups, showing that it is not aﬀected by the perturbation param-
eters. Other modal clusters are seemingly more scattered across diﬀerent perturbation
parameters. While the passive estimates are relatively consistent as expected since no
changes are made across the 4 performed tests. For the mode at 72 Hz it is clear that
the passive test estimate is systematically increased compared to perturbation estimates.
Note also that a systematic variation between diﬀerent supply pressures ps,HP can be
observed, see e.g. the mode at 67 Hz for the A-SSI where the 12 bar test seems to yield
a lower natural frequency compared to higher supply pressures.
Similar to the 1000 rpm case the predicted poles are sorted into separate modes. They
are sorted by using the approximate mean frequency of the 5 modes at (27, 54, 58, 67,
and 72Hz) across methods and test settings as the reference. The modal sorting allows
plots of the damping ratio for every mode as shown in Figure 4.37b.
106
==PAGE:118==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
(a) Undamped natural frequencies
(b) Damping ratios
Figure 4.37: Results from all tests and OMA methods. (a): undamped natural frequen-
cies. (b): damping ratios of sorted modes. The ﬁgure legend applies to both (a) and
(b).
Upon analyzing the damping estimates, it becomes apparent that modes 2 (54 Hz) and 5
(72 Hz) exhibit signiﬁcantly lower damping when estimated using the passive tests com-
pared to the perturbation tests. This observation aligns with the signal property plots
in Figure 4.35, where these modes dominate the passive test with notably lower damping
levels. It is also noted that the EMA estimate (passive) does not seem to correlate well
with the OMA passive tests.
Analyzing the diﬀerent perturbation settings it is noticed that the 12-bar tests (repre-
sented by crosses) are often closer to the passive estimates and exhibit greater concen-
tration across AC levels. See e.g. mode 2 where the damping appears to increase with
higher supply pressures.
The performance of every OMA method will now be analyzed one by one considering the
plots of the undamped natural frequency and damping ratio in Figure 4.37:
107
==PAGE:119==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
ITD:
The ITD does not predict the foundation mode at 27 Hz, while predictions of the
remaining modes seem to be adequate for ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation tests. It does however
show the largest spread of modal parameters across tests compared to the remaining OMA
methods. Using passive tests consistent estimates are obtained for modes 2 and 5 while
other modes are hardly found. Even though this method performs the worst, it should be
noted that it is by far the most easy to use and fastest to run.
A-SSI:
The A-SSI shows estimates at all observed modes including a weakly present
mode at 36 Hz. The extra mode is likely observed due to the increased model order
size compared to other methods. Passive estimates are also predicted rather consistent
although only 2/4 tests ﬁnd the mode at 27 Hz.
A-ERA:
The A-ERA estimates are similar to the A-SSI, predicting all modes and
showing consistent modal parameter patterns across tests with a minimal number of
spurious poles.
A-PR:
The A-PR also shows quite similar results as to the A-SSI and A-ERA, which
is rather unexpected considering its poor stabilization diagram. It does however show a
much larger spread of fn around 54-58 Hz.
FDD:
The FDD is the only frequency domain technique and relies on a frequency-
picking procedure hence the prediction of mode 3 at 58 Hz is not predicted since no clear
peak was found and chosen in the SVD plots. It’s estimates are very consistent across
perturbation settings. This is not necessarily a good characteristic since modal changes
caused by diﬀerent perturbation settings are not able to be observed. Although small
diﬀerences are seen considering the comparison of passive and perturbation results, espe-
cially for mode 2. Damping estimates of mode 4 and 5 agrees very well with passive results
but is signiﬁcantly lower than estimates of other OMA methods indicating a possible bias.
MAC analysis
To further enrich the modal analysis and gain knowledge of the diﬀerences between,
perturbation settings, passive tests, and EMA tests the modal estimates are compared
using the MACXP assurance criteria. First perturbation and passive results are compared
individually between themselves in Figure 4.39. A detailed explanation of both MACXP
axes is given in Figure 4.38 showing that the perturbation plot axis is ﬁrst split into
methods at every 24th tile and further subdivided into pressures at every 6th tile and
lastly into the AC levels.
Similar divisions are made for the passive results but only
separated into methods every 4th tile and further into the passive tests 1..4. If an estimate
is missing for a speciﬁc method-test combination those tiles are given the color grey.
First analyzing the MACXP plots for perturbation results in Figure 4.39a it is generally
observed across all modes that estimates from A-SSI and A-ERA match fairly well, while
FDD results often deviate signiﬁcantly from the remaining methods. The ITD method
estimates modes quite similar to other OMA methods however it often lacks prediction of
modes completely. The PR method performs fairly well with good agreement to the other
OMA methods but with more lacking estimates. A noteworthy pattern is also noticed
considering the comparison of A-SSI and A-ERA modes. Using mode 4 as an example
it is noticed that modes compare individually well for 12-bar (small square collection of
108
==PAGE:120==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
red tiles) and again for higher pressures at 40, 60, and 80 bar (bigger square collection of
red tiles). However, the comparison between the 12 bar and higher pressure estimates is
reduced. The pattern is also visible for modes 2 and 5 but is of lesser signiﬁcance. This
pattern indicates that the modal estimates change when changing the pressure from 12
to 40 bar, but less when further increased from 40 bar.

(b) Passive results

bation plots.

Figure 4.39: MACXP plots of perturbation (a) and passive results (b).
Now analyzing the passive results in Figure 4.39b, where it is important to highlight
that all tests should ideally match perfectly since tests are performed under the same
conditions.
It is observed that the A-SSI and A-ERA estimates compare well for all
modes while the ITD, PR, and FDD often compare less or completely lack estimation. It
is generally assumed that when a mode compares well across diﬀerent estimation methods
109
==PAGE:121==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
that mode is estimated with high accuracy, such assumption is normally accepted as
discussed by Paulsen in [31]. And by this assumption, it can be concluded that modes
2 and 5 are estimated with the highest accuracy, while modes 1, 3, and 4 are of lesser
accuracy. This is also expected analyzing the SVD plots of Figure 4.35 where modes 2
and 5 are clearly dominant while the remaining modes lack ampliﬁcation.
The perturbation and passive results are now compared to each other by MACXP and
MACX in Figure 4.40. The plot axis division follows a combination of the explanation in
Figure 4.38.

==PAGE:122==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
Analyzing the MACXP comparison in Figure 4.40a, apart from the FDD, it is seen that
modes 2 and 5 show poor comparison across perturbation and passive results. Indicating
that a signiﬁcant diﬀerence is found in modal parameters of modes 2 and 5 between
passive and perturbation tests. The perturbation FDD estimates of modes 2 and 5 show
a good comparison to ITD, A-SSI, and A-ERA passive results which might be induced by
the speciﬁc frequency picked points, although no clear explanation can be given. It is also
noticed that modes 1, 3, and 4 show better comparison between perturbation and passive
results indicating that these modes are less aﬀected by the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation.
It is also noticed that the 12-bar perturbation tests generally give slightly increased
MACXP across all modes.9
This is expected since lowering the supply pressure will
in the limit lead to passive lubrication.
Finally, upon comparing the MACXP and MACX plots from ﬁgure 4.40, it becomes
evident that the MACX plot exhibits a much better comparison between the perturbation
and passive results. This indicates that the mode shapes remain relatively unchanged
between the two test types, while the poles (natural frequency and damping) display
signiﬁcant variations between the perturbation and passive tests.
Optimal analysis method and test settings
If the aim is to perform non-invasive testing the passive tests are to be desired since no
external perturbation of the system is required. However, the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation is
still important to analyse for cases where passive excitation is not present. Hence the
analysis will continue by focusing on the best OMA and perturbation methods. Based
on previous discussions, it is observed that the A-SSI and A-ERA provide the most
consistent estimates across the modes, while the 12-bar tests show the closest comparison
to the passive tests. To further investigate the results, the undamped natural frequencies
(fn) and damping ratios (ζn) are plotted against the AC level using the A-SSI and A-ERA
in Figure 4.41. All pressure levels are included, but the 12 bar estimates are highlighted
with a black background. The passive results have been averaged across repeated tests
and shown with green lines. Only the A-SSI and A-ERA are used for the average which
is supported by their good comparison in the MACXP plots (Figure 4.39). The fn plots
show a ±5% interval of the passive estimate to establish a common sense of deviation
across modes.
Opposite to the 1000 rpm tests, the AC level does not appear to have a converging eﬀect
on the modal estimates. However small trends are found for varying AC levels, see. e.g.
the plot for ζ1 where the damping correlates positively with the AC level. Also for f2 a
small negative correlation with the AC level is found. However, in the general picture,
no clear trends can be established across modes. It can be concluded that all natural
frequencies are predicted within 5% of the passive estimates and even better for most
cases. Note also that the 12 bar estimates are often closest to the passive estimates,
especially considering the damping ratios.
912 bar tests are found right after every method marker on the y-axis.

==PAGE:123==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
Figure 4.41: Undamped natural frequencies fn and damping ratios ζn versus the AC-level
Analysing the available EMA results they deviate slightly from the passive estimates.
This deviation is likely caused by the shaker interaction. No clear softening or stiﬀening
trend is seen across modes hence it is hard to establish any signiﬁcant causes for fn
deviation. However, the EMA damping estimate is consistently higher than the passive
OMA estimates, indicating that the damping of the system is increased, as normally
expected using shaker excitation.
Modes 2 and 5 have been highlighted since these are the dominating modes of response at
7000 rpm. These modes also yield the smallest standard deviation of the passive estimates,
shown by thin green bands of these modal estimates in Figure 4.41. Whereas modes 1
and 3 yield higher standard deviations as expected since these are lesser present in the
data. The mode shapes of modes 2 and 5 are plotted for the average passive estimate
in Figure 4.42. As previously discussed and stated by Wagner in [9] these modes are
expected to belong to the second torsional foundation mode and ﬁrst backward bending
mode of the shaft. From Figure 4.42 no clear distinction can be made from the overall
shape of the orbit, however, the orbit direction is clearly backward (opposite the shaft
spin Ω) for mode 5 supporting the theory of a backward mode. The reason for similar
orbit proﬁles is likely caused by the coupled sensor-foundation movement.
