4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
4.9.6
Discussion and conclusion of OMA analysis at 7000 rpm
This section will discuss and conclude upon results obtained from the OMA analysis
performed at 7000 rpm.
Initially, it is essential to highlight the vital role of the at-speed run-out compensation.
This signal-processing technique forms the backbone of the rotor system analysis. ef-
fectively mitigating vibrations correlated to the rotors angular position. Without this
compensation, the the response becomes dominated by synchronous vibrations, posing
challenges to the estimation of unbiased system modes.
When running the APTJB system with passive lubrication at angular velocities above
5000 rpm it was shown that a natural excitation of the system existed. The excitation
is likely to arise from a marginal stability state of the machine, unbalance excitation, or
ﬂuid nonlinearities such as turbulence or two-phase ﬂow. It was shown that this excita-
tion provided a Gaussian response of the system that was successfully used as input for
OMA methods to estimate modal parameters. These modal parameters were assumed to
represent the ideal parameters of a non-invasive test, and hence used as benchmark pa-
rameters for both EMA results obtained at passive conditions and OMA results obtained
using ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation.
EMA tests were only performed using horizontal excitation due to poor coherence using
other excitation directions. Knowing that the system is passively excited explains the
reduced coherence, as the passive excitation acts as an unknown disturbance during an
EMA test. The EMA p-LSCF results did estimate 3 out of 5 modes with a good compar-
ison of natural frequencies within 2% deviation to passive OMA results. Damping ratios
were found to be increased compared to passive OMA tests up to 270%, attributed to
shaker interaction. Mode shapes were also found to be biased from the excitation show-
ing a dominating horizontal movement compared to OMA counterparts. A mode shape
bias is expected since the EMA tests are performed as SIMO experiments which disables
the ability to estimate bias-free mode shapes considering rotordynamic systems that are
generally non-self-adjoint. On the other hand from OMA testing, at least 2 uncorrelated
inputs have been applied, and the non-self-adjoint system modes are likely to be better
captured.
Fluid ﬁlm perturbation was performed using a centered spool at various supply pres-
sures (ps,HP = 12, 40, 60, 80 bar). Higher supply pressures were observed to increase the
overall response amplitude without a signiﬁcant impact on the natural frequencies but
with increased damping. Among these pressures, using ps,HP = 12 bar yielded the closest
estimates compared to passive tests.
The AC level σSV was observed to reveal small trends upon certain modal parameters
although no general conclusion could be drawn across modes. In contrast to the 1000 rpm
tests, the AC level did not provide a converging nature upon the modal estimates, and in
general, its inﬂuence was found insigniﬁcant. This is likely a result of the natural passive
excitation which will superimpose upon the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation, reducing the impact
of the perturbation amplitude (AC level).
The FDD method did not reveal signiﬁcant variations in modal parameters across dif-
ferent perturbation settings. One possible reason for this could be the use of the same
frequency picking across all tests. Damping estimates were signiﬁcantly smaller across all
115
==PAGE:127==
4.9. OMA ANALYSIS AT 7000 RPM
modes compared to other OMA methods, indicating a possible bias. Mode shapes were
also found to diﬀer as expected from a known mode shape complexity bias of the FDD.
The ITD performed signiﬁcantly better at 7000 rpm compared to the 1000 rpm tests.
This is likely explained by having fewer dominant and less damped modes in the fre-
quency range of interest. High participation modes 2 and 5 were consistently estimated
using passive excitation, while estimates of modes 1, 3, and 4 were lacking. For the ﬂuid
ﬁlm perturbation tests, modal parameters were estimated with the largest uncorrelated
spread compared to other OMA methods.
The A-PR method inconsistently yielded estimates of all 5 modal parameters. Its stabi-
lization diagrams showed no clear sign of stabilization, and even though decent estimates
were obtained, the A-PR method is not considered to be appropriate for automation
through stabilization diagrams. Its estimates are likely to gain their quality through hier-
archical clustering. Accuracy parameters of the PR method also had to be set signiﬁcantly
lower compared to A-SSI and A-ERA, generally decreasing its trustworthiness.
The A-SSI and A-ERA found all modes of interest using both perturbation and passive
tests. Both methods compared very well across diﬀerent tests and it was generally ac-
knowledged that these methods provided the most accurate modal parameters. It should
however be mentioned that the SSI and ERA are very similar in their mathematical
formulation which might increase their similarity without indicating increased accuracy.
Comparing passive estimates to the A-SSI and A-ERA results using ps,HP = 12 bar ﬂuid
ﬁlm perturbation bar showed a good estimation of undamped natural frequencies within
3%, while the damping ratios were increased by up to 300% for high participation modes 2
and 5. Low participation modes 1, 3, and 5 were predicted with better damping up to 70%
deviation. A high mode shape agreement was found considering the high participation
modes 2 and 5 across all OMA methods indicating good estimation accuracy.
From the above insights, some pros and cons of the perturbation methods can be given.
The ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation excited more modes in the frequency range of interest, making
it easier to obtain multiple modal estimates. In contrast, using only natural excitation
posed challenges in accurately estimating low participation modes.
The advantage of
utilizing natural passive excitation lies in the preservation of unaltered modal parame-
ters, ensuring non-invasive testing. However, when testing machinery operating under
smoother conditions, the ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation is likely to be necessary, as observed
during 1000 rpm testing.
In conclusion, non-invasive rotor-dynamic testing is possible at high speeds by utilizing
naturally induced excitation. External excitation through ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation results
in minimal deviation of natural frequencies and mode shapes, while damping ratios, par-
ticularly for the dominating modes of the system, are signiﬁcantly increased.
116
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 5
Conclusion
The main objective of this Master’s thesis was to investigate the possibility of combining
controllable lubrication techniques with operational modal analysis, and the computa-
tional tools for signal processing, to track changes in damping ratios, natural frequencies,
and mode shapes of a rotor-bearing-foundation test rig with an active tilting pad journal
bearing.
Due to a prior test rig breakdown, the rotor-bearing-foundation test rig was reassembled
to replace and repair worn-out parts including, new tilting pads, re-polished rotor, new
low-pressure oil injection nozzles, and new oil ﬁlters.
A theoretical basis for performing operational and experimental modal analysis was stud-
ied and implemented.
Five OMA methods were utilized, namely the Ibrahim Time
Domain (ITD), data-driven Stochastic Subspace Identiﬁcation (SSI), Eigensystem Re-
alization Algorithm (ERA), Poly Reference (PR), and the enhanced Frequency Domain
Decomposition (FDD). Aiming to automate the modal estimation procedures, an auto-
mated OMA (AOMA) routine was proposed and implemented using the SSI, ERA, and
PR methods. In terms of EMA methods, the Poly reference Least Squares Complex Fre-
quency method (p-LSCF), also known as PolyMAX, was studied and implemented using
a semi-automated approach.
An initial study of the bearing showed that the newly installed low-pressure injection
nozzles removed starvation problems at higher speeds. The relationship between rotor
position, servo valve input, and supply pressure was experimentally documented in re-
lation to the passive equilibrium position at 1000 and 5000 rpm. It was found that for
increasing rotational speed the servo valve input had a lesser eﬀect upon the rotor due to
increased ﬂuid ﬁlm forces. This analysis also yielded estimates of the servo valve input
required to center the servo valve spools of 2.6 and 2.0 V for servo 1 and 2 respectively,
aligning well with previous ﬂow experiments. The unbalance response of the rotor was
also studied. It was found that the 1Ωunbalance vibrations were dominating with the
existence of both sub- and super-synchronous vibrations. At the same time, it was docu-
mented how the rotor was naturally excited at high angular velocities above 5000 rpm, and
that the at-speed run-out compensation eﬀectively eliminated the harmonic synchronous
vibrations from the measured signals.
EMA analysis of the rotor-bearing-foundation system was performed at 1000 and 7000
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
117
==PAGE:129==
rpm. Modal estimates were obtained for both speeds and the mode shapes were catego-
rized for the 1000 rpm case. It was discussed how the foundation-sensor coupling made
it hard to categorize the obtained mode shapes and that the EMA estimates were likely
biased by shaker interaction. An FRF study was conducted for rotational speeds ranging
from 1000 to 7000 rpm. Notably, two modes increased signiﬁcantly in amplitude at high
speeds, corresponding to the second torsional mode of the foundation and the ﬁrst back-
ward rotor mode. An EMA study was then performed aiming to analyze how the active
hydrostatic lubrication aﬀected the response and modal parameters of the system at 1000
rpm. It was concluded that the natural frequencies remained unchanged while damping
ratios and response amplitude varied slightly.
Two main ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation strategies, namely centered spool (CS) and passive
equilibrium (PE) perturbation, were proposed. An experimental campaign was conducted
at 1000 and 7000 rpm, where the supply pressure and the AC and DC levels of the
perturbation signal were varied. The obtained data was analyzed using all OMA and
AOMA methods.
At 1000 rpm, it was observed that increasing the AC level signiﬁcantly improved the
signal-to-noise ratio, resulting in more consistent estimates of modal parameters. Using
the maximum AC level of σSV = 1 V was most beneﬁcial, which perturbed the rotor
within 17% of its radial clearance. The supply pressure was tested at 12 and 20 bar with
no observable diﬀerence in modal parameters. The DC level (CS or PE) did not have a
signiﬁcant eﬀect on the estimated modal parameters, although it was suggested that using
PE perturbation was optimal in terms of load capacity, while CS perturbation improved
signal quality for OMA analysis. It was found that the A-SSI and A-ERA provided the
most consistent estimates, while the A-ERA was deemed advantageous due to its use of
processed correlation functions. Comparing the modal parameters from A-SSI and A-ERA
with an AC level of 1V to EMA estimates, it was found that undamped natural frequencies
deviated within 1% and that the corresponding mode shapes exhibited high similarity.
Damping ratios were slightly higher than EMA estimates, reaching a maximum deviation
of 25%. In conclusion, non-invasive testing at 1000 rpm was feasible in terms of natural
frequencies and mode shapes within the estimation errors of the OMA methods. However,
due to the anticipated bias of the EMA estimates, a deﬁnitive conclusion regarding the
true passive modal parameters of the system could not be reached.
At 7000 rpm, PE perturbation was no longer possible, hence only CS perturbation was
utilized. At this speed, a natural passive excitation of the system existed and was found
to be applicable for OMA analysis. With respect to ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation, the sup-
ply pressure was tested at 12, 40, 60, and 80 bar showing a correlation with increased
damping ratios. Among these options, 12 bar proved to be the least invasive. The AC
level had minimal inﬂuence on the modal estimates, possibly attributed to the presence
of the natural excitation. Again, the A-SSI and A-ERA were deemed the most proﬁcient
estimation methods, although lower damping levels and less complicated system dynam-
ics lead to reasonable estimation from the ITD and FDD also. It was concluded that
complete non-invasive testing was possible by utilizing the natural passive excitation of
the system. These passive estimates were considered good approximations of the true
modal parameters of the system and hence used as benchmark parameters. Fluid ﬁlm
perturbation tests with a supply pressure of 12 bar led to accurate estimates of mode
shapes and natural frequencies within 3% of the passive estimates while damping ratios
118
==PAGE:130==
5.1. CONCLUSION ON ADDITIONAL STUDIES
were consistently elevated with a maximum deviation of 300%.
EMA estimates were
also found to match well in natural frequency although damping ratios and mode shapes
were found to diﬀerentiate signiﬁcantly, attributed to a shaker bias and the assumption
of self-adjointness.
The main advantage of using ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation for online monitoring of rotor-bearing
systems lies in the ease of testing, without signiﬁcantly disturbing the operating condi-
tions. Performing ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation directly through journal bearings, by adding
servo valves, oﬀers a simpler and more cost-eﬀective solution compared to installing AMBs
solely for the purpose of exciting the rotor system. Additionally, the use of AMBs neces-
sitates mounting rotor rings, which further disrupts the rotor dynamics.
Considering the application of OMA, the objective of machine monitoring is among others
to detect instabilities, often linked to reduced damping levels and self-induced vibrations.
This, in turn, signiﬁcantly improves the overall applicability and precision of OMA when
it is needed the most hence increasing its applicability.
The primary drawbacks arise from the inherent damped and coupled nature of rotating
machinery, leading OMA methods to predict numerous spurious and biased poles, which
complicate precise analysis. Additionally, ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbation poses a limitation in
measurement rank when only performed using a single bearing, reducing the ability to
distinguish noise from physical dynamics. Further, if the bearing is positioned in nodes of
critical modes, the perturbation and estimation of these modes become impossible. One
potential solution to address these issues is by employing multiple controllable bearings,
generating uncorrelated perturbations at diﬀerent positions within the system. This ap-
proach enhances the measurement rank and spatial distribution of inputs, providing more
appropriate conditions for OMA analysis.
Finally, to answer the research questions of this Master’s thesis:
RQ1 "Is it possible to produce non-invasive perturbations via an Active Tilting Pad Jour-
nal Bearing (ATPJB) in a rotordynamic test rig?" - There are strong indications
that non-invasive perturbations via an active tilting pad journal bearing are possible
in terms of natural frequencies and mode shapes at 1000 and 7000 rpm. However,
damping levels are expected to increase signiﬁcantly at higher rotational velocities
compared to passive conditions.
RQ2 "Can the non-invasive perturbations be used for output-only modal analysis (OMA)
to obtain consistent estimates of natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode
shapes?" - Yes, when using at-speed run-out compensation the signal obtained from
ﬂuid ﬁlm perturbations can be used for output-only modal analysis (OMA) to obtain
natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode shapes. Moreover, the quality of the
modal estimates is signiﬁcantly increased using enhanced automated OMA methods
such as the implemented A-SSI and A-ERA.
5.1
Conclusion on additional studies
Two comprehensive experimental modal analyses of the test rig foundation and rotor shaft
were also conducted, aiming to provide essential results for ﬁtting future ﬁnite element
models. The foundation analysis was performed in a Multiple Input Multiple Output
119
==PAGE:131==
5.2. FUTURE ASPECTS
(MIMO) setting, with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor, resulting in respec-
tively 11 and 16 modes below 250 Hz. The diﬀerences between the two foundation setups
were discussed, taking into consideration the applicability of the newly developed EMA
p-LSCF method. The experimental rotor shaft analysis was conducted in a Single Input
Multiple Output (SIMO) conﬁguration under approximate free-free conditions. Natural
frequencies, damping ratios, and mode shapes were successfully estimated for diﬀerent
shaft setups, although it was concluded that the estimates were likely biased due to an
accelerometer and shaker bias.
A comparative analysis was also conducted using the previously obtained foundation and
rotor data. By comparing the EMA SDOF approach to the p-LSCF, it was concluded
that the p-LSCF had advantages in capturing damped and coupled dynamics, particularly
when MIMO data was available. OMA methods were also applied to the EMA data. The
ITD and FDD methods were inadequate due to the limited number of sensors.
The
ERA, SSI, and PR methods predicted natural frequencies within 1% of the EMA p-LSCF
estimates, although damping estimates were found to be increased. Furthermore, it was
concluded that the OMA methods performed better using the MIMO foundation data,
where the measurement rank is generally higher compared to the single input rotor data.
5.2
Future aspects
Upon completion of this thesis, areas of potential improvement and further analysis have
been recognized. These can be divided into two categories: Practical aspects and academic
aspects. Listed below are some noteworthy suggestions along with brief explanations.
Academical aspects
• Document the eﬀect of changing the automated OMA input parameters, including
ﬁltering and decimation of signals.
• Conduct experimental analysis on other rotating machinery with the possibility of
hydrostatic controllable lubrication. Especially interesting would be rotor systems
with multiple active journal bearings allowing for improved OMA perturbation con-
ditions.
• Perform statistical tests or use a design of experiments methodology to statistically
determine the signiﬁcance of perturbation parameters.
• Further develop automated OMA routines, applicable in structural health monitor-
ing and condition monitoring situations, and test such routines in real-life scenarios,
trying to track modal parameters over longer time spans.
Practical aspects
• Minimize oil Leakage. In the future, it is recommended to address the issue of oil
leakage through the mechanical seals of the rotor. Suggestions for improvement
include enhancing the existing return line and pump or by reducing the diameter
of the low-pressure injection nozzles.
• Construct a test enabling proper estimation of the assembled radial clearance of the
newly assembled rotor-bearing system. See Salazar [5] for inspiration.
120
==PAGE:132==
5.2. FUTURE ASPECTS
• Mount additional (ﬁve) accelerometers at the displacement sensor pedestals to allow
for separation of foundation and rotor modes.

Appended Publication draft
A Novel Rotordynamic Experimental Testing via Con-

Analysis
This paper is aimed for submission to the journal of Mechanical Systems and Signal

A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
127
==PAGE:139==
A Novel Rotordynamic Experimental Testing via Controllable Lubrication
Techniques and Operational Modal Analysis
Sebastian V. Damsgaarda, Ilmar F. Santosa,∗
aDepartment of Mechanical Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark
Abstract
Flexible rotors – compressors, turbines, pumps, rotary atomizers, etc – supported on fluid film bearings
have their operational conditions limited by the lack of damping and lateral vibration instabilities. The
instability threshold is strongly influenced by the fluid film forces coming from the thin layer of the lubri-
cant as well as seal forces. The changes in natural frequencies and damping ratios of flexible rotors are
strongly depending on the rotor operational conditions, among others the angular velocity and the loading.
Online monitoring of the damping ratios of rotating machines is very much wished for by many industrial
monitoring systems. Output-only Modal Analysis or Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) in comparison
with conventional Experimental Modal Analysis (EMA) relies solely on vibration response measurements
without prior knowledge of excitation forces. A general challenge in the OMA framework is to be sure that
the environmental excitation sources behave as a Gaussian-distributed signal and, thinking of rotordynamic
applications, that unimportant harmonic components are properly eliminated from the rotor lateral vibra-
tion response before such response signals are processed and manipulated by OMA computational tools. The
paper gives an original contribution to the problem of rotordynamic experimental testing, investigating the
possibility of combining controllable lubrication techniques with OMA techniques and their computational
tools for signal processing to track the changes in damping ratio, natural frequencies, and mode shapes which
are depending on the rotor operational conditions. In simple words, a tilting-pad journal bearing is used as
a ”shaker” to dynamically perturb the rotor-bearing system without altering the rotor-bearing equilibrium
position (noninvasive excitation) and aid the estimation of rotor modal parameters under several opera-
tional conditions. The noninvasive fluid film forces are generated via the tilting-pad journal bearing with
radial oil injection controlled by two servovalves. The non-invasivity of such fluid film forces is thoroughly
investigated. The advantages of using such a novel technique are elucidated and thoroughly discussed for
industrial applications.
Keywords:
rotordynamic experimental testing, operational modal analysis, experimental modal analysis,
noninvasive fluid film forces, modal parameter estimation, monitoring of rotor instability
1. Introduction
Conventional machine elements like bearings, brakes, clutches, couplings, and seals among others, are
being transformed into smart mechatronic devices through the integration of sensing and actuation systems,
i.e., electromechanics. Due to the capability of storing a large amount of measurement data from smaller,
cheaper, and more reliable sensors as well as performing faster computational calculations with the data
stored, the use of health condition monitoring techniques in rotor dynamics has been significantly expanded
in the last decades, especially the last one driven by the Industry 4.0 framework.
Active Magnetic Bearings (AMBs) are smart mechatronic devices commonly used in industrial appli-
cations to levitate rotors and control and stabilize their movements, but also to excite and generate rotor
∗Corresponding author
Email addresses: seba98.dam@gmail.com (Sebastian V. Damsgaard), ilsa@dtu.dk (Ilmar F. Santos)
Preprint submitted to Elsevier
July 26, 2023
==PAGE:140==
movements in case of performing rotordynamic testing [1] [2]. In comparison with AMBs, controllable fluid
film bearings (CFFBs) are “young” smart mechatronic devices, designed in a close synergistic relationship
with different types of electromechanical actuators [3] [4]. For example, oil or water-lubricated bearings
are normally connected to servo valves and hydraulic servosystems [5] [6] [7] [8] [9] [10], while gas or air-
lubricated bearings are linked to piezoelectric actuators and pneumatic systems [11] [12] [13]. The fluid film
forces in CFFBs - as the electromagnetic forces in AMBs – can also be used to control as well as excite
rotor vibrations [1] [2] [14]. While the electromagnetic forces result from the dynamic interaction between
the electromagnetic fields of the rotor, the stator, and the coils, the fluid film forces result from the fluid
pressure fields between the rotor and the bearing surface, and they are dictated by the lubrication regimes,
i.e., hydrodynamic, hydrostatic, and controllable, in the case of liquid lubricants, or aerodynamic, aerostatic,
and controllable in the case of gas/air.
AMBs can generate “calibrated” forces to aid rotordynamic testing, taking advantage of their sensing
and actuating systems to control the electromagnetic field and build small perturbation forces. One of the
first contributions to the topic was given by Ulbrich [1] over three decades ago, Nowadays, the performance
of rotordynamic testing via AMBs is a reality for the commissioning of many large industrial re-injection
compressors [15].
CFFBs can also aid the performance of rotordynamic testing if the aerodynamic or
hydrodynamic pressure fields can be influenced and controlled, aiming at building small “noninvasive” per-
turbation forces. The first contribution to the topic was given by Santos and Cerda in [16] [17] about one
decade ago. With “noninvasive” perturbation forces, the authors meant forces capable of perturbing the
rotor-bearing system via fluid film without significantly changing the original static equilibrium position of
the journal, i.e., without altering the rotor-bearing system dynamics [14]. Nevertheless, building a “nonin-
vasive calibrated shaker” via the lubricant film is not a trivial task, once several transfer functions among
mechanical, hydraulic, and electronic components are necessary [16] [17] and the fluid film forces resulting
from the aerodynamic or hydrodynamic pressure fields are strongly dependent on the rotor angular velocity
and the fluid flow regime, in contrast to AMBs and the electromagnetic forces. One way of bypassing the
necessity of knowing all these transfer functions is by using output-only modal analysis (OMA) techniques
[14].
In this framework, the paper gives an original experimental contribution to the field of rotordynamic
testing using a novel noninvasive modal testing procedure that combines controllable lubrication and output-
only modal analysis techniques. A test rig composed of a flexible rotor supported by a controllable tilting-pad
journal bearing is used to validate the novel procedure. Servo valves are used to control the radial oil injection
and the generation of the perturbation fluid film forces.
2. Test Apparatus and Experimental Procedure
Experiments are carried out using a rotor-bearing-foundation test rig located in the laboratory of solid
mechanics at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The test rig is built to mimic an industrial
overhung compressor supported by an active tilting pad journal bearing (ATPJB). A picture of the test rig
is shown in Figure 1 with labeling of the main components. An electric motor 2 controlled by a VLT 1 drives
the flexible rotor shaft 12 through a layshaft 10 and flexible coupling 11. The flexible coupling aims to
decouple the motor and rotor dynamics. The rotor shaft is supported by a self aligning spherical ball bearing
3 in the drive end and an ATPJB 5 in the overhung end. Two replaceable rigid discs 7 are used to mimic
the loading of an overhung compressor and to control the severity of gyroscopic effects. All components
are mounted to a flexible cast iron foundation 9 supported by a rubber mat. An AMB 4 can be used to
excite the rotor but has not been utilized during this analysis. The rotor can be externally excited during
operation using a mechanical shaker 13 mounted via a stinger, force transducer, and excitation bearing 8.
Two servo valves 6 control the hydrostatic/controllable lubrication of the ATPJB. The test rig is mounted
with numerous sensors for monitoring the running conditions. A quadrature encoder A is utilized to obtain
the rotor angle and speed, and 10 displacement sensors B, C, D, E, and F are utilized to measure the
vibrations of the rotor shaft. The displacement sensors are mounted in vertical-horizontal pairs with even
and odd-numbering respectively.
2
==PAGE:141==
Fig. 1. Rotor-bearing-foundation test rig. The numbering of components corresponds to 1: VLT, 2: DC motor, 3: spherical
ball bearing, 4: AMB, 5: ATPJB, 6: servo valves, 7: loading discs, 8: Excitation bearing, 9: foundation, 10: layshaft, 11:
flexible coupling, 12: rotor shaft, 13: electromagnetic shaker.
And the designation of instrumentation corresponds to A:
quadrature encoder, B: displacement sensors (DS) 9 & 10, C: DS 7 & 8, D: DS 5 & 7, E: DS 3 & 4, F: DS 1 & 2.
Detailed images of the ATPJB are shown in Figure 2 and a schematic visualizing the internal flow lines
and direction is shown in Figure 3. The low-pressure (LP) oil for hydrodynamic lubrication enters at β and
is sprayed onto the shaft by the injection nozzles ε. The high-pressure (HP) oil for hydrostatic lubrication is
controlled through servo valves 1 and 2 denoted as α1 and α2, respectively. The HP oil is injected directly
through the pads via the injection orifice κ and nozzle η. All excess oil is returned through the oil return γ.
The four tilting pads ζ are numbered according to quadrants in the yz-plane and are able to tilt on steel
pivots θ fixed by the pivot pins δ. Tilting pads are symmetrically positioned around the bearing, separated
by 90 degrees between each pivot in a load-between-pad (LBP) configuration, meaning that the static load
of the rotor is placed between the two lower pads. The tiling pads have a pad offset of 0.5 meaning that the
pivot is placed centrally on the pad.
The flow direction of the hydrostatic lubrication depends on the input signal SVi sent to the servo valves
as illustrated in Figure 3. Centered spools (CS) are obtained for the input SV SC
i
= {2.6, 2.0}V, hence ±
deviations from this input control the injection pattern.
Fluid film perturbation is obtained by sending band-limited white noise signals to both servo valves.
During fluid film perturbation, the servo valve signal is described by its DC and AC levels. The signal can
in simple terms be described by the relation SVi = DCi + ACi(t) for servo valve i = {1, 2}, hence the DC
and AC levels are defining the constant and temporal properties of the signal, respectively. The AC level
ACi(t) is always a zero-mean Gaussian band-limited white noise process, purely defined by its noise standard
deviation σSVi = σSV , omitting the index i since the same standard deviation will always be used for both
servo valves. To excite the rotor horizontally, the same AC signal is sent to both valves and for vertical
excitation, equal AC signals with opposite signs are used. Although vertical and horizontal perturbation
offers a good comparison to EMA tests, they are not ideal for OMA, where it is desired to have as many
independent inputs as possible to avoid rank limitations. Thus, in this work, two uncorrelated servo signals
are employed by using different seeds for the Gaussian white noise generators.

2.1. Experimental procedure
Three types of experiments are performed under different excitation conditions: external shaker exci-
tation (i), controllable hydrostatic fluid film perturbation (ii), and pure passive hydrodynamic conditions
(iii). To estimate modal parameters experiments of type (i) enable the use of an EMA methodology with
known excitation forces, and types (ii) and (iii) require an OMA framework due to the presence of unknown
disturbances.
Fluid film perturbation experiments of type (ii) are performed with two different DC settings denoted as
a centered spool (CS) and passive equilibrium (PE) perturbation. CS perturbation implies that the spools
are centered; DCi = SV SC
i
= {2.6, 2.0}V, and PE perturbation is performed by adjusting the DC level such
that the rotor is positioned in its passive equilibrium, meaning the position of the rotor during its operating
condition with only passive hydrodynamic lubrication.
The frequency band of interest is [20, 250] Hz, and to improve resolution, signal-to-noise ratio, and anti-
aliasing performance all experiments are oversampled using a sampling frequency of fs = 3000 Hz providing
a Nyquist frequency of fN = 1500 Hz. To improve OMA processing times, experimental data of types
(ii) and (iii) have been low pass filtered and decimated 6 times to a sampling rate of fs = 500 Hz. The
measurement duration is T = 240 sec for all experiments, following the ANSI S2.47 standard.
2.2. Test plan
A test plan is built to characterize the bearing under different perturbation conditions at 1000 and 7000
rpm.
Fluid film perturbation tests aim at describing the influence of the hydrostatic supply injection pressure
ps,HP , the DC level at CS or PE perturbation, and the AC level σSV .
EMA experiments of type (i) are performed at 1000 rpm using horizontal, 45-deg, and vertical shaker
excitation, and at 7000 rpm using only horizontal excitation to obtain benchmark EMA parameters. Excita-
tion is limited to the horizontal direction at 7000 rpm due to coherence issues caused by increasing unknown
disturbances from running at passive conditions. These unknown disturbances are likely to arise from a
marginal stability state of the machine, unbalance excitation, or fluid nonlinearities such as turbulence or
two-phase flow. This disturbance is found to also allow for decent excitation of the system hence 4 passive
tests of type (iii) are performed at 7000 rpm.
All conducted experiments of type (ii) and (iii) are summarized in Table 1 and 2 for 1000 and 7000 rpm
respectively.
4
==PAGE:143==
Furthermore, smaller tests are also performed to characterize specific parts of the test rig, such as rotor
position vs. servo valve input, and EMA shaker tests during controllable hydrostatic lubrication.

2.3. Rotor Equilibrium Position - Influence of input signals and supply pressure
Initially, experiments are performed to experimentally document the relationship between rotor position
and servo valve input SVi, injection supply pressure ps,HP , and rotational speed Ω. Sensors 3 and 4 are
located closest to the APTJB, hence they are used to represent the y (horizontal) and z (vertical) coordinates
of the journal position inside the bearing. The parameters of interest, SVi, ps,HP , Ωare varied to cover
normal operational ranges, and the resulting journal position in y and z directions are shown as a function of
the servo valve inputs SVi for different supply pressures ps,HP at 1000 and 5000 rpm in Figure 4. Measured
points are shown by dots and the visualized surfaces are obtained by a R2 logistic least squares fit. The
rotor movement is naturally found to match the expected from studying the schematic of fluid flow in
Figure 3, e.g. by increasing SV1 fluid is injected through pad 2 and the rotor is consequently pushed in the
positive y and negative z direction. Increasing the supply pressure ps,HP has the effect of increasing the
effective displacement of the rotor as expected due to increased fluid forces. Moreover, by comparing 1000
and 5000 rpm figures, it is clear that the hydrostatic injection has less effect on the rotor displacement for
increased rotation speed, and therefore higher supply pressures are required to displace the rotor. Notice
especially from Figure 4d that it becomes impossible to position the rotor in the passive equilibrium using
only ps,HP = 20 bar. This makes it impossible to conduct PE perturbation with low supply pressures at a
high angular velocity, hence PE perturbation is not used for experiments of type (ii) at 7000 rpm.
The servo valve input required to center the spools is denoted as the CS input and is found when the
gradient of the rotor vs SVi curve is steepest, approximately SCSC
i
= {2.6, 2.0} V. Hence it also becomes
clear that the rotor position quickly saturates with respect to the servo valve input. And the effective input
range of the servo valves is approximately ±0.5 V around the CS input.

3. Mathematical and Computational Tools for EMA and OMA linked to controllable pertur-
bations via fluid film
3.1. At-speed run-out compensation
OMA methods are rarely utilized for rotating machinery due to the invasion of harmonic vibrations
violating basic OMA assumptions of random excitation. To eliminate intrusive synchronous harmonics from
rotordynamic systems, Svendsen [18] employed a notch filter targeting the specific harmonic frequencies,
while Novak [19] performed a run-out compensation at the operating speed to mitigate all synchronous
vibrations. The best results are obtained with the last technique, also used in this work. The technique
is denoted by an at-speed run-out of compensation. This compensation is paramount in Active Magnetic
Bearings (AMB) that rely heavily upon good-quality feedback and is mainly used to remove mechanical
and electrical run-out noise [20]. However, since synchronous vibrations are correlated to the rotational
speed and hence also the rotor angular position, performing run-out compensation at the operating speed
also has the property of removing the harmonic synchronous vibrations of the measured signals, which is
of great advantage considering the application of OMA for rotating machinery. In practice, the at-speed
run-out compensation is performed by measuring the shaft angular position at the operating speed using
the rotary quadrature encoder at a high sampling rate of fs = 16 kHz over 20 seconds. The measured
displacement and force signals are averaged across shaft angles and saved as a reference in look-up tables.
6
==PAGE:145==
The run-out compensation is then completed online by subtracting the reference from the recorded data
and is implemented directly into the acquisition system to allow for real-time active control of the system.
3.2. EMA algorithm
The employed EMA methodology follows the principles described by Ewins [21] and Heylen et al. [22].
From experiments of type (i) the rotor response x ∈RNo and excitation force f ∈RNi are measured at
No = 10 outputs and Ni = 1 input. EMA algorithms base their estimation on frequency response functions
(FRFs). The H1(ωk) estimator of discrete frequency ωk is utilized which bases its FRF estimate upon the
cross-spectral density Gyf and power spectral density Gff [22].
[H1(ωk)]No×Ni = [Gxf]No×Ni [Gff]−1
Ni×Ni
(1)
The spectral densities are calculated via Welch’s method with Hanning windows and 50% overlap. Modal
parameters are estimated using a MATLAB implementation of the global Poly-Reference Least Squares
Complex Frequency-domain (p-LSCF) method which is an extension of the well-established Least Squares
Complex Frequency-domain (LSCF) method. The p-LSCF is also commercially known as the PolyMAX
estimator which is a trademark of LMS-International. The p-LSCF method was first presented by Guillaume
et al. in [23] and further tested and described in [24, 25, 26]. The p-LSCF models the FRF by means of the
right matrix-fraction description (RMFD).

respectively the numerator and denominator polynomial coefficient matrices to be estimated. To estimate,
poles λn and participation vectors γn only the numerator polynomial coefficient matrices α = [A0 . . . Ap]T
are required. They are determined by formulating a sub-optimal linear least- squares problem of the type
Mα = 0 and solving for α from which poles and participation vectors are determined. Using the estimated
poles and participation vectors, mode shapes are obtained in a second step by the Least Squares Frequency

3.3. OMA algorithms
When experiments are of type (ii) and (iii) modal parameters are estimated via OMA. Several OMA
methods are utilized for two main reasons: To analyze their differences in applicability and to compare
modal estimates across methods. The OMA methodology employed follows the main principle described
by Brincker et al.
in [27].
Four time-domain methods are chosen namely the Ibrahim Time Domain
(ITD), data-drive Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI), Eigensystem Realization Algorithm (ERA), and
Poly Reference (PR), including one frequency domain method namely the enhanced Frequency Domain
Decomposition (FDD).
Time domain methods often define least squares problems by the use of block Hankel matrices. The
ITD, ERA, and PR base their estimates upon the correlation function (CF) matrix while the data-driven
SSI estimates the CFs internally. The CF matrix is calculated using the direct unbiased method under the
assumption of stationary [27].
bRx(k) =
1
N −k X(1:N−k)XT
(k+1:N),
k ≥0
(4)
Where X = [x(1), x(2), . . . , x(N)] is the data matrix, k represents the discrete time instance t = ∆tk with
time step ∆t, N is the total number of samples, and b[ ] denotes an estimated quantity. CFs are truncated
after the first Nk time lags to reduce the influence of the low amplitude noise tail, while removing the first
time lag often stabilizes OMA routines since it can be dominated by measurement noise. The number of
included time lags Nk depends on the damping level of the system and is summarized for different experiment
types in Appendix A.
The frequency domain methods base their estimation upon the spectral density (SD) matrix bGx(ωk)
defined by the discrete Fourier transform (DFT) of the CF matrix (4). However, the SD matrix is more
conveniently calculated by Welch’s method with Hanning windows and 50% overlap to reduce leakage.
The ITD technique was initially introduced by Ibrahim in [28] and further developed to a more modern
description as given in [27] which has been employed for this work. The ITD estimates a system matrix
A ∈R2No×2No by least squares hence only No = 10 modal parameters are available.
The SSI is thoroughly documented by Overschee and Moor [29] using a control theory terminology, while
an OMA oriented description is given by Brincker et al. in [27][30]. The implemented SSI follows algorithm
2 presented in [29]. A block Hankel matrix with 2s block rows is built from the measured output x(k)
and used to calculate a projection matrix O = ΓsX0 expressed by the observability matrix Γs and Kalman
states X0. By calculating the singular value decomposition of O = USVT , Γs and X0 can be efficiently
estimated by keeping the n most influential singular values.
bΓs = UnS1/2
n ,
bX0 = S1/2
n VT
n
(5)
8
==PAGE:147==
A discrete system matrix bAd can be obtained from the observability matrix from which n/2 modal parameters
can be obtained by the eigenvalue decomposition.
bAd = Ψ′[µi]Ψ′−1
(6)
Where [µn] is a diagonal matrix holding the discrete-time poles and Ψ′ is a matrix holding the mode shapes
in state coordinates.
The ERA was introduced in the mid-eighties by Juang and Pappa in [31] and Pappa in [32]. The ERA
is very similar in formulation to the SSI apart from its use of pre-calculated CFs. A discrete time system
matrix bAd is similarly estimated by the SVD keeping the n highest singular values denoted by the nth-order
realization. Modal parameters are then obtained by eigenvalue decomposition equivalent to (6) yielding n/2
modes.
PR identification is described by Brincker et al. in [27] where an auto-regressive (AR) model with NA
AR coefficients is fitted to the CF matrix. A companions matrix Ac is built from which NANo/2 modal
parameters can be obtained by the eigenvalue decomposition.
The enhanced FDD is well described by Brincker et al. in [27], where the SD matrix is decomposed
by SVD at discrete frequencies corresponding to peaks of the singular values, followed by modal filtering
and calculation of impulse response functions from bands around the SVD peaks. The 1-DOF ITD is then
utilized to estimate modal parameters from the isolated impulse response functions.
3.4. OMA automation
OMA automation (AOMA) is important considering the optimal use of non-invasive fluid film perturba-
tion, i.e. with the desired online monitoring it is required to obtain continuous estimates of modal parameters
without user interaction. Therefore a simple AOMA routine is developed. The routine is based upon an
unsupervised classification of stabilization diagrams and draws inspiration from Paulsen et al. [33][34], and
Dreher et al. [35] who both developed automated OMA methods based on the SSI technique. The SSI,
ERA, and PR methods can be used to construct stabilization diagrams and are automated denoted by the
A-SSI, A-ERA, and A-PR. The AOMA routine(s) follows the steps summarized below and also graphically
presented in Figure 6:
(a) Calculate modal parameters using the SSI, ERA, or PR method for different model orders:
∀n ∈[nmin, nmax].
The SSI and ERA allow for sub-solutions by truncating the n number of sin-
gular values of equation (5) whereas in PR identification the problem has to bed resolved at every
order NA.
(b) Certain spurious modal parameters are discarded by purely evaluating the pole λn. A Modal parameter
set is discarded when its damping is strictly less than 0 or greater than 1, i.e.
showing signs of
instability or non-oscillatory dynamics respectively. It is also required that the complex conjugate
of the corresponding pole exists in the set of modal parameters since these unaccompanied poles are
unlikely to represent physical dynamics.
(c) A stabilization diagram is built by comparing model estimates of model order n to model order n −1
for all model orders [nmin, nmax]. If their relative difference is within the accuracy parameters εf, εζ,
and εψ that modal set is identified as stable.
(d) An agglomerative hierarchical clustering using the dMACXP distance measure is used to cluster the
stable modal sets. A cluster cutoff dMACXPcut and the number of returned clusters Nclust must be
specified.
(e) Global estimates are obtained by a MACXP weighted average as described in [23].
(f) Lastly to avoid duplicated sub-clusters caused by parameter variation through different model orders, if
two clusters are within the frequency tolerance εf,c, only the cluster with the highest number of modal
parameters is retained. Care should be taken in selecting the value of εf,c to balance the removal of
sub-clusters without discarding closely spaced modes.
9
==PAGE:148==
Fig. 6. Flowchart of the A-OMA routine(s).
It should be noted that full automation is not strived for. Automation is only acquired after setting several
crucial parameters which require expertise knowledge and pre-analysis. However, the routines can be used for
any system by re-tuning the AOMA parameters. A summary of used pre-processing and AOMA parameters
are given for analysis of all fluid film perturbation experiments of type (ii) for 1000 and 7000 rpm in Table
A.1 and A.2, of for passive experiments of type (iii) in Table A.3 in Appendix A.
Examples of stabilization diagrams plotted on top of the singular values of the SD matrix Gx for fluid
film perturbation (ii) and passive tests (iii) are shown in Figure 7 for 7000 rpm. Perturbation results (ii)
are shown in (a), (b), and (c) for ps,HP = 40 bar, and σSV = 0.5V. And passive results are shown in (d),
(e), and (f) for passive test 2. Poles in the grey region with low response amplitude are excluded during
clustering. The grey ranges are simply depicted for completeness and reproducibility

By analysis of Figure 7 (a), (b), (d), and (e) five stabilized frequencies are identifiable below 80 Hz
at approximately 27, 54, 59, 68, and 72 Hz for both perturbation and passive results. Where the 54 and
72 Hz components clearly align with peaks of the singular values as clear indications of physical modes.
10
==PAGE:149==
Stabilization diagrams for the SSI and ERA of passive results in Figure 7 (d) and (e) show significantly
faster stabilization compared to perturbation results in (a) and (b). Especially for the ERA method, the
maximum model order can be reduced to nmax = 40 for passive results while good mode stabilization is
still obtained. This behavior is explained by analysis of the singular value curves where the perturbation
results in (a) and (b) clearly indicate a more damped and coupled system compared to (d) and (e) where
clear peaks of the singular values are found allowing for better separation of modes. It is also observed that
the SSI estimates many poorly stabilized modes in the grey region compared to the ERA, which is mainly
attributed to the ERA using processed correlation functions compared to the SSI using the measured data
directly.
The PR stabilization diagrams in Figure 7 (c) and (f) exhibit very poor stabilization with almost no
indication of stabilized modes.
3.5. Modal assurance criteria (MAC)
Modal assurance criteria are widely used within the above EMA and OMA routines and as a tool to
analyze and compare experimental results. In general any MAC(v1, v2) ∈[0, 1] results in a real scalar where
1 implies that the vectors are parallel/equivalent, and 0 means that they are orthogonal. Vacher et al. [36]
describe different extensions of the original MAC criterion. Due to the general complexity of modal sets,
only the MAC extensions denoted by the MACX and MACXP are utilized. The MACX presented in (7)
is able to compare two complex non-monophase vectors v1 and v2, i.e. complex vectors that are not equal
phase modulo π.
MACX (v1, v2) =

The MAC criteria are also used as distance measures dMACX and dMACXP for hierarchical clustering
defined as dMACX = 1 −MACX. Figure 8 shows an exemplary use of the MACXP, by comparing modal
estimates of a single mode across estimation methods and perturbation settings. Hence there are 12 tiles
separating each method, with every 6th tile representing the same supply pressure. The left and top tiles
include the three EMA tests (horizontal, vertical, and 45-deg), enabling EMA-OMA comparison. When a
modal estimate is missing for a specific method-perturbation setting those tiles are given the color grey.
11
==PAGE:150==
Fig. 8. Detailed explanation of MACXP plot by zoom-in of the lower right corner of mode 6 using PE perturbation at 1000
rpm.
By simply considering the MACXP zoom, the comparison shows that the ITD only estimates mode 6
when σSV = [0.7, 1] V while the FDD estimates mode six for all settings. The ITD estimates compare well
to EMA estimates performed with 45-deg and horizontal excitation and generally less to FDD estimates for
small σSV .
3.6. Controllable lubrication technique
3.6.1. Passive hydrodynamic vs hydrostatic lubrication
Figure 9 presents FRFs obtained using horizontal shaker excitation for different controllable and passive
lubrication setups. The average temperature of the outlet oil from the ATPJB is also obtained for each
experiment. No fluid-film perturbation is performed hence the oil flow is constant throughout the experi-
ments. The p-LSCF routine is applied for all shown FRFs. Insignificant fluctuations are found considering
natural frequencies as also indicated by the alignment of FRF peaks. However, the damping ratio is found
to vary slightly for the modes at 60, 92, and 97 Hz, where the damping ratios of the latter are indicated.
Fig. 9. FRF comparison using passive and controllable lubrication. SC: spool center, PE: passive equilibrium.
12
==PAGE:151==
Assessing the passive vs. controllable lubrication FRFs, differences are found in the low-frequency range
below 60 Hz while also around the 90-100 Hz resonances. Other regions in the analyzed spectrum show
small to no variation between FRFs.
Amplifications of the FRFs in the low-frequency range for CS lubrication indicate that the rotor system
is softened proportional to the supply pressure. It is also seen that extra damping has been introduced for
the 97 Hz mode, which also fits the theory of softening, allowing for better energy dissipation considering
rotor-related modes.
The PE lubrication has a much smaller effect on the low-frequency band. The amplitude of the rotor
modes around 95Hz is again proportional to the supply pressure. Although for ps,HP ≤20 the response is
almost unchanged with respect to the passive FRFs. The damping ratio of the 97 Hz mode is also found to
reduce slightly for high supply pressures.
3.6.2. Effect of fluid film perturbation - DC and AC levels
OMA methods assume Gaussian responses, making it crucial to validate this assumption. Verifying it
helps gauge the potential bias in the final modal parameters. Therefore to analyze the distribution of the
measured signals a histogram and normal probability plot are shown in Figure 10 for sensor 4 (vertical)
using experiments made with σSV = [0.1, 1] V, ps,HP = [12, 20] bar, and with two different DC levels; CS
(SV = {2.6 ,2 } V) and PE (SV ≈{2.8 ,1.7 } V).
Fig. 10. Histograms and normal probability plot of sensor 4’s signal during different perturbation conditions.
Table 3

The histograms clearly demonstrate that using
CS perturbation results in a wider distribution that
closely resembles a normal distribution. This obser-
vation is further supported by the normal probability
plots, which show that the data approximately fol-
lows a straight line. However, deviations from nor-
mality are evident when considering highly negative
data points, which indicate drops in the rotor’s neg-
ative Z-directions. These drops are likely caused by
irregularities in the fluid film, possibly caused by a
known flaw in the high-pressure pump.
When the DC level is changed such that the rotor
is positioned in its passive equilibrium (PE) the dis-
tributions become more narrow and also appear to be right-skewed especially for σSV = 1V, meaning that
the rotor is mostly displaced in the positive Z-direction. This result is explained by the saturation of the
servo valve signal with respect to the rotor position. When the DC level of the valves is adjusted to push
the rotor into its equilibrium position, following Figure 4 it is clear that the servo input saturates faster in
one direction with respect to rotor displacement. OMA assumes that responses are Gaussian, hence it is
expected that the CS perturbation will provide better conditions for OMA techniques.
13
==PAGE:152==
When performing fluid-film perturbation it is of high interest to know the perturbation range with respect
to the radial clearance, since this can indicate the invasiveness of the perturbation. A Gaussian distribution
is fitted to data for all AC levels, and 3 times the standard deviation is used to represent the maximum
displacement in the y and z directions inside the bearing. The results are summarized in Table 3. The AC
levels clearly correlate with increased rotor displacements as expected from the increased spool movement
leading to increased fluid flow. The assembled radial clearance of the bearing is 84 µm hence the fluid film
perturbation excites approximately 1-17% of the possible rotor movements.
3.6.3. Analysis of the spectral density matrix
An analysis of the SD matrix before modal parameter estimation allows for an overview of the dominating
modes and the energy distribution in the measured signal. Figure 11 shows the singular values of the spectral
matrix for exemplary experiments with varying supply pressure and AC levels at 1000 and 7000 rpm. For
7000 rpm a passive test with only hydrodynamic lubrication is also included.
(a) 1000 rpm
(b) 1000 rpm
(c) 7000 rpm
(d) 7000 rpm
Fig. 11. Singular values of the SD matrix for different perturbation settings. The legend text refers to parameters: (DC level,
supply pressure, AC level) or (SVi, ps,HP , σSV ). (a) and (b) shows plots for 1000 rpm and (c) and (d) for 7000 rpm.
First, it is highlighted from Figure 11 that two singular value curves are seen to rise above the noise
floor for almost every test. This observation suggests that two independent inputs have been applied to
the system, consistent with the use of two uncorrelated servo valve signals. Hence it is also concluded that
the rank of the measurement system is likely reduced to 2 which is small compared to recommendations
from Brincker [27]. And it is hence expected that all OMA estimates will be biased to some degree by the
measurement noise especially considering closely spaced resonances.
For 1000 rpm tests in Figure 11 (a) and (b) the AC level significantly changes the response amplitude
as also observed from Table 3. However for 7000 rpm tests Figure 11 (c) and (d) the response amplitude
remains very similar. This is expected since a passive excitation is present as also shown by the black curve.
It is also observed that the singular value peaks are significantly sharper for the passive test compared to the
perturbation tests, again showing that the damping is significantly altered using fluid film perturbation at
7000 rpm. By comparing 1000 and 7000 rpm tests it also becomes apparent that the system dynamics have
changed significantly, both in damping and natural frequencies, as expected due to increasing gyroscopic
forces and journal-bearing effects.
Furthermore for the 7000 rpm tests in Figure 11 (a) and (b) an amplification peak is observed in the
singular value plots around 117 Hz. This frequency component is equal to the 1Ωsynchronous vibrations
and is likely present due to difficulties of performing a perfect at-speed run-out at 7000 rpm, where speed
variations are likely to occur.
14
==PAGE:153==
4. Experimental Results and Comparisons
4.1. OMA and EMA results
4.1.1. Results at 1000 rpm
The results obtained by fluid film perturbation at 1000 rpm using the ITD, A-SSI, A-ERA, A-PR, and
FDD OMA methods will now be presented and compared to EMA results obtained by average p-LSCF
estimates using horizontal, 45-deg, and vertical shaker excitation.
To gain an overview of the pole placement, the resulting undamped natural frequencies fn are presented
in Figure 12a. The marker type and color indicate the test type, pressure, and AC level respectively. The
different OMA methods are distributed on the y-axis and further subdivided into test types (ps,HP and DC
level). EMA results are indicated by black lines.
(a) Undamped natural frequencies
(b) Damping ratios
Fig. 12.
Results from all tests and OMA methods.
(a): undamped natural frequencies.
(b): damping ratios of modes
corresponding EMA estimates. The figure legend applies to both (a) and (b).
15
==PAGE:154==
First, it is noticed that many frequencies are scattered in-between EMA estimates using the AOMA
methods. This is likely a result of a model over-specification which is required to capture all meaningful
dynamics but has the downside of yielding mathematical and spurious modes. A higher density of frequencies
is nevertheless found around EMA modes, whereas other clusters of modal parameters are also identified at
47, 150, and 174.7 Hz, marked by red dashed lines. These frequency components are found at frequencies
with low amplification and are deemed to be possible noise components in the signal.
Considering the
clusters around EMA estimates, no apparent trends in natural frequency or damping are observed when
varying test parameters, indicating that the influence of the perturbation settings is likely minimal.
To simplify the analysis of modal parameters, it is assumed that parameters closest to the EMA estimate
for every test are considered an estimate of that mode. This assumption is considered decent for some modes
where the OMA estimates are rather consistent: i.e. 60 and 69 Hz whereas less good for estimates of the
modes at 92 and 98 Hz. The simplification is made nevertheless and its effect has to be considered. The
sorting allows plots of the damping ratio for every mode as shown in Figure 12b.
To further enrich the analysis, all modal estimates are compared using the MACXP assurance criteria in
Figure 13. Separate MACXP plots are made for the two spool perturbation settings: Centered spool (CS)
and passive equilibrium (PE) such that a comparison between the two perturbation settings is possible by
matching the plots. A detailed explanation of the MACXP axes is shown in Figure 8.
By analysis of both Figures 12 and 13 the different OMA methods will be discussed:
The ITD method does not predict all modes of interest and it is often unable to separate closely spaced
modes. The ITD’s overall performance is expected due to several limitations. Having only No = 10 input
channels, the ITD has a maximum prediction capacity of 10 modes and is further known to struggle with
coupled and damped responses.
The A-SSI shows estimates at all expected EMA frequencies including a significant amount of spurious
poles. Spurious poles are expected due to the high modal order required for the data-driven SSI to properly
model the noise in the signal. Undamped natural frequencies are consistent for modes at 27, 60, 69, and 140
Hz ignoring estimates for low AC levels, and damping estimates are often reasonable but scattered across
tests.
The A-ERA shows much fewer estimates of spurious poles compared to the other AOMA methods, as
expected from its more clear stabilization diagrams and its use of processed CFs.
The A-PR shows a more random scatter of undamped natural frequencies across the frequency band, as
expected from the confusing stabilization diagram.
The FDD is the only frequency domain technique and relies on a frequency-picking procedure of the
8 frequency points {27, 60, 69, 92, 98, 117, 125, 140}Hz. Hence only an estimate of these modes is expected.
Weirdly an estimate of the 140 Hz mode is missing but an extra mode below 117 Hz is seen. All modes are
consistently estimated however they all seem to be underestimated in both frequency and damping. The
coupled response of the ATPJB system combined with a measurement rank of two, makes it very hard for
the modal filtering of the FDD to work properly and the estimates will be accordingly biased.
By further analysis of the MACXP comparison Figure 13, it is clearly noticed that almost no estimates
are found for modes 2 (32Hz) and 10 (182Hz) explained by low participation of these modes.
Across all plots, a checker pattern is observed, see e.g. mode 4 where this effect is clear. The pattern
arises due to missing estimates when the AC level becomes too small. Hence it becomes clear that for
all estimation methods, there is a limit to when the signal-to-noise ratio becomes so small that physical
information of the system modes disappears. To consistently obtain modal estimates using all OMA methods,
it is recommended to use an AC level of at least σSV = 0.7V.
By comparing CS and PE perturbation plots they appear very similar although small differences can be
observed for individual modes where the CS perturbation method provides a slight increase in the number
of estimated parameters, see e.g. modes 3, 4, 6, and 8 where the number and comparison between successful
modal estimates increases.
Modal parameter sets that compare well to EMA estimates are often found using the A-SSI,
and A-ERA methods.
See e.g.
modes 1, 3, 4, 6, and 9 indicated by a red color along the top
and right center tiles corresponding to A-SSI, A-ERA, and EMA comparisons.
These modes gen-
erally also compare well with other AOMA methods indicated by off-diagonal blocks of red tiles.
16
==PAGE:155==
(a) MACXP for mode 1-5
(b) MACXP for mode 6-10
Fig. 13. MACXP comparison for all tests and OMA methods. The grey color indicates a missing estimate.
17
==PAGE:156==
Fig. 14. Axis explanation
Based on the preceding knowledge, it is concluded that
the A-SSI and A-ERA methods consistently identify
the most relevant modes while minimizing the num-
ber of spurious poles. Additionally, employing a high
AC level above σSV = 0.7V yields an optimal signal-
to-noise ratio, thereby enhancing the performance of
OMA methods.
The subsequent analysis will now put focus on how
the different perturbation parameters affect the AT-
PJB modal estimates.
And if they do so, in what
way?
MACXP plots are generated by discarding poor
estimation methods and perturbation settings. Hence
the ITD, FDD, and A-PR methods are removed, while
tests using an AC level of σSV = {0.05, 0.1, 0.3, 0.5}V
are also removed.

shown in Figure 15 with an explanation of the new
axes in Figure 14.
(a) MACXP for mode 1-5
(b) MACXP for mode 6-10
Fig. 15. MACXP comparison for A-SSI and A-ERA for AC levels σSV = {1, 0.7}V
Analyzing the reduced MACXP plots in Figure 15 it is clear that modes 3, 4, 6, and 9 are predicted very
18
==PAGE:157==
consistently among AOMA methods, EMA methods, and different perturbation settings. Hence it must also
be concluded that no effect of the different perturbation settings is visible in the resulting modal estimate.
Mode 5 is estimated consistently across both AOMA methods however the overall comparison is not
great and modes are generally not alike, which is also the case between EMA tests. However, it is clear that
using CS perturbation increases the comparison across the AOMA methods compared to PE perturbation.
Ignoring missing estimates, a similar trend can be found considering modes 1 and 7 where the CS pertur-
bation provides slightly better comparisons between all methods, indicating that improved OMA conditions
are obtained using CS perturbation compared to PE perturbation. This is likely explained by the skewed
response distributions obtained from PE perturbation violating OMA assumptions.
The OMA methods often fail to predict Modes 2, 7, 8, and 10 which can be attributed to their low
response participation, as evident from the analysis of the singular values of the SD matrix. Since low
participation modes have less impact on the system dynamics, obtaining accurate estimates for these modes
is less critical for condition monitoring purposes.

Table 4
Comparison of modal parameters between EMA and OMA methods (A-SSI, and A-ERA) using σSV = 1V and different
perturbation setups. In parenthesis is the relative deviation (RD) to the EMA estimate, calculated as
RD = fOMA−fEMA
fEMA
100%.
A good agreement is generally seen comparing the undamped natural frequency with a relative deviation
of all below 3% and many below 1%. Analyzing the damping ratios generally larger deviations are found
up to 25%, while the damping estimates from OMA are generally larger than those predicted by EMA,
indicating that the damping might be slightly increased using fluid film perturbation. However, for mode 1
the OMA method consistently estimates a damping of 3.4, 3.5% which is significantly smaller than the EMA
estimate of 4.67%. The damping ratio of modes 8 and 9 shows the best agreement between EMA and OMA
estimates, which is likely caused by the smaller damping ratio of these modes allowing for better separation
of these modes. Note also that a deviation of 25% is considered small when analyzing damping estimates
that can easily differentiate by orders of magnitude.
The discrepancies seen between OMA and EMA methods do not necessarily mean that the OMA is
wrong, and vice versa. The EMA estimates are expected to be biased due to the shaker attachment. With
that said one could come to the conclusion that the OMA estimate is the true (or close to true) value of
the modal parameters, especially considering there exists a quite small difference in both fn and ζn across
both method and perturbation setups. However, such a conclusion is not necessarily true either. As already
discussed it is still unknown if the fluid film perturbation has an effect on the modal parameter. However,
the effect (if it exists) is likely smaller than the error related to the OMA methods since no significant trends
19
==PAGE:158==
can be found when varying the fluid film perturbation settings. With an AC level of σSV = 1 V the rotor
amplitude variations were within 17% of the assembled radial clearance hence it is also concluded that this
perturbation range is likely to be non-invasive in terms of modal parameters. Andr´eas and Santiago also
conclude in [37] that linear journal bearing coefficients are still applicable, hence also modal parameters, for
amplitude variations within 50% of the radial clearance, backing up this conclusion.
4.1.2. Results at 7000 rpm
Analysis at 7000 rpm has been conducted similarly to the above for 1000 rpm including the analysis of
pure passive tests (experiment type (iii)). The analysis is focused on modes below 80 Hz where in total
5 modes are identified. EMA estimates are obtained by the p-LSCF method using only horizontal shaker
excitation. Again the A-SSI and A-ERA are found to perform the best of the five OMA methods.
The undamped natural frequencies fn and damping ratios ζn are plotted against the AC level using the
A-SSI and A-ERA in Figure 16. All pressure levels are included, but the ps,HP = 12 bar estimates are
highlighted with a black background. The passive results have been averaged across repeated tests using
the A-SSI and A-ERA and are shown with green lines. The fn plots show a ±5% interval of the passive
estimate to establish a common sense of deviation across modes. Small trends are found for varying AC
levels, see. e.g. the plot for ζ1 where the damping correlates positively with the AC level. Also for f2 a
small negative correlation with the AC level is found. However, in the general picture, no clear trends can
be established across modes. It can also be concluded that all natural frequencies are predicted within 5%
of the passive estimates and even better for most cases. Note also that the 12 bar estimates are often closest
to the passive estimates, especially considering the damping ratios. Analysing the available EMA results
they deviate slightly from the passive estimates. This deviation is likely caused by the shaker interaction.
Modes 2 (54 Hz) and 5 (72 Hz) have been highlighted since these are the dominating modes of the system
at 7000 rpm, as found by analysis of singular value plots in Figure 11. These modes also yield the smallest
standard deviation of the passive estimates, shown by thin green bands.
Fig. 16. Undamped natural frequencies fn and damping ratios ζn versus the AC-level estimated at 7000 rpm.
Concluding that the 12-bar estimates are most accurate in terms of non-invasiveness they are compared
to the passive and EMA estimates in Table 5 for exemplary AC levels. Assuming that the passive OMA
results resemble estimates from perfect non-invasive tests these will be used as benchmark parameters. All
methods are therefore compared to the average passive results and their relative deviation (RD) are shown
in parentheses, calculated as RD = fest−fpas
fpas
100%, while the passive results have their standard deviation
across tests in parenthesis.

==PAGE:159==
Natural frequencies are estimated consistently with deviations below 3% for almost every mode and
testing method, whereas the damping ratios show significant deviations compared to passive results. High
participation modes 2 and 5 have passive damping ratios of 1.4 and 0.67% respectively, whereas perturbation
results are increased by 100-300% for both modes. Low participation modes are predicted with a damping
much closer to the passive estimates, with a maximum deviation of 70%. Additionally, the EMA results
display significant deviations in damping, primarily attributed to shaker interaction.

Exemplary mode shapes are shown for the AC level σSV = 0.5 and all estimation methods and modes
in Figure 17. Mode shape 1 clearly shows a dominating horizontal movement for perturbation A-SSI and
A-ERA and passive tests indicating that this mode likely corresponds to the first bending mode of the
foundation. The remaining modes are harder to categorize purely by analyzing the shape of their orbit due
to a sensor foundation coupling which makes it impossible to separate foundation and rotor modes without
further knowledge of foundation vibrations. It is however noticed that the dominating modes 2 and 5 are
predicted very consistently across all methods, indicating that these modes are estimated accurately. It
should also be noted that the FDD seems to provide the least accurate mode shapes compared to other
methods. This is expected since visualization of rotor mode shapes relies heavily on the complex part of the
eigenvectors which is known to be heavily biased using the FDD [27]. Analyzing the EMA mode shapes they
are seemingly dominated by horizontal movement (movement in xy-plane) compared to the OMA estimates.
This is likely a bias of the EMA mode shape induced by the horizontal excitation.

(b) Mode 2

5. Conclusion
The DC level (CS or PE) did not have a significant effect on the estimated modal parameters, although
it was suggested that using PE perturbation was optimal in terms of load capacity, while CS perturbation
improved signal quality for OMA analysis. The hydrostatic injection supply pressure was found to correlate
with increased damping levels, but a low supply pressure of 12 bar was sufficient for proper perturbation.
At 1000 rpm, the AC level had a significant impact on the signal-to-noise ratio, while its influence on the
modal parameters remained minimal. The optimal choice was the highest AC level of σSV = 1 V, which
perturbed the rotor within 17% of its radial clearance. At 7000 rpm the AC level had minimal influence on
both the signal-to-noise ratio and the modal estimates, possibly attributed to the additional presence of a
natural excitation source.
22
==PAGE:161==
The developed automated methods, A-ERA and A-SSI, were found to yield improved estimates compared
to other OMA methods, especially when considering damped and coupled responses.
It is therefore proposed to combine the A-ERA and A-SSI for online monitoring of rotor-bearing systems.
Consequently, these methods were also employed to validate the modal parameters obtained from fluid film
perturbation.
At 1000 rpm, it was concluded that combining non-invasive testing and OMA methods was feasible for
estimating undamped natural frequencies and mode shapes within 1% of EMA estimates. However, damping
ratios were slightly elevated compared to EMA estimates reaching a maximum deviation of 25%.
Furthermore, it was observed that increasing the angular velocity of the rotor resulted in a natural
passive excitation source. This natural excitation was found to be satisfactory for accurately estimating
the dominating modes of the system at 7000 rpm, making complete non-invasive testing possible. At 7000
rpm, the non-invasive fluid-film perturbation testing again yielded accurate estimates of mode shapes and
undamped natural frequencies within 5% of the passive estimates, and 3% for dominating modes of the
system. However, damping ratios were consistently elevated with a maximum deviation of 300%. Passive
and EMA estimates were also found to match well in natural frequency although damping ratios and mode
shapes were found to differentiate, attributed to a shaker bias.
The main advantage of using fluid film perturbation for online monitoring of rotor-bearing systems lies
in the ease of testing, without significantly disturbing the operating conditions. Performing fluid film per-
turbation directly through journal bearings, by adding servo valves, offers a simpler and more cost-effective
solution compared to installing AMBs solely for the purpose of exciting the rotor system. Additionally, the
use of AMBs necessitates mounting rotor rings, which further disrupts the rotor dynamics.
Considering the application of OMA, the objective of machine monitoring is among others to detect
instabilities, often linked to reduced damping levels and self-induced vibrations. This, in turn, significantly
improves the overall applicability and precision of OMA when it is needed the most hence increasing its
applicability.
The primary drawbacks arise from the inherent damped and coupled nature of rotating machinery,
leading OMA methods to predict numerous spurious and biased poles, which complicate precise analysis.
Additionally, fluid film perturbation poses a limitation in measurement rank when only performed using a
single bearing, reducing the ability to distinguish noise from physical dynamics. And also if the bearing
position is positioned in nodes of critical modes, the estimation of these modes becomes impossible. One
potential solution to address these issues is by employing multiple controllable bearings, generating uncor-
related perturbations at different positions within the system. This approach enhances the measurement
rank and spatial distribution of inputs, providing more appropriate conditions for OMA analysis.
Overall, this paper has demonstrated the successful integration of controllable lubrication techniques,
OMA methods, and computational tools for signal processing to accurately estimate modal parameters for
use in the online monitoring of rotor-bearing systems.
