Appendix B
Additional theory
B.1
Interpretation of rotor modes
This section will describe how the interpretation of complex rotor modes can be expressed.
The discussion and derivation given below is inspired by Krämer [51].
Considering that the eigenvalues and eigenvectors are generally complex for rotating rotor-
bearing systems the general real solution to an LTI problem considering a single mode k
can be written using Euler’s formula for the DOF i.
xik(t) = Ckeαkt [rik sin(ωkt + γk) + sik cos(ωkt + γk)]
(B.1)
Where xik(t) is the displacement of the i’th DOF caused by the k’th mode. αk, ωk are
the real and imaginary parts of the eigenvalue λk = αk + jωk. rik, sik are the real and
imaginary parts of the i’th DOF of the k’th eigenvector (mode shape). While Ck, γk are
constants to be found from initial conditions. The aim of this discussion is to understand
the general vibration shape hence the exponential decay term is neglected αk = 0 while
constants are restricted to Ck = 1 and yk = 0, which is unrestricting considering the
upcoming conclusion. Thus (B.1) becomes.
xik(t) = rik sin(ωkt) + sik cos(ωkt)
(B.2)
Rotor measurements are typically performed in stationary planar frames in horizontal
and vertical directions. Hence by evaluating eq. (B.2) in a horizontal x1 and vertical x2
DOF, omitting the index k one obtains.
x1(t) = r1 sin ωt + s1 cos ωt
(B.3)
x2(t) = r2 sin ωt + s2 cos ωt
(B.4)
Equation (B.4) describes an arbitrary elliptic path in the plane, which is either forward of
backward in relation to the direction of the rotor spin. To study the rotation and half-axis
of the ellipse, one introduces the complex displacement.
z(t) = x1(t) + jx2(t)
(B.5)
Using (B.4) and the deﬁnition of sines and cosines, (B.5) can be written.
z(t) = 1
2 [(r2 + s1) + j (−r1 + s2)] ejωt + 1
2 [(−r2 + s1) + j (r1 + s2)] e−jωt
(B.6)
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
155
==PAGE:167==
B.2. MODE INDICATOR FUNCTIONS
and in exponential form.
z(t) = Rforej(ωt+αfor) + Rbacke−j(ωt−αback)
(B.7)
where
Rfor = 1
2
q
(r2 + s1)2 + (−r1 + s2)2,
αfor = arctan −r1 + s2
r2 + s1
Rback = 1
2
q
(−r2 + s1)2 + (r1 + s2)2,
αback = arctan r1 + s2
−r2 + s1
(B.8)
The complex displacement z(t) is thus a summation of positive and negative rotating
vectors. One vector of length Rfor rotates in the positive (forward) direction which is
by deﬁnition in the same direction of the spin, while the vector of length Rback rotates
in the negative (backward) direction. For the speciﬁc mode if Rfor > Rback, the mode
is dominated by the forward whirl and is denoted a forward whirl mode, while in the
opposite case Rfor < Rback the mode is denoted a backward whirl mode. Moreover, it can
be shown that the major axis a, minor axis b, and the rotation of the real axis to the
major axis α is given by.
a = Rfor + Rback,
b = |Rfor + Rback|,
α = αfor + αback
2
(B.9)
To obtain the full bent form modeshape, the above analysis is repeated for all measurement
nodes/planes along the shaft. The mode shape then consists of the interconnection of the
points from individual ellipses occurring at the same time instance. Hence it also comes
apparent that for the same natural frequency, simultaneous forward and backward whirls
can exist at diﬀerent positions along the rotor, although this type of modeshape is rarely
observed.
B.2
Mode indicator functions
MIMO experimental tests often deal with a vast amount of FRFs and it becomes cumber-
some to analyze the global dynamics of the system. Also, some modes might not appear
in all FRFs which makes it hard to identify how many modes exist. For this reason,
mode indicator functions have been developed that exhibit local minima or maxima at
the modal frequencies of the system. A few modal indicators will be discussed below,
for further discussion of the topic see Avitabile [52] or Ewins [10] and for a theoretical
overview see Rades [53].
The most simple mode indicator function might be the sum of all FRFs. The sum function
produces one single curve that is obviously better than one FRF that might not show
all modes. It is a reasonable indicator when modes are well separated however it has
a tendency to hide smaller features or closely spaced modes. Better model indicators
functions (MIFs) are therefore developed that are capable of identifying closely spaced
modes.
Mode Indicator Functions are generally calculated using FRFs measured at No response
points, Ni inputs and Nf discrete frequencies normally obtained from MIMO excitation
methods. The two MIFs used in this project are the Multivariate MIF (MMIF) and the
Complex MIF (CMIF).
156
==PAGE:168==
B.2. MODE INDICATOR FUNCTIONS
Multivariate Modal Indicator function - MMIF
The MMIF is an extension of the general MIF which is based upon the assumption that
the real part of the FRF becomes zero at resonances. Using the ﬂexibility/displacement
FRF matrix H(ωk)Ni×No the MMIF is deﬁned in [10] by the plotting the eigenvalues α
for every frequency ωk of the generalized problem.
HT
I HIf = α
 HT
RHR + HT
I HI

f
(B.10)
ωk is omitted for readability. Where HR and HI are the real and imaginary parts of
HNi×No. The matrix products are of order Ni so that there are as many MMIF curves as
the number inputs. Drops of the smallest eigenvalue indicate undamped natural frequen-
cies and at frequencies where more than one MMIF curve drops simultaneously indicate
repeated natural frequencies. Due to the assumption of zero real part at resonances, the
MMIF is sensitive to distortion or phasal information (complex modes). The MMIF is
also known to sometimes provide fallacious drops due to cross-eigenvalue eﬀects or at
antiresonances. It is therefore suggested to always use the indicator together with other
MIFs or stabilization diagrams.
MMIFs for mobility and accelerance FRFs are simply obtained by multiplying the FRF
H matrix by iω and −ω respectively.
Complex Modal Indicator function - CMIF
The CMIF is based upon the economical SVD of the FRF matrix at each spectral line ωk
deﬁned as.
H(ωk)No×Ni = UNo×NiΣNi×NiVH
Ni×Ni
(B.11)
The Σ is the diagonal matrix of singular values, U and V are respectively the left and
right singular vectors. The CMIF is then deﬁned by the singular values plotted as a
function of frequency. Hence the number of CMIF curves will equal the number of inputs
Ni, and peaks of the singular value curves will indicate damped natural frequencies.
The frequencies where more than one curve peaks at the same time indicate a repeated
natural frequency. The CMIF indicator performs well for lightly damped structures but
has problems with closely spaced modes in damped structures. Here the MMIF performs
better since it locates the undamped natural frequencies that are mode distinct from each
other, [53]. It should also be noted that the CMIF is closely related to the frequency
domain decomposition (FDD) technique described in section 2.3.7, where the SVD of the
SD matrix is used to derive modal parameters of the system.
157
==PAGE:169==
Appendix C
Experimental modal analysis of
test-rig foundation
This Chapter will cover an experimental modal analysis of the test-rig foundation. The
rotor shaft has been removed for maintenance, allowing for the rotor and foundation
dynamics to be separated experimentally. The analysis is performed to provide valuable
experimental data for validating the FEM model of the foundation, and also to test the
implemented MIMO p-LSCF method.
The main experimental analysis will focus on transverse vibrations in the vertical plane,
while a smaller analysis is also performed to obtain the ﬁrst transverse bending mode in
the horizontal plane.
C.1
Experimental setup - Vertical plane
The experimental modal analysis of transverse vibrations in the vertical plane of the test-
rig foundation is performed by MIMO experiments with 2 vertical shaker inputs and 2
roving accelerometers. The test method is also known as Multi-point Random (MPR)
[10], where the advantage of having incoherent inputs reduces the probability of making
systematic errors in the FRF measurements. A picture of the test rig foundation with
the highlighted main components is shown in Figure C.1. It should be noted that not all
charge ampliﬁers, accelerometers, or power ampliﬁers are visible in the picture and that
the VLT-tower is detached from the foundation. The input and response positions are
shown in Figure C.2. The test rig is placed on a rubber mat used to dampen the dynamics
of the structure. To provide more references in terms of updating a FEM model and also
to understand better the dynamics of the foundation the experimental modal analysis is
performed twice with and without the VLT-tower and Drive Motor. The VLT-tower is
simply bolted to the foundation at its two legs. The drive motor is bolted in a frame to the
foundation and it is expected to provide both local mass and stiﬀness to the foundation.
In total 60 response points were used resulting in 30 roving experiments producing a total
of 120 FRFs. The position of excitation is chosen based on the previous estimation of
modeshapes see e.g. Wagner [9] and Gani [6]. The aim is to avoid nodal points of the
known mode shapes below 250Hz such that all modes of interest are excited.
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
158
==PAGE:170==
C.1. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP - VERTICAL PLANE
Figure C.1: Picture of the test rig showing the main components used during testing.
AMB: Active magnetic bearing. ATPJB: Active Tilting-pad journal bearing.
Figure C.2: Schematic of test-rig foundation showing the response and input positions.
C.1.1
Measurement hardware and data acquisition
The data acquisition is performed using the existing dSpace DS1103 and CP1103 controller
and connector panel respectively as described in section 3.3. The dSpace environment
is programmable through MATLAB-Simulink where the practical acquisition system is
designed. The main components of measurement hardware are listed below.
• Mechanical shakers
1. MB Electronics Shaker. Model PM 50.
2. Brüel & Kjær Vibration Exciter. Type 4809.
• Force transducers
1. Brüel & Kjær force transducer. Type 8230-001 Deltatron, 22 mV/N. Serial
number: 4159. Connected to the MB Electronics Shaker via a metal stinger.

==PAGE:171==

2. Two Brüel & Kjær force transducer.
Type 8230-001 Deltatron, 22 mV/N.
Serial number: 58626. Connected to the Brüel & Kjær Vibration Exciter via
a metal stinger.
• Accelerometers
1. Two Brüel & Kjær piezoelectric accelerometers type 4382, 3.16 pC/ms2. Serial
numbers 1652201 and 1320231.
• Charge ampliﬁers

The accelerometers are calibrated using a Bruel & Kjær
calibration exciter type 4294. Charge ampliﬁers all have
analog low-pass ﬁlters adjusted to 1kHz serving as anti-
aliasing ﬁlters. The sensitivity of force transducers and
accelerometers have been adjusted either directly via
their charge ampliﬁer or in the post-processing of the
signals.
The frequency range of interest is [20, 250]Hz based on
previous analysis of the rest-rig e.g. Wagner [9]. The
data is obtained during T
= 300 sec using a sam-
pling rate of fs = 3kHz giving a Nyquist frequency
fNy = fs/2 = 1.5kHz hence the signal is signiﬁcantly
oversampled helping to reduce signal noise and aliasing
eﬀects.
C.1.2
Excitation
Initially, both random and chirp excitation was investigated in terms of input correlation,
required testing time, and the required number of averages to obtain reasonable quality
FRFs. It was found that using Chirp-down and Chirp-Up signals for shakers 1 and 2
respectively gave the overall best results. The chirp signals vary within [20, 250]Hz with
a sweep period of 10 s.
C.2
Experimental setup - Horizontal plane
The experimental modal analysis in the horizontal plane of the foundation is performed
as a roving SIMO experiment. The MB mechanical shaker is now attached horizontally
160
==PAGE:172==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
at response point (1/15) perpendicular to the x-axis according to ﬁgure C.2. A picture of
the setup is shown in Figure C.3
The same measurement hardware and data acquisition system is used for this analysis
however the chirp range is increased to [20, 450]Hz to capture potential higher frequency
modes.
C.3
Results of foundation EMA
This section will present and compare the results of the experimental modal analysis of
the foundation with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor.
Signal quality
An example of the measured time series from output positions (4/1) and (4/15) is shown
in Figure C.4.
Figure C.4: Example of time series from foundation experiment with chirp excitation and
output in position 1 and 2. With VLT-tower and drive motor.
Figure C.5: Power spectrum of in-
put signals
It is seen that the force amplitude varies slightly
during each frequency sweep (chirp) meaning that
the input energy is likely to be frequency depen-
dent. The variation in input energy is likely caused
by shaker-foundation interference or incapabilities
of the mechanical shakers. This can also be shown
by a plot of the input power spectrum, see Figure
C.5. Here it is seen that the power of input is de-
creasing in the frequency interval of interest. This is
not desired since equal energy should be distributed
to all frequencies of interest.
However, the input
energy does not show any signiﬁcant drops and is therefore still deemed suitable. The
acceleration signals show a periodic nature as a consequence of the chirp excitation re-
peatedly covering diﬀerent resonances of the foundation.
The MIMO FRF is calculated using the H1 estimator for all experiments and ordered
into the FRF matrix H1(ωk)Ni×No between No = 60 outputs and Ni = 2 inputs. Welch’s
method is used with 20 averages, Hanning windows, and 50% overlap. Providing a fre-
quency resolution of fres = 0.066 Hz. To validate the quality of the estimated FRF’s the
161
==PAGE:173==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
Multiple coherence function γ2
M(ωk) is calculated for all 60 output positions, shown in
Figure C.6 (a) and (c) for experiments with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor
respectively. Clearly, the multiple coherence is satisfactory for all inputs and frequencies
although small dips are seen for some outputs around 50-100 Hz. These are explained by
possible poor excitation at the measurement location. During testing, these dips occurred
when accelerometers were placed close to the ATPJB or rotor pedestals. Overall it is con-
cluded that the coherence is satisfactory indicating good signal quality and input-output
linearity.
When performing MIMO testing it is crucial to have uncorrelated inputs to enable the
separation of output contributions. Therefore the correlation of the two input forces is
analyzed using the ordinary coherence γ2(ωk). The coherence between the input forces is
calculated for all 30 experiments and shown in Figure C.6 (b) and (d). It is seen that the
coherence is generally very low showing that input forces are indeed uncorrelated.
(a) + VLT and motor
(b) + VLT and motor
(c) −VLT and motor
(d) −VLT and motor
Figure C.6: Multiple coherence function for all output positions (a) and (c), and coherence
of input forces for all tests (b) and (d). For experiments with and without VLT-tower
and drive motor (± VLT and motor).
Similar response and input coherence are obtained for the experimental campaign without
the VLT-tower and drive motor showing that input signals are uncorrelated and that the
Multiple coherence is generally good.
Estimated Model parameters
The modal parameters are estimated using the implemented poly-reference LSCF method
(section 2.4.4). Stabilization diagrams for the experimental campaign with and without
the VLT-tower and drive motor are shown in Figure C.7 on top of the MMIF modal
162
==PAGE:174==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
indicator. The Figures only show an interval of model orders since a signiﬁcantly high
model order is needed for reasonable convergence of all modes.
(a) −VLT and motor
(b) + VLT and motor
Figure C.7: Stability charts produced by the p-LSCF routine shown together with the
MMIF modal indicator. Physical stable poles are indicated by arrows.
First analyzing the stability diagram where the VLT-tower and drive motor have been
removed (Figure C.7a) a total of 11 physical modes are found. Physical modes are iden-
tiﬁed by repeated stability status and also by a signiﬁcant decrease in the MMIF. Modal
estimates seem to have converged above a model order of 80 except for the mode around
200 Hz where modal properties lack convergence, however, a clear drop in the MMIF still
indicates that a physical mode exists in this region. Two close modes are found around
124 Hz which both show good convergence.
A higher model order is needed to obtain convergence when the VLT-tower and motor are
attached to the test rig, see Figure C.7b. Now a total of 16 physical modes are identiﬁed
showing that the foundation dynamics are increased in complexity. A few modes are
163
==PAGE:175==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
identiﬁed with poor convergence around [34-36]Hz, 196Hz, and 200Hz. These are still
identiﬁed as physical poles since the stabilization chart shows a clear straight line of
points. A straight line of points is also seen at 187Hz however this frequency has not
been deemed as physical plate mode due to no drop in the MMIF and not fully stabilized
modal parameters. This frequency component might belong to a component on top of
the foundation that is not caught properly by the sensor positions.
Comparing the two stabilization charts new physical modes are found at [34,36],94,116,180,
and 235Hz when the VLT-tower and drive motor are attached while a mode has disap-
peared at 155Hz.
A ﬁnal validation of the modal model is performed by comparing the reconstructed FRF
using the pole residue LSFD model equation(2.162) to the measured FRF. A good match
is found across all FRFs and the estimated modal parameters are therefore accepted.
Examples of LSFD ﬁts are shown in Figure C.8.

In general, the low-frequency modes appear with the highest participation for both ex-
perimental setups. With the attached VLT-tower and drive motor the modes at 196Hz
and 235Hz appear with very small participation showing that these modes participate
very little in the overall response of the foundation and they might not correspond to
other vibration components of the test rig. This is also backed up by not seeing these
frequency components when the VLT and motor have been removed. Damping ratios are
found within [1,4]% showing that the foundation is reasonably damped as also expected
due to the rubber damping mat. The standard deviations are generally low, yet a few
have noticeably high standard deviations e.g. at 201Hz which was also seen to have poor
convergence properties in the stabilization chart.
165
==PAGE:177==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
The mode shape complexity is a measure of "in phase" or "out of phase" movement of the
mode. A large mode complexity means that the mode has a traveling wave nature. Zero
complexity corresponds to proportional damping where energy is dissipated where it is
(standing wave) and high modal complexity infers that kinetic energy is transferred to
places of higher damping where it can be dissipated.
The Modeshapes of both experiments without and with the VLT-tower and drive motor
are shown in Figure C.9 and C.10 respectively. A MACX comparison is also shown of the
mode shapes of the foundation with the VLT-tower and drive motor to itself in Figure
C.11.
An analysis of the modeshape without the VLT-tower (Figure C.9) and drive motor is
given below:
• Mode shape 1 at 29.7Hz corresponds to the ﬁrst transverse bending mode of a
free-free plate with 2 nodal lines across the width of the plate.
• Mode shape 2 at 36.2Hz corresponds to the ﬁrst torsional mode of a free-free plate
with a nodal point in the center of the plate.
• Mode shape 3 at 65.9Hz corresponds to the second torsional mode of a free-free
plate with two nodal points.
• Mode shape 4 at 73.3Hz is the second transverse bending mode with 3 nodal lines
across the width of the plate.
• Mode shape 5 at 124Hz represents the third torsional mode. This mode has a high
complexity showing that phase delay is seen. This is likely a result of coupling to
the mode at 124.8Hz.
• Mode shape 6 at 124.8Hz corresponds to a mode where the free end is in bending
across the width of the plate and the drive end is in bending across the longitudinal
direction. It is noticed that torsional components are apparent in the modeshape
showing signs of coupling to the close neighbor mode at 124Hz. This mode also has
high complexity likely to be caused by the coupling.
• Mode shape 7 at 134.1Hz is the third transverse bending mode of the foundation.
• Mode shape 8 at 154.2Hz corresponds to a single bending in the drive end and
almost zero modal amplitude in the free end.
• Mode shape 9 at 178.5Hz corresponds to the fourth torsional mode of the foundation.
• Mode shape 10 at 201.5Hz corresponds to the fourth transverse bending mode of
the foundation. However, the modeshape shows steep gradients around the position
of the AMB and ATPJB showing that the modeshape is highly related to these
components.
• Mode shape 11 at 221.9Hz is a mode corresponding to internal bending of the
foundation in the free end and small vibrational amplitudes in the drive end.

==PAGE:178==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
Figure C.9: First 11 identiﬁed modeshapes of the foundation without the VLT-tower and
drive motor.
An analysis of mode shapes with attached VLT-tower and drive motor from Figure C.10
and their AutoMAC (Mode shapes compared to their self) in Figure C.11 is given below:
• Mode shape 1 at 29.7Hz corresponds to the ﬁrst transverse bending mode of a
free-free plate with 2 nodal lines across the width of the plate.
• Mode shape 2 and 3 at 34.2 and 36.5Hz both correspond to the ﬁrst torsional mode
of a free-free plate. One of these modes must belong to other structural elements
coupled to the foundation. It is found that the mode at 34.2Hz is a mode of the
VLT-tower, discussed further in the next section.
167
==PAGE:179==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
• Mode shape 4 at 64.2Hz corresponds to the second torsional model of a free-free
plate with two nodal points.
• Mode shape 5 at 71.2Hz is the second transverse bending mode with 3 nodal lines
across the width of the plate.
• Mode shape 6 94.8Hz is similar to the transverse bending mode at 71.2Hz with
a MACX of 0.5 while also some torsional components are visible. This mode is
likely related to the movement of other structures on the foundation not captured
by the response points. It is found that removing the drive motor also removes this
modeshape, discussed further in the next section.
• Mode shape 7 at 116.9Hz looks like a combination of the modes at 64.2, 127.4 and
175.9Hz indicated by the MACX. It is also found that removing the drive motor
removes this mode.
• Mode shape 8 at 124.8Hz corresponds to a mode where the free end is in bending
across the width of the plate and the drive end is in bending across the longitudinal
direction but with a smaller amplitude.
• Mode shape 9 at 127.4Hz is likely the third torsional mode of the plate.
• Mode shape 10 at 135.7Hz is the third transverse bending mode of the foundation.
• Mode shape 11 at 175 corresponds to a single bending in the drive end and smaller
modal amplitudes in the free end.
• Mode shape 12 at 180.7Hz corresponds to the fourth torsional mode of the founda-
tion.
• Mode shape 13 and 14 at 196.7 and 201.6Hz both show the nature of the fourth
transverse bending mode of the foundation. The mode at 196.7Hz has a signiﬁcantly
higher complexity and is likely related to the movement of other structures on the
foundation. It is found that this mode component is reduced when the drive motor
is detached. This is also apparent comparing the stabilization diagrams Figure C.7a
and C.7b.
• Mode shape 15 at 221.5Hz is a mode corresponding to internal bending of the
foundation in the free end and smaller transverse bending in the drive end.
• Mode shape 16 at 235.3Hz corresponds to out-of-phase vibration of the upper left
corner with respect to the remaining foundation. This is the position of the drive
motor and this mode is therefore likely to be highly inﬂuenced by the mass and
stiﬀness of the motor.

C.3.1
Comparison of foundation dynamics with and without
VLT-tower and drive motor
To further analyze the eﬀect of the VLT-tower and drive motor a direct comparison of
FRFs is used. Figure C.12 shows representative FRFs of the foundation with the VLT-
tower and drive motor, with only the drive motor and without both components. Figure
C.13 gives a MACX comparison of the estimated mode shapes from both setups.
Figure C.12: Comparison of FRFs from input 2 to response points (4/1) and (4/15) in
the drive and free end respectively.
170
==PAGE:182==
C.3. RESULTS OF FOUNDATION EMA
Figure C.13: MACX comparison of foundation modeshapes with and without the VLT-
tower and drive motor.
When only the VLT-tower is removed the FRFs remain very similar except around
[34,26]Hz where two resonance peaks are reduced to one when the VLT-tower is removed.
This explains the repeated mode shape seen at 34.2 and 36.5Hz in Figure C.10 where the
modeshape at 34.2Hz must correspond to the movement of the VLT-tower. Further anal-
ysis also showed that this mode is sensitive to the torque used to fasten the VLT-tower
to the test rig. It is therefore suggested to either separate the VLT-tower from the test
rig or to build a more secure connection making its dynamics more predictable.
When also removing the drive motor the FRFs change in both amplitude and frequency.
Resonance ampliﬁcations in the interval [80-120]Hz have disappeared which is also noticed
in Figure C.13 where modes 6 and 7 for the VLT and motor are not found to correspond
well to any modes without the VLT and motor. Interestingly is also a seemingly new
mode at 154Hz.
This mode only appears in the shown FRF from input 2 to output
(4/1) which is explained by analyzing its corresponding modeshape in Figure C.9. The
mode only exhibits movement in the drive-end and is consequently not excited by input
position 1 but very well by input position 2. From the MACX comparison, it can also be
concluded that this mode at 154Hz is very similar to the mode at 175.9Hz with VLT and
motor. This great reduction in natural frequency is explained by the removed mass from
the motor. And since the motor/mass is placed in an area of high modal amplitude it is
expected to have a signiﬁcant softening eﬀect on this mode.
It is also seen from the FRFs and MACX that mode 16 at 235.3Hz disappeared when
removing the motor, which is expected when analyzing its modeshape which is heavily
dominated by the movement of the motor position.
The inﬂuence of the drive motor concerning changes in natural frequencies can also be
further discussed. The added mass will in general have a softening eﬀect on the natural
frequencies.
This eﬀect can be observed on the low side of the frequency spectrum.
However, the added stiﬀness of the motor frame will have a stiﬀening eﬀect on the natural
frequencies of modes with high bending at the position of the motor. This eﬀect is clear
in the higher end of the frequency spectrum where e.g.
the mode around 180Hz has
171
==PAGE:183==
C.4. HORIZONTAL EXCITATION RESULTS
increased in frequency when adding the motor. A high amount of bending is also found
in the motor position in the corresponding mode shape supporting the argument.
The MACX comparison also indicates that modes 5 and 6 without the VLT and motor
have switched the order in terms of the frequency concerning their corresponding mode 8
and 9 of the foundation with the VLT and motor.
C.4
Horizontal excitation results
The results from excitation in the xy-plane will now shortly be presented. The main
interest of this analysis is to identify the ﬁrst bending mode of the foundation in the
xy-plane. The mode has been successfully identiﬁed and it was found to be the only
dominating resonance in the xy-plane within [20, 450]Hz. Modal parameters are obtained
using the p-LSCF method following the same procedure as presented above. Figure C.2
presents the modal parameters with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor.

C.5
Conclusion of foundation EMA
A MIMO experimental modal analysis has been performed of the test rig foundation
with 2 inputs and 60 response points using the p-LSCF method. Two foundation setups
were tested, with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor, and the experimental setup
172
==PAGE:184==
C.5. CONCLUSION OF FOUNDATION EMA
where given together with the measurement hardware. Good coherence and minimal input
correlation were found using up- and down-chirp excitation signals for the two inputs. 11
and 16 modes were identiﬁed for the foundation with and without the VLT-tower and drive
motor respectively. The stabilization diagram obtain without the VLT-tower and drive
motor converged within a model order of 120. Whereas the stabilization diagram with
the VLT-tower and drive motor converged within a model order of 300 showing that the
foundation dynamics signiﬁcantly increased in complexity. Modal parameters including
undamped natural frequencies, damping ratios, mode shapes, and modal participation
factors were estimated for both setups. A comparison of the estimated modal parameters
from both foundation setups was compared. It was found that the VLT-tower had a
resonance around the ﬁrst torsional mode of the foundation at 34Hz. And also that the
motor drive had both softening and stiﬀening eﬀects in diﬀerent modes depending on
their mode shape. The drive motor also gave rise to additional resonances that were not
captured well by the response positions and are likely to correspond to the movement of
other components mounted on the foundation.
The ﬁrst transverse bending mode in the horizontal direction was successfully identiﬁed
at respectively 255.56 and 255.64 with and without the VLT-tower and drive motor.
With respect to the FEM model updating it is clearly harder to model the full test rig
with the VLT-tower and drive motor due to unexplained modal behavior. Therefore it is
recommended to either remove the VLT-tower and drive motor from the test rig such that
they don’t interfere with the foundation dynamics. Or produce well-described structural
fastening components that are easy to model.
Future aspects of this analysis are to remove more components from the foundation and
discuss their inﬂuence on the response. This would further ease the FEM model validation
and updating. However, this is a lengthy process and the current test without the rotor,
VLT-tower, and drive motor already gives a much simpler dynamic response.
173
==PAGE:185==
Appendix D
Experimental modal analysis of the
rotor shaft
This chapter will cover the experimental modal analysis of the rotor shaft with an increas-
ing amount of mounted components such as discs, couplings, sleeves, and bearings. The
analysis is performed to conﬁrm or update results made by Santos [54] and Østergaard
[2] in 2001 and Sørensen [3] in 2004. These experiments did not yield clear modeshapes
of the rotor and mainly provided the main natural frequencies. Since then the rotor shaft
has had many years of use while also during this project the bearing surface has been
re-polished.
The analysis aims to provide valuable information regarding modal parameters to be used
for FEM model updating. When developing FEM models it is standard to use Free-Free
boundary conditions, hence the experimental modal analysis should also aim to provide
free-free conditions.
D.1
Experimental Setup
The experimental modal analysis is performed as SIMO experiments using shaker excita-
tion and 2 roving accelerometers. Shaker excitation is chosen above hammer excitation
to improve the consistency of the input position across diﬀerent setups and also to reduce
test times. Two suspension setups have been tested trying to provide free-free conditions.
First, the shaft is suspended vertically by an eyebolt and then horizontally by two rope
slings. Previously approximate free-free boundary conditions had been obtained by hang-
ing the rotor shaft vertically using a threaded eyebolt. This approach is therefore taken
ﬁrst. Pictures of the experimental setups are shown in Figure D.1.
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
174
==PAGE:186==
D.1. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP
(a)
(b)

Figure D.1: Experimental setups aiming at free-free conditions. (a) Vertical suspended
shaft with excitation bearing (not used), (b) Horizontal suspended shaft with excitation
through the end connector hub. (c) Horizontal suspended shaft with one disc. (d) Hor-
izontal suspended shaft and fully instrumented with 2 discs. The white cloth is used
to protect the bearing surface.

Spherical bearing.
The steel shaft is 1150mm long, has a varying diameter between 40 and 100mm, and
weighs 49.3 kg. The two diameter 295mm steel discs, disc 1 and disc 2 have a thickness
of 100 and 80mm and weigh 37.3 and 46kg respectively [9]. When only mounting 1 disc a
steel spacer is needed of length 99mm and weight 3.9 kg. The AMB rotor rings, coupling
hub, spherical bearing and, excitation bearing weigh 6.5, 0.4, 0.7 and, 1.3 kg respectively.
To avoid drilling holes directly in the shaft the force transducer is mounted in series
to an external component such as the excitation bearing or the coupling hub using a
threaded stud. The shaker is suspended using a crane to reduce its inﬂuence on the rotor
shaft dynamics, and the shaker is connected to the force transducer using a steel stinger.
Accelerometers are mounted directly on the shaft using a thin layer of wax.
A schematic of the shaft and discs indicating the general measurement and excitation
175

nodes is shown in Figure D.2. The measurement nodes are changed slightly for diﬀerent
setups to cope with the added components. 18 measurement points are used along the
rotor shaft. And 4 extra measurement points on the discs are used to capture yawing
motion of the discs.
It is avoided to place accelerometers on the bearing surface to
protect the surface.

Measurement Hardware and data acquisition
The general measurement hardware, data acquisition, and post-processing parameters
are summarized in table D.1. The ﬁrst 4 modes of the ﬂexible shaft are of interest, from
FEM analysis and previous experiments, the fourth mode is found around 2600 Hz [54].
The sampling rate is therefore chosen to be fs = 10 kHz leaving a Nyquist frequency of
fNyq = 5 kHz. To avoid aliasing analog low-pass ﬁlters of all signal ampliﬁers are set to
3 kHz. Here it is worth mentioning that initially two MMF M68 charge ampliﬁers were
used with a cutoﬀat 10 kHz, hence well-excited modes between 5 −10 kHz would be
mirrored inside the frequency spectrum of interest. In practice, it was also concluded that
this setup gave huge aliasing problems yielding many erroneous peaks in the spectrum.
The problem was solved completely by changing the ampliﬁers to ones with appropriate
low-pass cut-oﬀ.
The Block size used for spectral density calculation is given by an interval in Table D.1
since this parameter is adjusted to obtain the best quality FRFs for the speciﬁc tests.
The excitation signals chirp and random noise are found to provide similar quality FRFs,
however, random noise is generally used unless otherwise stated.

Introductory experiments
Initially, the excitation bearing was used to mount the mechanical shaker see Figure D.1a.
This setup was unable to provide well-conditioned force signals, especially at resonances
where the force lacks by 90◦and a nonlinear play phenomenon (Thomsen [55]) is likely to
happen which will ruin the signal quality. This problem was avoided by instead exciting
the shaft through the coupling hub mounted at the other end of the shaft. An experimental
campaign was conducted using the vertical setup with excitation through the coupling
hub and modal parameters was estimated. However, it was found that the vertical setup
led to signiﬁcantly diﬀerent natural frequencies than previously obtained by Østergaard
[2]. At the same time, modes appeared more damped than expected considering a pure
steel shaft. It was therefore concluded that the free-free conditions were violated. The
older experiments used a diﬀerent eyebolt that may have provided better free conditions.
To avoid this problem the shaft is suspended horizontally using two rope slings.
As
explained by Heylen et al.
[11] approximate free-free conditions can be obtained by
suspending the test object in nodal points of the modes of interest. This idea can be
further developed by minimizing the driving points residues (DPR) deﬁned by.
Ajjr = ψ2
jr
i2ωr
(D.1)
Where Ajjr is the DPR, ψjr the modeshape (ideally mass normalized), and ωr the natural
frequency at the j’th input and output of the r’th mode. By analysis of the DPR values
of all modes of interest, a minimum can be determined with respect to suspension points.
This analysis is performed using the experimental modeshapes obtained from the vertical
suspension, yielding the suspension points xsus = {918, 290}mm. When discs or other
components are mounted the suspension positions need to be adjusted in order to comply
with the large change in mass distribution, see Figure D.1.
177
==PAGE:189==
D.2. RESULTS OF IMPACT EXCITATION
The idea of minimizing the DPR values might also explain why the vertical suspension
is a poor free-free condition. Even though transverse modes are of interest the eyebolt
mounting will provide some transverse stiﬀness at points where all modes are expected
to have high DPR (modal amplitude), hence also the mass of the external eyebolt will
interfere signiﬁcantly.
When hanging the shaft horizontally it is important that the stiﬀness of the slings is low
such that the rigid body modes of the shaft are far below the lowest frequency of interest.
The slings should also be long enough such that the pendulum motion of the full system
is found at frequencies below the lowest frequency of interest. In general, it is expected
that the pendulum motion of the shaft is lower than the rigid body modes. Therefore
to minimize the eﬀect of the suspension slings the shaft is excited horizontally aiming to
excite transverse horizontal bending modes of the shaft that are found at much higher
frequencies than of the pendulum motion.
Simple impact excitation
It is expected that the shaker-shaft connection including the coupling hub is expected
to provide a signiﬁcant change to the modal parameters of the shaft. Therefore a single
impact test is made for all rotor shaft setups before mounting the shaker to obtain an
indication of shaker interference. This test yields the damped natural frequencies fn,d of
the shaft by peaks in the FFT of the response signal. This analysis is expected to provide
the most correct estimates of damped natural frequencies and it is recommended that the
damped natural frequencies obtained from this test should be used as target values for
FEM-model updating. While remaining modal parameters such as modeshapes, damping,
and participation factors will be estimated by EMA using the shaker.
The impact test is performed using an impact hammer at node 1 with a metal tip to
obtain good excitation of high-frequency modes. The impact force is not measured since
only the frequency content of the response is of interest. The response is measured by
one accelerometer at node 2.
Test plans
Four experimental setups are tested using the horizontal suspended shaft: (1) Only cou-
pling hub (Figure D.1b) , (2) 1 disc (Figure D.1c), (3) 2 discs (Figure D.1d without the
spherical bearing, AMB rotor-ring, and excitation bearing) and, (4) all preceding com-
ponents including the spherical bearing, AMB rotor-ring, and excitation bearing (Figure
D.1d).
For every setup, an impact response is measured before mounting the shaker and coupling
hub.
In general 18 measurement nodes are used along the shaft leaving a total of 9 experiments
pr. setup. when using 2 accelerometers
For the last setup (4) extra tests are performed to allow proper classiﬁcation of the mode
shapes. Measurement positions on the discs are utilized and an additional set of mea-
surements are made by rotating the shaft 90◦around the x-axis. In total 44 DOFs are
measured in 22 tests allowing for a full 3D representation of the transverse bending modes
and disc movement.

Results of impact excitation

Figure D.3.

The clean shaft shows 4 resonance peaks below 2700Hz at 388, 946, 1658, and 2617Hz.
When all components are mounted the number of resonances has clearly increased. Closely
spaced resonances are observed which are likely xy,xz-plane variants of the same mode.
An extra test is performed to quickly investigate this claim by changing the impact and
measurement angle by 90◦. This has the eﬀect of interchanging the dominant closely
spaced resonances with the less dominant ones. This will also be further discussed using
the modal parameters estimated from EMA. It is also observed that an extra isolated
resonance (no clear sign of a repeated mode) is seen around 652Hz. This resonance likely
belongs to other vibrating components such as motion of the discs.

From Table D.2 it is seen that when mounting more components the damped natural
frequencies are found to decrease. This is likely caused by the additional mass added to
the system. It should also be noted that the coupling hub has a large inﬂuence upon the
ﬁfth f5,d damped natural frequency. Whereas the discs have very little inﬂuence upon
this mode, i.e. going from 1 to 2 discs the damped natural frequency of the highest mode
only drops from 2250 to 2245Hz, while adding the hub the damped natural frequencies
decrease to 2055 and 2053Hz respectively. This nature can be understood by analysis
of the corresponding bending mode 4 shown in Figure D.7.
The end of the coupling
hub shows large ampliﬁcation and the additional mass will therefore inﬂuence this mode
signiﬁcantly, while at the end of the disc, there is almost no movement when discs are
mounted meaning that discs will have little to no inﬂuence on the mode.
D.3
Results of rotor shaft EMA
This section will present the experimental modal analysis of the rotor shaft with an
increasing amount of components. Modal parameters are estimated using the implemented
poly-reference LSCF method (section 2.4.4).
The intermediate steps of identiﬁcation
(stabilization diagrams and modal ﬁts) are not discussed since this aspect has already
been discussed for the more complicated MIMO case in section C.3. However, it should
be noted that the p-LSCF method in general predicts more modes than those included
in the upcoming analysis. Important physical modes are chosen with respect to FRF
amplitude and participation factors.
It is also desired to match the damped natural
frequencies presented in Table D.2 with modes obtained from EMA, which is motivated
by the fact that mounting the shaker is likely to cause new unknown resonances and
disturbances into the systems that are undesired in the ﬁnal analysis. Stability diagrams
used to estimate the modal parameters are shown in Figure D.4 indicating the chosen
stabilized modes.

The FRF quality is evaluated using the ordinary coherence function. Visualizations of
all coherence functions across all experiments are shown in Figure D.6. In general good
coherence is obtained below 2700Hz. Although as expected using shaker excitation, drops
in the coherence are seen at antiresonances and resonances of the rotor shaft.
Large
coherence drops are also seen when an accelerometer position coincides with a nodal
point. The coherence also drops slightly for out-band frequencies as expected due to the
decreasing input energy. However, despite low input energy for higher frequencies, it is
concluded that the FRF quality is satisfactory within the frequency interval of interest.

Similar to the impact analysis it is observed that the number of modes increases for
higher rotor shaft complexity. While also closely spaced resonances are observed when
mounting discs. When all components are mounted 9 modes are identiﬁed in total. 8
modes are found to be related to the ﬁrst four transverse bending modes in pairs -
{214.4,227.1},{428.1,453.3},{1131.4.4,1161.1},{1848.7,1912.0}, while one mode at 642Hz
is likely related to the movement of the discs. This claim will be supported throughout
this section.
In general, it can be observed that the undamped natural frequencies fn reduce when
more components are attached to the rotor shaft. This is expected since the components
are attached in ways that do not provide any signiﬁcant stiﬀness hence only the mass will
contribute to a softening eﬀect of the system.

It is noticed from Table D.4 that the undamped natural frequencies predicted from EMA
are generally smaller than the corresponding damped natural frequencies. The two fre-
quencies are theoretically related via fn,d = fn
p
1 −ζ2
n, showing that the damped natural
frequency should be slightly lower than the undamped natural frequency, which is op-
posite of the observed. This decrease in natural frequencies is likely caused by the force
transducer and shaker, which may provide a small amount of external mass, lowering
the natural frequencies. This fact is important to have in mind when performing shaker
testing in general. And this analysis shows that shaker analysis is not optimal for this
experimental setup. For example, larger and heavier structures such as the test-rig foun-
dation are much better suited for shaker excitation since the overall dynamics are less
eﬀected by the external shaker system.
The damping ratios ζn are found to be low below 1% for almost all modes as expected for
a steel shaft without any damping components. When adding more discs and components
the damping ratio is generally increased. This can be expected since mounting external
components often comes with new energy dissipation eﬀects such as friction or component
play. When adding the spherical- and excitation bearings for all components the damping
increases across all modes likely caused by these naturally loose and rattly components.
The main transverse bending modes are identiﬁed by inspection of the corresponding
mode shapes. However many mode shapes appear to be very similar, especially for closely
184
==PAGE:196==
D.3. RESULTS OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
spaced resonances indicated by their AutoMAC, as an example for the fully instrumented
rotor shaft see Figure D.8a. And since the modal analysis is only performed in one plane
for the ﬁrst three setups (hub, 1 disc, and 2 discs) one has no clear indication of what
the modes actually represent. However, by analysis of the modal participation factor
πn one sees that experiments with 1 and 2 discs show that one of every closely spaced
resonance has a signiﬁcantly higher participation factor. This mode is hence best excited
by the planar excitation and is likely to also represent the bending mode shape best. And
when all components are mounted it is seen that the participation factors are almost equal
considering the pairs of closely spaced modes. This is a consequence of the extra excitation
dimension which provides good excitation information for all transverse bending modes.
The main transverse bending modes are identiﬁed considering the above arguments and
compared through all rotor shaft setups in Figure D.7. All mode shapes are normalized
at node 1 to ensure that they are not phase-shifted and to allow for a better amplitude
comparison between the diﬀerent setups.
Figure D.7: Main bending mode shapes of the rotor shaft below 2.5kHz compared trough
diﬀerent shaft setups. Colors refer to shaft setups including preceding components accord-
ing to:
Only coupling hub.
1 disc,
2 discs.
All components. The excitation
position is shown by
.
First analyzing the mode shapes of the rotor shaft with only the coupling hub it is seen
that these relate very well to the ﬁrst four transverse mode shapes of a free-free rod/beam.
Where the ﬁrst mode has 2 nodal points, the second 3, and so on. When adding compo-
nents to the rotor shaft the ﬁrst mode shape is seen to have reduced vibrational amplitude
in the end of the discs while also the nodal points are moved toward the discs. This is
expected since the discs add a signiﬁcant amount of mass moving the mass center of the
system towards the discs. Similar trends are seen for higher-order bending modes where
nodal points move toward the discs while the vibrational amplitude in the disc-end re-
duces. Especially interesting are the higher-order modes such as bending mode 4 where
the transverse vibrational amplitude of the discs almost disappears.
185
==PAGE:197==
D.3. RESULTS OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
It is also noticed that when all components are mounted the mode shapes generally become
less smooth. This is a consequence of having more components mounted, meaning that
more intricate movement is possible that is hard to capture smoothly. E.g. at node 3
the accelerometer is positioned on top of the spherical bearing which is likely to cause
accelerations not proportional to measurements measured directly on the shaft, giving the
mode shape a spike in amplitude.
The general spatial resolution of measurement points is generally good for all modes
although some jaggedness is seen for bending mode 4. Especially around the bearing
surface where it has been avoided to attach accelerometers to protect the surface ﬁnish.
Classiﬁcation of repeated bending and disc modes
Now that the main bending modes have been discussed for all rotor shaft setups, a further
analysis is given of how the remaining modes are classiﬁed. This analysis is performed
considering the fully instrumented shaft, with measurement and excitation points in two
orthogonal planes along the shaft including measurements on the discs.
To allow for proper classiﬁcation of all 9 modes in the frequency interval it is important to
have suﬃcient measurement DOFs such that spatial aliasing is avoided. The AutoMAC is
used to analyze the spatial aliasing of the diﬀerent experimental setups using only shaft
measurements, disc measurements, and an extra measurement plane. Figure D.8 shows
the AutoMAC of the three experimental setups using the MACX.
(a) 2D Shaft
(b) 2D shaft and discs
(c) 3D shaft and discs
Figure D.8: AutoMAC of mode shapes for the fully instrumented rotor shaft when in-
cluding more measurement positions. (a) planar measurements along the shaft only. (b)
planar measurements including the response of the discs. (c) combined measurements
from two orthogonal planes of the rotor shaft and discs.
From Figure D.8a it is clearly seen that heavy spatial aliasing exists. By including disc
measurements (Figure D.8b) the spatial aliasing is signiﬁcantly reduced. Now the 5th
mode is uniquely estimated showing that this mode is likely related to disc movement.
Mode {1, 2}, {3, 4}, {6, 7} and {8, 9} are still pairwise very similar. By including a sec-
ond measurement dimension all modes becomes uniquely determined as indicated by the
diagonal AutoMAC in Figure D.8c. Hence it must be concluded that the pairwise similar
mode shapes are likely variants of the same transverse bending mode but in diﬀerent
planes. This can also be illustrated by pairwise plotting the estimated mode shapes in
3D, see Figure D.9a. It is seen that the mode order is the same for every mode pair
however they vibrate in diﬀerent planes along the rotor shaft.
186
==PAGE:198==
D.3. RESULTS OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
To further analyze the possible disc-related mode 5, the planar mode shapes of the two
closest bending modes are plotted together with mode 5 in Figure D.9b. It is seen that
the transverse shaft bending of mode 5 is approximately a superposition of modes 4 and
6. Modes 4 and 6 show that both discs are tilting in phase while for mode 5 the discs are
tilting out of phase. It should also be mentioned that the modes shapes are normalized
at node 1, however, for mode 5 the modal coordinates of the discs are almost twice
the magnitude of the maximum shaft coordinate (not indicated by the ﬁgure). These
discoveries strongly support the theory of the mode being related to out-of-phase disc
movement which concludes the classiﬁcation of the 9 identiﬁed mode shapes of the fully
instrumented shaft.

are obtained by combining measurements from

discs.
(b) Planar mode shapes 4, 5, and 6 of the fully
instrumented shaft including the movement of

Comparison of old and new results
The previous experimental analysis performed by Østergaard [39] estimates the damped
natural frequencies fn,d by impact excitation followed by peak picking prominent peaks
in the FFT. These will be compared to the newly obtained damped natural frequencies
following a similar approach. The main diﬀerence between the two experiments is the
vertical and horizontal suspension of the shaft. Østergaard only picks single peaks in the
frequency spectrum hence no description of repeated resonances is found. A comparison
of the damped natural frequencies for no discs, 1 disc, and 2 discs is shown in Table
D.5, while a comparison of FFT using 2 discs is shown in Figure D.10. A more in-depth
comparison of modeshapes and damping is not directly possible since modeshapes and
damping have not been previously estimated.

By comparison of the new and old estimates of damped natural frequencies in Table D.5
it is seen that good agreement is found for the clean shaft without any discs. However,
when discs are included the agreement becomes less good. The new estimates are generally
found to be smaller than the old estimates. This diﬀerence is likely explained by a newly
added feature with respect to the disc mounting. The initial shaft uses a small threaded
locking ring to secure the discs, while the new setup uses a larger locking ring with a
smooth surface used for displacement measurements. Hence the newly added ring has an
increased mass and is likely to provide a reduction of the natural frequencies.
Moreover, the repeated natural frequencies usually have one frequency matching signiﬁ-
cantly better to the old results. This might be caused by the frequency picking performed
by Østergaard who might not have noticed/measured the closely spaced peaks. Although
one cannot be very sure of this statement.
Another reason for discrepancies between the estimates could be caused by the vertical
and horizontal support. When the shaft is mounted vertically gravitational forces will
give rise to normal forces in the shaft. As described by Nelson [56] normal forces will
provide a stiﬀening eﬀect on the rotor shaft that is likely to give a small increase in the
natural frequencies.
As was also observed during this project the vertical support showed an increase in damp-
ing compared to the horizontal support. By comparison of FFTs from the old and new
experiments in Figure D.10 using 2 discs it is also noted that damping seems to have been
reduced for the new horizontal setup. The increased damping could possibly smoothen
the response such that possible closely-spaced resonances would appear as one. However,
note that a direct comparison of FFT amplitude cannot be made since plots are shown
for diﬀerent input-output positions. Another reason that the old vertical setup does not
yield closely spaced resonances could be that the imbalance provided by the discs is min-
imized since gravity will naturally center them on the shaft. Whereas when the shaft is
suspended horizontally gravity will naturally misalign the discs and shaft in the case of
large tolerances between the hole and shaft diameter.
It should also be mentioned that previously the frequency component at 708Hz for the
188
==PAGE:200==
D.3. RESULTS OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
2-disc setup was assumed to be a bending mode of the rotor shaft. However, the best
matching frequency at 656Hz for the new setup is classiﬁed as disc-related mode. This
discovery might explain a common problem of large deviations between FEM and the
experimental frequencies of this mode.
See e.g.
Wagner [9] who ﬁnds 18% deviation
between model and experiment, and also notes that this speciﬁc mode is likely aﬀected
by disc interaction not captured by the model.
(a) New impact FFT from impact at node 1 to output at node 2
(b) Old impact FFT. Figure courtesy to Santos [54].
Figure D.10: FFT from old (a) and new (b) experiments using 2 discs.
D.3.1
Discussion of parameter robustness and sources of error
During the experimental analysis of the rotor shaft, many considerations were made re-
garding proper free-free conditions and also the overall parameter robustness. Of which
the most important will be discussed in this section.
Roving accelerometers and Shaker inﬂuence
It was noticed during testing that roving experiments had small systematic changes in the
natural frequency. It was generally seen that when accelerometers were mounted at the
extremities of the rotor shaft the ﬁrst natural frequency was approximately 1Hz smaller
compared to mounting accelerometers in the middle of the shaft. This change in the
natural frequency is explained by the added mass which has a large eﬀect when mounted
at the position of general high modal amplitude, such as the extremities of the shaft. Due
to the very low damping of the shaft, this eﬀect becomes quite signiﬁcant since the width
of the resonance peaks are very small and therefore appear quite diﬀerently for diﬀerent
accelerometer positions. The problem becomes less apparent when mounting components
on the shaft increase the total mass and damping.
This problem is known to cause
problems for global estimation methods [11] such as the implemented p-LSCF method.
The method is seen to converge relatively slowly and the LSFD mode shape estimation
becomes poor. To reduce this problem, it was chosen to spread out the accelerometers
such that only one accelerometer was placed at an extremity. This helped signiﬁcantly in
terms of p-LSCF convergence and mode shape estimation. Although, from this discussion,
189
==PAGE:201==
D.3. RESULTS OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
it can generally be said that all estimated natural frequencies (Table D.3) and damped
natural frequencies (Table D.2) are likely to be slightly underestimated as a consequence
of external accelerometer masses. For further studies, the known moving accelerometer
mass can be used to perform a sensitivity analysis by properly multiplying the mass of
the accelerometers onto modal estimates obtained using an SDOF approach, [11].
Disc imbalance
Figure D.11: FRF comparison focused on the
ﬁrst and second modes of the fully instru-
mented rotor shaft when a disc is rotated.
If the shaft was perfectly circular symmet-
ric along the x-axis all the natural frequen-
cies of repeated bending modes should co-
incide.
This is exactly what is observed
when no discs are mounted on the shaft.
However, when the discs are mounted, re-
peated mode shapes are spaced signiﬁ-
cantly in frequency. A clear explanation for
this phenomenon is diﬃcult to pinpoint al-
though it is likely related to the imbalances
in the discs. From this belief, a hypothe-
sis is that rotating the discs on the shaft
will change the dynamics of the shaft sig-
niﬁcantly. A test is therefore performed by rotating the second disc 90◦. The eﬀect is
visualized by focusing on the ﬁrst repeated bending mode in Figure D.11. It is observed
that the natural frequency is changed from 241.3 to 213.1 Hz and from 225.4 to 225.0
Hz while the maximum amplitude is also slightly reduced. Similar eﬀects are observed
for higher-order modes. Hence rotation of the discs does have an eﬀect on the natural
frequencies of the rotor shaft, meaning that they are likely to exhibit imbalances. The
disc imbalance might not necessarily lie within the structural elements of the disc but
might be concerned with noneccentric mounting due to improper tolerances between hole
and shaft. From this, one would also expect that the tightening torque used to fasten
the disc would have a visible eﬀect. This rotor shaft phenomenon has speciﬁcally been
investigated by [57].
It sadly also shows that the robustness of modal parameters found within this analysis
cannot be deﬁned by their calculated standard deviations. Since small changes to the
physical setup can easily disturb parameters within a few Hz.
Eﬀect of boundary conditions - Approximating free-free conditions
As already discussed in the section on the experimental setup the vertical hanging sus-
pension was avoided due to high damping and poor suspension position. The shaft was
instead suspended horizontally by two textile slings. The stiﬀness of the slings should
be low enough such that the rigid body modes of the shaft are well below the frequency
of interest. And since no indication of rigid body movement above 100Hz is seen this
assumption is assumed valid. When no discs are mounted on the shaft the suspension
points are chosen by minimizing the driving points residues of the ﬁrst four bending
modes. Hence this suspension is expected to provide a good approximation of free-free
conditions. However, when mounting discs it becomes impossible to position the slings
properly due to the highly shifted center of mass. Instead, one sling is mounted very close
to the discs and one is positioned to balance the shaft. The sling close to the discs is
likely in a good position since generally small modal amplitudes are seen for all modes
in this position, see Figure D.7. However, the second sling is not well placed and might
190
==PAGE:202==
D.4. CONCLUSION OF ROTOR SHAFT EMA
interfere with the free-free assumption.
D.4
Conclusion of rotor shaft EMA
An experimental modal analysis of the rotor shaft has been performed using roving SIMO
experiments in approximate free-free conditions. Initially, the suspension of the shaft was
studied. It was found that good free-free conditions could be obtained by suspending the
shaft horizontally using two rope slings,
The shaft was tested in 4 diﬀerent conﬁgurations with no discs, 1 disc, 2 discs, and
ﬁnally including bearings and AMB-rotor rings. Before every shaker test, an impact test
was performed to obtain estimates of the damped natural frequencies unaﬀected by the
force transducer and shaker. EMA tests were performed using 18 measurement points
along the shaft, however, to enable proper identiﬁcation of all transverse bending modes
within 2700Hz it was needed to repeat measurements in an orthogonal plane including
disc measurements.
Modal parameters were identiﬁed using the implemented p-LSCF method for all rotor
shaft setups. In general, the natural frequencies decreased while the damping increased
as more components were added to the rotor shaft. The number of modes increased from
4 to 9 when going from zero to all components. The extra modes were identiﬁed to arise
from repeated mode shapes of the same order but in diﬀerent planes, and also one mode
related to out-of-phase disc movement.
Comparing the newly estimated damped natural frequencies to the old results a good
agreement was found when no discs were added and a signiﬁcant decrease was seen when
adding 1 and 2 discs. The decrease in frequency is likely caused by the increased mass of
new components.
By discussion of the parameter robustness and sources of error, it was concluded that
the undamped natural frequencies obtained from EMA are likely underestimated due to
the external accelerometers and force transducer mass. The damped natural frequencies
obtained from impact excitation are likely more precise although still aﬀected by the
accelerometer masses. It was also concluded that the repeated mode shapes are likely
to arise from disc imbalances and that rotating the discs changes the modal parameters
signiﬁcantly.
Hence the parameter robustness is not very good for experiments using
discs.
With respect to using the estimated modal parameters for FEM-model updating it is
important to consider the discussed parameter robustness. It is recommended that natural
frequency comparisons should be made using the damped natural frequencies since these
are least aﬀected by external sources. However, mode shapes and damping factors are
likely to be well estimated from the EMA experiments and can be safely used to validate
the overall shape and magnitude of mode shape and damping levels.
191
==PAGE:203==
Appendix E
Comparison of EMA and OMA
identiﬁcation methods
E.1
Comparison of P-LSCF and SDOF experimental
modal identiﬁcation methods
This section will compare the developed poly reference LSCF identiﬁcation method to the
simple peak-picking SDOF approach. The biggest diﬀerence between the two schemes is
that the SDOF approach naturally assumes well-spaced resonances and only provides
local modal estimates whereas the p-LSCF method can identify repeated resonances and
provides global estimates of modal parameters.
A big advantage of the p-LSCF and
other multi-dof methods is that noise can be modeled and separated from the real modal
parameters by over-specifying the model order. Moreover, the p-LSCF method allows the
user to provide a weighting function such that frequency bands of low coherence (poor
signal-to-noise ratio) can be penalized in the global ﬁt.
To analyze the diﬀerence between the two methods, the SDOF approach is used on the
FRF data obtained from the test-rig foundation with the VLT-tower (Appendix C) and
the fully equipped rotor shaft (Appendix D). The SDOF method is semi-automated by
searching for peaks in user-deﬁned frequency bands and by using the power-point crite-
ria. The SDOF method is applied to all available FRFs, i.e. 120 and 44 FRFs for the
foundation and rotor shaft experiments respectively, and will provide a local estimate of
modal parameters for every FRF. A disadvantage of this approach is that some FRFs will
not indicate resonances in the chosen bands due to nodal points or high modal coupling.
This will ruin the ﬁt of modal parameters and provide no physical meaning. Ideally, vi-
sual inspection is required for every ﬁt to remove such poor candidates, however, this has
been deemed infeasible due to the high amount of resonances and FRFs. This also helps
to illustrate the disadvantage of the SDOF approach for large data sets. To reduce the
inﬂuence of the poorly estimated modal parameters outliers are removed by discarding
the upper and lower 5% percentiles of the individual modal parameter sets. The ﬁnal
modal parameters are then estimated by the mean value of the local modal parameters.
Standard deviations are also calculated to represent the spread of modal estimates across
the diﬀerent FRFs. This brings up an advantage of the local SDOF methods over the
global estimation methods. Global estimation methods often suﬀer from typical measure-
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
192
==PAGE:204==
E.1. COMPARISON OF P-LSCF AND SDOF EXPERIMENTAL MODAL IDENTIFICATION
METHODS
ment errors such as the mass loading eﬀect due to moving accelerometers. Whereas, the
SDOF methods can be used to quantize these FRF to FRF measurement errors and even
by properly accounting for external mass sources provide the ability to perform a modal
sensitivity analyses [11].
p-LSCF and SDOF modal parameters are compared in Table E.1 and E.2 for the founda-
tion and shaft respectively. The relative deviations of the SDOF to p-LSCF parameters
are given in parentheses. Analyzing the relative deviation of both the undamped natu-
ral frequency and damping ratios for the foundation and rotor shaft it is observed that
slightly smaller relative deviations are found for the rotor shaft. This is expected since
the dynamic response of the foundation is signiﬁcantly more damped and modes are con-
sequently harder to separate which breaks the main SDOF assumption of well-separated
modes. The rotor shaft is signiﬁcantly less damped which has the eﬀect of providing
better separation of resonance peaks that are easier ﬁtted by the SDOF models.
Analyzing the standard deviations for the foundation estimates in Table E.1 it is clear that
the SDOF method has much higher standard deviations compared to the p-LSCF method.
However, as explained above these standard deviations cannot be directly compared since
they represent model-order to model-order and FRF to FRF deviation for the p-LSCF
and SDOF methods respectively. Large standard deviations of the SDOF method for
both fn and ζn are generally found for the ﬁrst 3 and the last 4 modes. From previous
analyses, these modes are known to appear in damped and coupled areas which is likely
reducing the consistency of the SDOF ﬁts.
Looking at the SDOF rotor shaft standard deviations of the undamped natural frequen-
cies fn they are generally increasing with frequency. In Appendix D it was explained
how accelerometers had noticeable eﬀects on the natural frequencies. Hence the increas-
ing standard deviations might suggest that the moving accelerometer mass has a larger
eﬀect on higher modes. The standard deviations of the damping ratios do not show a
similar trend, suggesting that the moving accelerometers do not signiﬁcantly inﬂuence the
damping of the system.
Further comparison of the SDOF method is also made to OMA methods in the next
section where box plots are used to analyze the modal estimates.

The SDOF approach can also be used to estimate the modeshapes as described in section
2.4.3. Since a vast amount of FRFs are available it is decided to use the imaginary part
of the FRF as an indication of the modal amplitude since this is easily automatized once
the natural frequencies are known. The foundation experiment has 2 inputs hence two
SDOF mode-shape estimates exist (one for each input). The mean of these estimates is
used as the overall modeshape.
SDOF modeshapes are shown and compared to p-LSCF modeshapes Figure E.2 and E.3

for the foundation and rotor shaft respectively.

Figure E.1: MACX comparisons of SDOF (imaginary) and p-LSCF mode shapes of the

By analysis of the foundation MAC comparison Figure E.1a generally, good coherence
between modeshape estimates is found.
Small clusters of alike modeshapes are seen
which is expected due to insuﬃcient measurement DOFs as explained in section C.3.
However, the SDOF method struggles to separate mode 8 from 9 and mode 11 from 12.
This is also clearly visible in the illustration of the SDOF mode shapes E.2 where these
modes appear similar and diﬀerent from the p-LSCF counterpart. By analysis of the
corresponding natural frequencies, FRFs, or stabilization diagrams (see e.g. Figure C.7b)
it is also observed that these modes are closely spaced with modes 9 and 12 dominating
the response. Hence it is expected that the SDOF method cannot separate the diﬀerent
modeshapes due to its underlying assumptions. While in damped and coupled situations
it is expected that the multi-input p-LSCF method has the ability to separate closely
spaced resonances.
In general, it is found that the rotor shaft modes are well estimated by the SDOF approach.
From Figure E.1b only the SDOF-mode 3 seems to correlate to both p-LSCF-mode 3 and
4 which is also visible by analysis of Figure E.1b. The reason for the SDOF approach
to estimate the rotor shaft modes well is likely explained by the light damping of the
shaft allowing for well-separated peaks in the FRF which makes it easy to separate modes
by simple peak "picking". And since the experiments have been performed with single-
input the p-LSCF method does not have the improved ability to separate repeated/close
resonances, which might set its estimates closer to the SDOF approach.
