E.2
Comparison of EMA and OMA methods using
foundation and shaft data sets
This section will use OMA methods on the output signals from the EMA data sets of
the foundation and shaft. The modal parameters from OMA methods will be compared
to EMA counterparts and their uncertainties will be discussed. This analysis is mainly
performed to familiarize the user with the diﬀerent OMA techniques, that are to be used
in the later stages of this project, and also to study and identify the pros and cons of
diﬀerent EMA and OMA techniques.
It is chosen to focus on two main data sets: (i) the roving foundation experiments with the
VLT tower and drive motor with 16 identiﬁed modes (see appendix C), and (ii) the roving
shaft experiments of the fully instrumented shaft utilizing experiments in two orthogonal
planes with 9 identiﬁed modes (see appendix D).
197
==PAGE:209==
E.2. COMPARISON OF EMA AND OMA METHODS USING FOUNDATION AND SHAFT DATA
SETS
(i) Foundation data
The foundation response data consists of 30 tests each with No = 2 measured acceleration
outputs. The acceleration signals are sampled at 3 kHz. All data have been low-pass
ﬁltered using FFT ﬁltering with Hanning tapering from 250-252Hz and subsequently
decimated by a factor of 6 to a sampling rate of 0.5 kHz. Decimation is performed to
increase the speed of the model identiﬁcation, which has a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the time
domain methods. Correlation functions are estimated using the direct approach using 100
time lags. The ﬁrst time lag of the CF functions is removed to reduce the inﬂuence of
noise that normally dominates the ﬁrst time lag.
(ii) Shaft data
The rotor shaft response data consists of 22 tests, each with No = 2 measured acceleration
outputs. The acceleration signals are sampled at 10 kHz. Prior to analysis signals have
been low-pass ﬁltered using FFT ﬁltering with Hanning tapering from 2000-2200 Hz and
subsequently decimated by a factor of 2 to a sampling rate of 5 kHz. Correlation functions
are estimated using the direct approach using 200-time lags. The number of time lags is
increased with respect to the foundation data since the shaft is signiﬁcantly less damped.
The ﬁrst time lag of the CF functions is removed to reduce the inﬂuence of noise. Data
quality for OMA identiﬁcation
Using data sets meant for EMA comes with large disadvantages considering the general
application of OMA. The EMA experiments have been performed using shaker excitation
with few response sensors which greatly reduces the rank of the measurement system. The
foundation experiment is performed using two uncorrelated shaker inputs and two output
sensors limiting the measurement system rank to 2. Hence two closely spaced resonances
should be identiﬁable however then noise cannot be separated from the physical response.
For the shaft experiment, only a single shaker input is used with two output sensors
limiting the measurement system to rank 1. Hence it is not expected that closely spaced
resonances can be found, or that noise can be separated from the physical response. See
section 2.3.2 for a more theoretical discussion on the subject.
To investigate these claims the singular values of the spectral density matrix are plotted
together with a stabilization diagram obtained using the SSI technique. Figure E.4 shows
the plot for two representative roving experiments of the foundation and shaft.
Analyzing Figure E.4a of the foundation it is noticed that the singular value lines both
show signs of clear ampliﬁcation indicating that both singular values describe physical
responses. It is seen that closely spaced resonances can be found by the SSI technique
around 125 Hz however around 30 Hz there are clearly closely-spaced resonances that are
not identiﬁed well. Not being able to identify the close modes might be caused by having
more than 2 modes contributing to the response in this region. Or that the input forces
from the shakers become correlated within this region due to high vibrational amplitude at
both shaker positions considering that the ﬁrst bending and torsional modes dominate this
region (see Appendix C). Analyzing Figure E.4b of the shaft it is clear that the spectral
lines are signiﬁcantly separated in magnitude by approximately 30 dB. Compared to
Figure E.4a only a 10 dB diﬀerence is found. The larger spacing of singular value curves
shows that one singular value is dominating while the other is likely to represent noise.
This is expected using single shaker input which practically reduces the SD matrix rank
to 1. This also has the eﬀect of reducing the capability of the SSI to identify closely
spaced resonances, e.g. at 220 Hz only one stable mode is indicated where two modes are
198
==PAGE:210==
E.2. COMPARISON OF EMA AND OMA METHODS USING FOUNDATION AND SHAFT DATA
SETS
known to be found. However in other regions around 400 Hz and 1800 Hz closely spaced
resonances are still found but with poor damping stability (green points).
(a) Foundation.
(b) Shaft.
Figure E.4: Examples of singular values of the spectral density matrix and stabilization
diagram (SSI). (a) Foundation with VLT tower and drive motor at response points (4/12)
and (4/4). (b) Shaft with all components at response points 1 and 10.
Identiﬁcation methods and assembly of roving test data
Initially, the time domain methods ITD, SSI, ERA, and PR together with the FDD
frequency domain are considered. However, since only two response measurements No = 2
are available for all tests the ITD system matrix will become 2No × 2No and only allow
for the identiﬁcation of 2No/2 = 2 modes. And since the considered data has a minimum
of 16 and 9 modes for the foundation and shaft respectively the ITD method has not been
applied. However one could still apply the ITD method by repeatedly bandpass ﬁltering
the data as long as no more than 2 closely spaced resonances are within every band, or
by adding pseudo measurements.
Using the enhanced FDD method relies on isolating the modal coordinates by modal
ﬁltering. However, it is not possible to perform modal ﬁltering using more modes than
the number of sensors (two in this case No = 2). And again since the analyzed responses
include signiﬁcantly more than 2 modes the FDD technique has not been applied.
The remaining OMA techniques are adjusted with respect to model order, such that a
reasonable ﬁt to the correlation functions is obtained.
The SII technique is used with s = 30 block rows for both the foundation and shaft
parameter sets, corresponding to a model with sNo = 60 modes. The SVD matrices
(2.71) are reduced to include the ﬁrst 42 and 49 singular values for the foundation and
shaft parameters sets respectively.
The ERA technique is used with s = 20 and s = 30 block rows of the block Hankel
matrices corresponding to a model with 40 modes and 60 modes for the foundation and
shaft data respectively. The SVD matrices (2.87) are reduced to the ﬁrst 40 singular
values for both data sets.
The PR technique is used with NA = 20 and NA = 30 AR parameters resulting in a
model with 40 and 60 modes for the foundation and shaft data respectively. The PR
199
==PAGE:211==
E.2. COMPARISON OF EMA AND OMA METHODS USING FOUNDATION AND SHAFT DATA
SETS
technique outputs all modes hence no lower-order realization is possible.
It should be noted that normally one does not need to use oversized models for the ERA
and PR techniques, however, in this study, it was found crucial to include many modes
in the models to obtain good modal estimates. This is likely caused by the fact that
the noise cannot easily be separated from the physical response due to rank deﬁciency
problems of the measurement system (rank 2 and 1). Therefore the estimation methods
are adjusted to estimate many more poles than exist in the response such that extra poles
can be used to model the noise.
Figure E.5: Example of clustering with poles
from the ERA technique used on the founda-
tion data in the frequency interval [60, 140].
The identiﬁcation techniques SSI, ERA,
and PR are applied to every roving data
set leading to 30 and 22 modal parame-
ter sets for the foundation and shaft data
respectively. The ﬁnal modal parameters
are estimated as an average over all data
sets. However, combing many modal data
sets is not a trivial task since modal esti-
mates will diﬀer from data set to data set
while some sets might completely lack in-
formation on certain modes due to poor
accelerometer placements (nodal points).
The OMA methods are also known to pro-
duce many non-physical noise poles, which
is especially important considering that the
model order of the estimation methods is
signiﬁcantly oversized to account for noise.
This procedure has therefore been per-
formed with the help of hierarchical clustering and clustering diagrams. All modal pa-
rameters are clustered using an Euclidian distance measure on normalized (fn,i, ζn,i) data.
Where n is the mode number while i = 1..30 and i = 22 is the data set number of the
foundation and shaft respectively. The cluster cutoﬀis deﬁned by the user by trial and
error. The resulting clustering is plotted in a clustering diagram which is the most impor-
tant tool for the determination of physical and noise poles. An example of a clustering
diagram for the ERA method on the foundation data focused on the frequency interval
[60, 140] is shown in Figure E.5. The Figure clearly indicates 7 groups of modal parame-
ters whereas noise poles are scattered more or less randomly. Modal parameter estimates
could be obtained by simply taking the average value of the found clusters. However,
this might introduce biased results since clusters are not guaranteed to include informa-
tion from all roving experiments. Therefore another approach has been taken where the
mean values of the clusters are used as starting values for a frequency-picking procedure.
The frequency-picking procedure works by deﬁning a target frequency and a frequency
tolerance for every mode. Modal parameters within the frequency tolerance are then cho-
sen from all roving modal parameter sets with the smallest absolute deviation from the
frequency targets. This method would also allow for the correct merging of modeshape
components since all roving experiments are considered. However, since the experimental
data has been performed as roving experiments without reference sensors, estimation of
the modeshapes is sadly not possible (lacking common modal coordinates for normaliza-
tion). When performing this modal picking procedure it also becomes apparent which
200
==PAGE:212==
E.2. COMPARISON OF EMA AND OMA METHODS USING FOUNDATION AND SHAFT DATA
SETS
modes are strongly and weakly present in the overall response since these modes will be
found in many and few roving tests respectively.
Comparison of EMA and OMA estimation methods
The time domain methods SSI, ERA, and PR have been applied to all data sets for
the foundation with VLT tower and drive motor as well as the fully equipped shaft as
described above. A comparison will now be made by comparing the EMA methods to
the applied OMA methods.
It is important to note that the spread of data through
diﬀerent methods is likely to arise from diﬀerent sources. I.e. the SDOF, SSI, ERA,
and PR methods all use modal estimates from every roving experiment to calculate mean
estimates, whereas the p-lSCF method provides global estimates considering all data sets
simultaneously, and calculates mean estimates across model orders. Hence, it is likely
that the spread of modal parameters is much smaller for the p-LSCF method compared
to the other methods. Care should therefore be taken when making conclusions about the
uncertainty of the modal parameters compared across the diﬀerent identiﬁcation methods.
Since the data processing after the identiﬁcation has a huge inﬂuence on the ﬁnal spread
of modal parameters. Having made this disclaimer it is generally acknowledged by the
literature to assume that global EMA methods such as the p-LSCF method will provide
the most accurate modal parameters, see eg. Ewins [10] or Heylen [11]. The p-LSCF
method is therefore generally used as the baseline of good modal estimates.
Tables showing the mean value of modal parameters together with the relative deviation
to the p-LSCF estimate are shown in Tables E.3, E.4, E.5, E.6. From the Tables it is, in
general, found that the undamped natural frequencies estimated by the OMA methods are
quite good for both the foundation and shaft data compared to the p-LSCF estimates with
relative deviations all below 1%. However, the estimates are slightly better considering
the foundation data which is expected since the data is multi-input which allows the
measurement system to be of rank 2. The estimates of the damping ratios are less good
but often in the same order of magnitude. Again the foundation estimates are generally
signiﬁcantly better than the shaft estimates.
A general observation can also be made considering the estimated damping from OMA
methods to be regularly higher than the estimates given by the p-LSCF method, see
Tables E.4 and E.6. An explanation for this might be caused by the shaker excitation.
Since the p-LSCF is an EMA method it has the ability to account for the input forces and
therefore also model the shaker-stinger system better. Whereas the OMA methods do not
have this ability, and hence the added damping and mass of the shaker-stinger system
cannot be accounted for. As explained by Brincker [27] a signiﬁcant bias is expected in
the resulting modal estimates from this type of OMA testing.
Modal parameters are compared visually using box plots of the modal parameter sets
otherwise used to calculate the mean estimate of the modal parameters. Box plots are
shown for the undamped natural frequency fn and damping ratio ζn for every mode of
interest in Figures E.6, E.7, E.8, and E.9.
To allow for an adequate visualization of
the box plots across all estimation methods the data beyond the black dashed lines are
considered extreme. Meaning that the value of the data beyond the black dashed lines
does not correspond to the scale of the shown y-axis but is only plotted to indicate its
existence. Note also the y-axis range for every subﬁgure of the individual modes. The
scaling is kept constant for fn box plots to allow direct comparison of the box plot width
across modes, whereas the scaling is customized when visualizing ζn hence care should
201
==PAGE:213==
E.2. COMPARISON OF EMA AND OMA METHODS USING FOUNDATION AND SHAFT DATA
SETS
be taken when performing a visual comparison between the damping ratio of diﬀerent
modes. The number of box plots is quite large and many small takeaways can be made
by deeply studying them, however, some overall observations will be discussed below.
As expected the p-LSCF methods clearly show the smallest spread (width of box plots)
of modal parameters across both foundation and shaft experiments. The SDOF method
clearly does the worse considering the spread of the modal estimates. However, its median
is often still a decent estimate compared to the p-LSCF estimate as also seen earlier
considering the mean. The spread of modal parameters from the OMA methods is found
to vary more from mode to mode.
As previously discussed using the shaft data it would become hard if not impossible to
estimate closely spaced resonances due to the single shaker input. However, as seen in
Figure E.8 the ﬁrst two close-spaced resonances (29 and 34 Hz) are still reasonably well
estimated. From further analysis, this can be explained by having measurement data from
two orthogonal planes, where data from one plane excites one of the modes well and vice
versa. This realization can also be made for other closely-spaced modes of the shaft such
as modes 3 and 4, and 6 and 7.
By simultaneously considering the singular value plots such as the ones shown in Figure
E.4a and E.4b and the box plots, a connection can be made for modes with seemingly
high modal coupling leading to a large spread of the modal parameters estimated by all
OMA techniques. For example, a small spread of the modal parameters for all OMA
methods can be found for modes 4 (64 Hz) and 5 (71 Hz) of the foundation. From Figure
E.4a these modes are clearly isolated well from other resonances and also appear with
clear ampliﬁcation of the singular values (low damping) leading to decent conditions for
the OMA methods. A large spread of modal parameters (wide box plots) can be found
for modes 1 (214 Hz) and 2 (227 Hz) of the rotor shaft. These modes are very closely
spaced resonances and since the rotor shaft measurement system is only rank 1 (singular
input) the OMA methods cannot distinguish well between them.
From Figure E.6 and E.7 it is also noticed that The ERA method cannot estimate the
second mode of the foundation. This is likely caused by the closely spaced resonances
which cannot be estimated well due to the lack of measurement system rank. It is also
seen that the SSI and PR method shows a large spread of undamped natural frequency
showing that their estimate is likely poor.
It is also noticed that the ERA and PR box plots are identical from the fourth mode and
up considering the foundation data. This is reassured by Table E.5 and E.6 where modal
parameters are equal for the ERA and PR methods from mode 4 and up. This is likely
explained by both models using a model order of 40 and hence their estimation methods
are very similar. Brincker [27] also observes this phenomenon when using the ERA and
PR techniques with the same model order.

E.3. CONCLUSIONS ON THE COMPARISON BETWEEN EMA AND OMA MODAL
IDENTIFICATION METHODS
E.3
Conclusions on the comparison between EMA
and OMA modal identiﬁcation methods
This chapter used previously obtained experimental data of the foundation with the VLT
tower and drive motor and the fully instrumented rotor shaft to compare EMA and OMA
estimation methods.
First, a comparison of the EMA methods: poly-reference-LSCF (p-LSCF), and the SDOF
peak-picking approach was made and discussed. It was concluded by comparing poles
and mode shapes that the global p-LSCF method was advantageous considering damped
responses with closely spaced resonances since the main assumptions using the SDOF
approaches were broken. While the p-LSCF method has even further increased abilities
considering MIMO experiments. And also that the SDOF approach is cumbersome to
apply for data with many FRFs such as for the multi-input foundation experiment. How-
ever, an advantage of the SDOF approach is that a measure can be given describing the
FRF-to-FRF variation of modal parameters which the global EMA methods are unable
to provide directly.
Next, the SSI, ERA, and PR time domain OMA methods were used to analyze the
experimental data. The ITD and FDD techniques were found inadequate due to the small
number of response sensors in relation to the number of modes. Data was decimated to
required sampling rates to increase processing times and the paring of modal parameters
across diﬀerent roving tests was discussed. Global mode shapes could not be estimated
due to the lack of reference censors. Diﬃculties of using EMA data for OMA estimation
were also discussed. It was concluded that the experimental data sets were not ideal for
OMA analysis since only 2 sensors and a few uncorrelated inputs were used which reduced
the general rank of the measurement system. It was therefore also found necessary to over-
specify the model order if OMA methods signiﬁcantly to allow proper separation of noise
and physical dynamics.
By comparing the EMA and OMA methods it was concluded that regardless of the rank
deﬁciency problems the OMA modal estimates were very close to the p-LSCF counterparts
with all undamped natural frequencies below 1% standard deviation. The damping ratios
from the OMA methods tend to be larger than the p-LSCF estimates which could be ex-
plained by the external shaker excitation. Modal parameters predicted by the foundation
were found to be slightly better than those from the rotor shaft which was explained by
the increased measurement system rank. It was also seen that the general accuracy of the
OMA modal parameters was decreased for closely-spaced resonances of both data sets.
Sources of error and other complications
Due to the high amount of roving experiments, it was diﬃcult to pair model estimates
across the many modal sets. This procedure is likely to yield inadequate pairings which
leads to a higher inconsistency of modal parameters that is not necessarily related to the
identiﬁcation method itself.
Diﬃculties were also had in drawing general conclusions due to the high amount of modes
in the experimental data. Hence for future analyses of this kind, it is recommended to
focus the analysis on fewer modes of simpler structures.

Appendix G
Sensor Calibration
The 10 displacement censors have been re-calibrated. Previous calibrations have been
performed by Bull [8]. The sensors are placed in a calibration rig containing a micrometer
screw with an attached ST. 60-2 specimen. The micrometer skrew is initially zeroed and
the displacement sensor is positioned touching the specimen (zero initial displacement).
The calibration is then performed by reading the output voltage V when facing the speci-
mens at diﬀerent distances x. A linear ﬁt is then performed on the measured data points.
If the measurement points deviate signiﬁcantly from a linear behavior at the ends these
points are excluded from the ﬁt. Therefore it is also important to note the voltage value
V ∗which approximates the middle of the linear measuring range and should be used as
a reference when installing the sensors. Calibration data and linear ﬁt for all sensors are
shown in Figure G.1.
A Novel and Non-Invasive Rotordynamic Testing Combining Operational Modal Analysis and Active Lubrication
Techniques - Theory and Experiments
